Inconvertibility of Remaining Negatives
Author argues that remaining negatives are inconvertible and addresses an objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why temporal negative propositions are inconvertible and trace how inconvertibility propagates upward through a hierarchy of proposition types.

Core Idea
Inconvertibility of the most specific negative propagates upward to all more general negatives.

In this passage, the author tackles the remaining negative propositions that, unlike some affirmatives, cannot be converted. He focuses on the temporal negative as the most specific example and uses a hierarchical argument to show that if the most specific cannot be converted, then none of the more general types can. An objection about the nature of generality is then addressed, reinforcing the logical chain.

Guided Path
1
Identify the most specific inconvertible negative
The author identifies the temporal negative (al-waqtiyyah) as the most specific type among the remaining negatives. It takes the form 'Necessarily, no J is B not always but at time T'.
This is a negative proposition with a temporal qualification.
2
Understand why temporal negative fails conversion
Given the temporal negative, the converse would be 'Necessarily, no B is J not always but at time T'. This fails because it is possible that B is always J while J is not B except at that time.
The false converse scenario: B is J at all times, so the converse would be false.
3
Trace the hierarchy upward
The author lists a chain of more general negatives: distributive, non-perpetual existential, non-necessary existential, particular possible, absolute universal, general possible. Since conversion fails at the most specific (temporal), it fails for all more general types because conversion in a general type is by virtue of its essence and would imply conversion in all its instances.
The logic: if a general type had a valid conversion, its most specific subtype would also be convertible—but it is not.
4
Analyze the objection about essence
An objector argues that the general might have a property not due to its essence, so failure in the specific might not transfer. The author responds that it is possible for a general term to have a property accidentally, but that the impossibility in the specific nevertheless necessitates impossibility in the general by entailment.
The author's response is more subtle: the general's conversion would conflict with its own entailments if the specific does not convert.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۷] وأما السوالب الباقية فإنها لا تكون منعكسة عندهم أصلا لامتناع العكس في الأخص منها، وهي الوقتية فإنه يصدق أن يقال بالضرورة لا شيء من ج ب لا دائما بل وقت كذا، أو لا يصدق بالضرورة لا شيء من ب ج لا دائما بل وقت كذا فإن من الممكن أن يكون ج ليس ب إلا في ذلك الوقت و ب هو ج دائمًا؛ ومتى امتنع العكس في الأخص منها امتنع في الأعم لما أن الانعكاس في الأعم إذا كان بحسب ذاته. وكما أنها لا تنعكس فكذلك المنتشرة التي هي أخص من الوجودية اللادائمة التي هي أخص من الوجودية اللا ضرورية التي هي أخص من الممكنة الخاصة التي هي أخص المطلقة العامة التي هي أخص من الممكنة العامة. ثم لقائل أن يقول فيه هذا من المسلمات إذا كان الانعكاس في الأعم بحسب ذاته وطبيعته؛ وفيه من الكلام فإنه لا يمتنع أن يكون الأعم متصفا بصفة لا يكون اتصافه بتلك الصفة بحسب ذاته على ما عرف. ولأن الامتناع في الأخص منها إذا كان مستلزما للامتناع في الأعم فلا يخلو من أن يكون مستلزمًا مع كون الانعكاس في الأعم مستلزما للانعكاس في الأخص؛ وإنه ممتنع فإنه لا يكون مستلزما إلا وأن يمتنع الانعكاس في الأعم ضرورة امتناع العكس في الأخص منها كما مر فيكون الانعكاس في الأعم منافيا لما يلازم
[7] As for the remaining negatives (al-sawalibu 'l-baqiyah), they are not convertible according to them (the logicians) at all, due to the impossibility of conversion in the most specific of them, namely the temporal (al-waqtiyyah). For it is true that one can say 'Necessarily, no J is B not always but at such-and-such a time', or it is not true that 'Necessarily, no B is J not always but at such-and-such a time'. For it is possible that J is not B except at that time, while B is J always. And whenever conversion is impossible in the most specific, it becomes impossible in the more general, because the conversion in the more general is according to its essence. And just as they do not convert, likewise the distributive (al-muntashirah), which is more specific than the non-perpetual existential (al-wujudiyatu 'l-la-da'imah), which is more specific than the non-necessary existential (al-wujudiyatu 'l-la-daruriyyah), which is more specific than the particular possible (al-mumkinatu 'l-khassah), which is more specific than the absolute universal (al-mutlaqatu 'l-‘ammah), which is more specific than the general possible (al-mumkinatu ‘l-‘ammah). Then an objector might say: This is among the accepted premises (musallamat) if the conversion in the more general is according to its essence and nature. But there is discussion about it: for it is not impossible that the more general is qualified by a quality that is not according to its essence, as has been known. And because if the impossibility in the most specific necessitates impossibility in the more general, then it cannot be that it necessitates while the conversion in the more general necessitates conversion in the most specific; and that is impossible, for it would necessitate unless the conversion in the more general is impossible due to the impossibility of conversion in the most specific, as previously. So the conversion in the more general would conflict with what it entails.
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This final passage discusses the remaining types of negative propositions (sawalib baqiyah) that are not convertible at all. The author identifies the temporal proposition (waqtiyyah) as the most specific: e.g., 'Necessarily, no J is B not always but at a specific time T'. The converse would be 'Necessarily, no B is J not always but at time T', but this does not follow because it is possible that B is always J while J is not B except at time T. Since conversion fails at this most specific level, it also fails for all more general types that include this specificity. The author then lists a hierarchy of more general propositions: distributive (muntashirah), non-perpetual existential, non-necessary existential, particular possible, absolute universal, general possible. All are inconvertible because they contain the temporal type as a special case. The passage then presents an objection: if we assume that conversion in the more general is according to its essence (i.e., inherently), then the impossibility in the specific would not automatically transfer to the general. The author responds that it is possible for a general term to have a property not due to its essence, and also that if impossibility in the specific necessitates impossibility in the general, then the general's conversion would require the specific's conversion, which is impossible, leading to a contradiction. The reasoning is circular: the general cannot convert because the specific does not, and the specific does not because the general does not? The author's final point seems to be that the general conversion would conflict with its own entailments. The text appears cut off, but the gist is that the inconvertibility of the most specific negatives extends upward, and objections are addressed.

Key Terms
السوالب الباقية
الوقتية
المنتشرة
بحسب ذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the temporal negative proposition (al-waqtiyyah) not convert?
A) Because its subject and predicate are not related.
B) Because it is possible for B to always be J while J is not B except at the specified time.
C) Because it contains a modal operator that prevents conversion.
D) Because it is a particular proposition.

Key Takeaway
The inconvertibility of the most specific negative proposition necessarily invalidates conversion for all more general negatives that contain it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the following temporal negative proposition from fiqh: 'No transaction involving gharar is permissible at the time of the contract.' Attempt to convert it and explain why conversion fails. Then, using the hierarchy, determine which more general negative types would also be inconvertible.
?
Identify a negative proposition in a qiyas argument from usul al-fiqh that includes a temporal condition. Show how the inconvertibility rule preserves the argument's validity.

Conversion of Special Conditional Negatives
Author refutes conversion claim and addresses an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a special conditional negative proposition converts to a proposition of the same kind and identify the correct partial converse.

Core Idea
Special conditional negatives do not convert universally—only partially.

In this passage, the author corrects a common mistake about the conversion of special conditional negatives. You will learn why 'No C is B, not always but as long as C' does not yield 'No B is C, not always but as long as B' but only a partial result. This is crucial for understanding the limits of immediate inference in modal logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of proposition
Recognize the special conditional negative (العرفية الخاصة): a negative proposition with a temporal condition, e.g., 'Nothing of C is B, not always but as long as C'. Note the structure.
2
State the claimed conversion
The mistaken claim is that the proposition converts like itself, i.e., the converse would also be a special conditional negative: 'Nothing of B is C, not always but as long as B'.
3
Test the conversion with a counterexample
The author shows that from the original, you cannot infer the converse universally. He points out that it is possible that some B is always B (i.e., always C). Under that possibility, the original is true but the converse would be false if taken as universal.
Counterexample: Suppose C is 'created thing' and B is 'eternal'. The original 'No created thing is eternal, not always but as long as it is created' may be true, but 'No eternal is created' is false if some eternal things are always eternal.
4
Determine the correct partial converse
The author states that the proper inference is only partial: 'Nothing of B is C' is true when it is B always in some cases, not always. That is, the converse holds in a modified, weaker form.
This means the converse is not of the same kind but a different type of proposition (perhaps existential or partial).
5
Connect to the hierarchy of negatives
The passage argues that if conversion fails for the most specific type (temporal negative), it fails for more general types, because conversion in the more general is only considered if it is in itself. Understand the principle: if a more specific type does not convert, the more general also does not convert unless the conversion is in its nature.
The hierarchy: temporal → existential non-permanent → existential non-necessary → special possible → absolute universal → general possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٦] وأما العرفية الخاصة فقد قيل إنها تنعكس كنفسها؛ وذلك خطأ. فإنه لا يلزم من صدق قولنا لا شيء من (ج) (ب) لا دائما بل ما دام «ج» صدق قولنا لا شيء من (ب) (ج) لا دائما بل ما دام «ب» وقد كان من الممكن أن يكون بعض «ب» هو «ب» دائمًا بل يلزم منه أن يصدق لا شيء من (ب) (ج) حين هو «ب» دائما في البعض، لا دائما في البعض. وأما أن هذا هو العكس أو عكسها هو عكس العامة؛ فالكلام فيه نحو الكلام في المشروطة الخاصة.
[۷] وأما السوالب الباقية فإنها لا تكون منعكسة عندهم أصلا لامتناع [۱۲] العكس في الأخص منها، وهي الوقتية؛ فإنه يصدق أن يقال بالضرورة لا شيء من (ج) (ب) لا دائما بل وقت كذا، أو لا يصدق بالضرورة لا شيء من (ب) (ج) لا دائما بل وقت كذا فإن من الممكن أن يكون «ج» ليس (ب) إلا في (ج) ذلك الوقت و (ب) هو (ج) دائمًا؛ ومتى امتنع العكس في الأخص منها امتنع في الأعم لما أن الانعكاس في الأعم إذا كان بحسب ذاته. وكما أنها لا تنعكس فكذلك المنتشرة التي هي أخص من الوجودية اللادائمة التي هي أخص من الوجودية اللا ضرورية التي هي أخص من الممكنة الخاصة التي هي أخص من المطلقة العامة التي هي أخص من الممكنة العامة.
ثم لقائل أن يقول: فيه هذا من المسلمات إذا كان الانعكاس في الأعم بحسب ذاته وطبيعته؛ وفيه من الكلام فإنه لا يمتنع أن يكون الأعم متصفا بصفة لا يكون اتصافه بتلك الصفة بحسب ذاته على ما عرف. ولأن الامتناع في الأخص منها إذا كان مستلزما للامتناع في الأعم فلا يخلو من أن يكون مستلزمًا مع كون الانعكاس في الأعم مستلزما للانعكاس في الأخص؛ وإنه ممتنع فإنه لا يكون مستلزما إلا وأن يمتنع الانعكاس في الأعم ضرورة امتناع العكس في الأخص منها كما مر فيكون الانعكاس في الأعم منافيا لما يلازم كونه مستلزما، وإنه مما ينافي كونه مستلزما، أو لا مع كون الانعكاس مستلزما، وإنه ممتنع أيضًا. فإن الشيء لا يكون مستلزما للشيء إلا وأن يكون نقيض اللازم مستلزما لنقيض ذلك الشيء. لكنا نقول: ما ذكرتم أولا من جملة ما يتوجه إلى شيء بحسب النظر فيه وفيما يحققه وينافيه على خلاف ما ذكرتم ثانيا؛ فإن ذلك في حيز المنع لما أن الانعكاس في الأعم لا يكون منافيا لما يلازم كونه مستلزما بل يكون داخلا فيما ينافيه وهو المجموع المركب من الامتناع في الأخص والانعكاس في الأعم كما مرَّ. ومثل هذا المنافي متحقق في كثير من الصور وهي صور تحقق ما يمتنع تحققه بدون غيره؛ وهذه الصور متعددة حسب تعدد كل أمر من الأمور الواقعة في نفس الأمر؛ إذ ما من أمر إلا ويمتنع تحققه دون تحقق غيره، وهذا ظاهر.
[6] As for the special conditional [negative], it has been said that it converts like itself; but that is an error. For it does not follow from the truth of our statement 'Nothing of C is B, not always but as long as C' the truth of our statement 'Nothing of B is C, not always but as long as B'; and it is possible that some B is B always; rather, it follows from it that 'Nothing of B is C' is true when it is B always in some [cases], not always in some. As for whether this is the converse or its converse is the converse of the universal; the discussion about it is similar to the discussion about the special conditional. [7] As for the remaining negatives, they are not convertible at all according to them due to the impossibility of conversion in the most specific of them, namely the temporal; for it is true that it is said necessarily 'Nothing of C is B, not always but at such time', and it is not true necessarily 'Nothing of B is C, not always but at such time'; for it is possible that C is not B except at that time of C and B is C always; and whenever conversion is impossible in the most specific of them, it is impossible in the more general, because the conversion in the more general is [only] if it is in itself. And just as they do not convert, so also the diffuse [negatives] which are more specific than the existential non-permanent, which are more specific than the existential non-necessary, which are more specific than the special possible, which are more specific than the absolute universal, which are more specific than the general possible. Then one might say: This is among the accepted premises if the conversion in the more general is in itself and its nature; but it is subject to discussion, for it is not impossible that the more general be qualified by an attribute such that its being qualified by that attribute is not in itself, as is known. And because the impossibility in the most specific, if it necessitates impossibility in the more general, then it is either with the conversion in the more general necessitating conversion in the most specific—and that is impossible, for it would not necessitate unless the conversion in the more general is impossible by virtue of the impossibility of conversion in the most specific, as has passed, so the conversion in the more general would be contrary to what necessitates its being necessitating—and that is contradictory to its being necessitating, or not with the conversion necessitating, and that is also impossible. For a thing does not necessitate another thing unless the contradictory of the consequent necessitates the contradictory of that thing. But we say: What you mentioned first is part of what pertains to something with respect to consideration of it and what verifies it and contradicts it, contrary to what you mentioned second; for that is subject to denial because the conversion in the more general is not contrary to what necessitates its being necessitating, but rather falls under what contradicts it, namely the composite of impossibility in the most specific and conversion in the more general, as has passed. And such a contradiction is realized in many cases, namely cases of what is impossible without another; and these cases are multiple according to the multiplicity of every matter found in reality; for there is no matter except that its realization is impossible without the realization of another, and this is clear.
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This passage is a critical exposition on the conversion of special conditional negatives in logic. The author begins by refuting the claim that the special conditional negative ('al-'urfiyya al-khassa) converts like itself. He demonstrates that from the truth of 'No C is B, not always but as long as C' does not necessarily follow 'No B is C, not always but as long as B', because it is possible that some B is always B. Instead, the truth of the original only yields that 'No B is C' is true in some cases when B is always, not universally. The author then discusses whether this resulting statement is the true converse or its converse is the converse of the universal, referring to the discussion on the special conditional ('al-mashruta al-khassa') for similar treatment. Next, the author addresses the remaining negatives, stating they are not convertible at all, using the temporal negative ('al-waqtiyya') as an example. For the temporal 'No C is B, not always but at a specific time', it is possible that C is not B except at that time, while B is always C, so the converse is not necessarily true. He argues that if conversion fails for the most specific type, it fails for more general types because conversion in the more general is only considered if it is in itself. He then lists a hierarchy of more specific negatives (diffuse, existential non-permanent, etc.) to show the point. The author then presents an objection: if we grant that conversion in the more general is in itself, then the impossibility in the most specific necessitates impossibility in the more general, leading to a dilemma: either the conversion in the more general necessitates conversion in the most specific (impossible because then the more general would contradict its own necessitation), or it does not, which is also impossible due to logical relations of necessitation and contrapositives. The author responds by denying the objection's premise: conversion in the more general is not actually contrary to what necessitates its necessitation; rather, it is included in the composite of impossibility in the most specific and conversion in the more general, which is a common scenario where something is impossible without another. This is explained as a general principle: every matter's realization is impossible without the realization of another. The author concludes the point is clear.

Key Terms
العرفية الخاصة (al-'urfiyya al-khassa): A special conditional negative proposition where the negation holds 'not always but as long as the subject exists', possibly with a temporal condition. [Note: This term is specific to later Arabic logic; in earlier texts like Aristotle, such modal distinctions are not explicit.]
الوقتية (al-waqtiyya): A negative proposition that is true 'not always but at a specific time', i.e., temporarily. [Note: Stable across the tradition; similar to temporal modalities in Stoic logic.]
المنتشرة (al-muntashira): A diffuse negative proposition, more specific than the existential non-permanent, possibly indicating a negation that holds in a distributed manner. [Note: Not widely used; may be a technical term specific to this text or its tradition.]
الانعكاس (al-in'ikās): Conversion, i.e., the immediate inference swapping subject and predicate while preserving truth conditions. [Note: Stable; standard term in Arabic logic.]
الأعم والأخص (al-aʿamm wa al-akhass): The more general and the more specific; in this context, referring to types of propositions in a hierarchy of specificity. [Note: Standard logical relation; no significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the special conditional negative 'No C is B, not always but as long as C' not necessarily imply 'No B is C, not always but as long as B'?
A) Because the converse requires B to be always C, which is not guaranteed.
B) Because it is possible that some B is always B, and the original only guarantees negation when C is as long as C.
C) Because conversion only works for universal affirmatives.
D) Because the time conditions are incompatible.

Key Takeaway
Special conditional negatives do not convert universally; their converse is only partial, and the failure of conversion in more specific types propagates to more general types unless conversion is in the nature of the latter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task: Given the proposition 'No intoxicant is permissible, not always but as long as it is intoxicating' (a special conditional negative), determine what the conversion would yield and why it is not a full conversion. Use the author's reasoning from the passage.
?
Task: In a Fiqh context, consider the proposition 'No wine is halal, not always but as long as it is wine'. Would converting it to 'No halal is wine, not always but as long as it is halal' be valid? Explain using the hierarchy of negatives discussed.

Particular Negatives Do Not Convert
Author explains that particular negatives never convert due to the impossibility of subsuming the more specific under the more general.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why particular negative propositions do not convert and test a given proposition pair for conversion validity.

Core Idea
A particular negative never converts: you can't infer 'Some B is not A' from 'Some A is not B'.

In this passage, the author explains a key rule of categorical logic: particular negatives (O propositions) never convert. He shows that the direction of generality prevents the conversion. Let's walk through his reasoning with the example of 'human' and 'writer'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Recognize that the proposition is a particular negative, i.e., 'Some A is not B'. In the example, 'Some human is not a writer' – here 'human' is the subject (A) and 'writer' is the predicate (B).
2
Determine the generality relation
Assess which term is more general (aʿamm) and which is more specific (akhass). In the example, 'human' is more general (since it includes both writers and non-writers), and 'writer' is more specific. The author notes that it's possible to deny the specific of the general (some humans are not writers), but not vice versa.
The text states: if C is more general than B, then 'not every C is B' can be true, but 'not every B is C' will be false.
3
Test the conversion
Attempt the conversion: from 'Some A is not B' to 'Some B is not A'. In our example, 'Some writer is not a human' is false because every writer is necessarily human (in logical terminology). Hence the conversion fails.
The author emphasizes that this failure holds universally, not just for this example.
4
Apply the rule to a new example
Use the genus-species relation: 'Some animal is not human' is true, but 'Some human is not animal' is false. This confirms that particular negatives do not convert.
Think of any such pair: the denial of a specific from a general never implies the denial of the general from the specific.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجزئية من السوالب فإنها من حيث هي هي لا تنعكس أصلا في أي مادة كانت وبأي جهة. فإنه يمكن سلب الأخص عن الأعم في جميع الصور سلبًا جزئيا ولا يمكن سلب الأعم عن الأخص البتة فلا يلزم من صدق قولنا ليس كل ج ب صدق قولنا ليس كل ب ج إذا كان «ج» أعم من «ب»، فانظر فيما ترى من الأجناس وأنواعها، ومن الأنواع وأصنافها فإنه يمكن سلب النوع عن بعض الجنس، وسلب الصنف عن بعض النوع؛ ولا يمكن البتة سلب الجنس عن بعض النوع، ولا سلب النوع عن بعض الصنف. وعلى هذا في سائر الأمور العامة وخواصها؛ إذ يصدق أن يقال بعض الإنسان ليس بكاتب، ولا يصدق أن يقال بعض الكاتب ليس بإنسان، فإن الكاتب لا يكون إلا وأن يكون إنسانًا بالضرورة، ولا يقال إنما يكون كذلك إذا كان الكاتب عبارة عن إنسان له الكتابة. أما إذا كان عبارة عن شيء له الكتابة فلا. فإن الكاتب وإن كان بحسب اللغة عبارة عن شيء له الكتابة فبحسب الاصطلاح عبارة عن إنسان له الكتابة.
As for the particular negative, it is in itself not convertible at all in any matter and in any mode. For it is possible to negate the more specific of the more general in all cases partially, and it is never possible to negate the more general of the more specific, so from the truth of our statement 'Not every C is B' does not follow the truth of 'Not every B is C' if C is more general than B. Consider what you see of genera and their species, and of species and their varieties; for it is possible to negate the species of some of the genus, and to negate the variety of some of the species; but it is never possible to negate the genus of some of the species, nor to negate the species of some of the variety. And similarly for all general matters and their properties; for it is true to say 'Some human is not a writer', but it is not true to say 'Some writer is not a human', for a writer cannot be unless he is necessarily a human. And it is not said that it is only thus if 'writer' means a human who writes. But if it means a thing that writes, then not. For 'writer', even if linguistically it means a thing that writes, terminologically it means a human who writes.
Source pages p. 465

Commentary
This passage addresses the conversion of particular negative propositions. The author states that a particular negative, such as 'Some C is not B', never converts to 'Some B is not C'. The reason is based on the logical relationship between the more general and the more specific: it is always possible to deny the more specific of the more general partially (e.g., 'Some animal is not human'), but the reverse—denying the more general of the more specific—is impossible (e.g., 'Some human is not animal' is false). The author illustrates with genera and species: one can say 'Some genus is not this species' but not 'Some species is not this genus'. Then he provides a concrete example: 'Some human is not a writer' is true, but 'Some writer is not a human' is false because a writer is necessarily a human. The author also notes that this depends on the definition of 'writer': if by language it means any thing that writes, then the conversion might hold, but in logical terminology, 'writer' is specifically a human who writes. Thus, the particular negative does not convert in standard usage.

Key Terms
السالبة الجزئية
الأخص والأعم
الكاتب

Checkpoint

A) Because 'writer' is more specific than 'human'.
B) Because a writer must be human, so the second statement is false.
C) Because particular negatives never convert under any circumstances.
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
A particular negative proposition never converts because the more specific term cannot be denied of the more general term in the reverse direction.

Why Particular Negatives Do Not Convert
Author states that particular negatives do not convert and rebuts an objection with genus-species examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why a particular negative proposition (saliba juz'iyya) has no valid converse, and resolve apparent counterexamples by analyzing the genus-species relation.

Core Idea
The specific can be denied of the general, but the general cannot be denied of the specific.

This passage tackles a key rule of immediate inference: particular negatives do not convert. The author demonstrates this using the logical relation of genus and species, where the more general term cannot be negated from the more specific. He then addresses a subtle objection that seems to violate the rule, showing that the objection only works when the terms are not in a strict genus-species hierarchy. Mastering this principle is essential for correctly handling negations in Islamic argumentation, especially in usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conversion rule
Locate the opening statement: 'As for the particular negative, it as such does not convert at all, in any matter or mode.' This is the absolute rule being defended.
The rule applies to all particular negatives, regardless of the terms' content.
2
Extract the logical reason
The author explains using the general-specific relation: if J is more general than B, then 'Not every J is B' can be true, but 'Not every B is J' is false because B is entirely contained under J. Example: 'Not every human is a writer' (true) vs. 'Not every writer is a human' (false, since every writer is human).
The impossibility of negating the general from the specific is the core argument.
3
Analyze the objection
The objector says 'Some human is not a scholar' and 'Some scholar is not a human' both seem true. This would violate the rule. Understand why this appears to be a counterexample: it seems like both terms can be negated from each other.
The objection rests on an apparent symmetry that does not hold in strict genus-species relations.
4
Understand the author's resolution
The author distinguishes absolute and contextual generality. In the objection, 'human' is more general than 'scholar' in one direction, and 'scholar' is more general than 'human' only if we consider 'scholar' as a genus and 'human' as a species—which is not the natural order. When both terms overlap, the rule does not apply because there is no unidirectional genus-species relation. The truth of 'Some scholar is not a human' would require 'scholar' to be a broader category, which it is not in normal usage.
The rule only holds when one term is truly more general (genus) and the other more specific (species).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجزئية من السوالب فإنها من حيث هي هي لا تنعكس أصلا في أي مادة كانت وبأي جهة. فإنه يمكن سلب الأخص عن الأعم في جميع الصور سلبا جزئيا ولا يمكن سلب الأعم عن الأخص البتة فلا يلزم من صدق قولنا ليس كل ج ب صدق قولنا ليس كل ب ج إذا كان ج أعم من ب. فانظر فيما ترى من الأجناس وأنواعها، ومن الأنواع وأصنافها فإنه يمكن سلب النوع عن بعض الجنس، وسلب الصنف عن بعض النوع؛ ولا يمكن البتة سلب الجنس عن بعض النوع، ولا سلب النوع عن بعض الصنف. وعلى هذا في سائر الأمور العامة وخواصها؛ إذ يصدق أن يقال بعض الإنسان ليس بكاتب، ولا يصدق أن يقال بعض الكاتب ليس بإنسان، فإن الكاتب لا يكون إلا وأن يكون إنسانا بالضرورة، ولا يقال إنما يكون كذلك إذا كان الكاتب عبارة عن إنسان له الكتابة. أما إذا كان عبارة عن شيء له الكتابة فلا. فإن الكاتب وإن كان بحسب اللغة عبارة عن شيء له الكتابة فبحسب الاصطلاح عبارة عن إنسان له الكتابة. وإن قيل هب أنه كذلك لكنه يشكل بقولنا بعض الإنسان ليس بعالم بعض العالم ليس بإنسان مثلا؛ وقد صدق كل واحد منهما بالضرورة. فنقول: الصدق في هذا وذلك بحسب أن يكون المسلوب عنه أعم في مثل هذه الصورة لا بحسب أن يكون الإنسان مطلقا أعم من العالم مطلقا، والعالم مطلقا أعم من الإنسان مطلقا؛ وذلك لا يمكن بل الإنسان أعم من العالم الذي هو تحته، والعالم من الإنسان الذي هو تحته كذلك. وكان لقائل أن يقول بعض الإنسان ليس بعالم هو إنسان، وبعض العالم ليس بإنسان هو عالم؛ وهذا من جملة ما قد مر الكلام فيه فاعتبر بما عرفت وبالله التوفيق.
As for the particular negative, it as such does not convert at all, in any matter or mode. For it is possible to negate the more specific from the more general in all forms as a particular negation, and it is not possible to negate the more general from the more specific at all. So from the truth of our saying 'Not every J is B' it does not follow the truth of our saying 'Not every B is J' when J is more general than B. So consider what you see of genera and their species, and of species and their varieties: it is possible to negate the species from some of the genus, and to negate the variety from some of the species; but it is not at all possible to negate the genus from some of the species, nor to negate the species from some of the variety. And likewise in all universal matters and their properties. For it is true to say 'Some human is not a writer', but it is not true to say 'Some writer is not a human', for the writer is necessarily only a human. And it is not said that it is so only if 'writer' means a human who writes; if it means something that writes, then not. For 'writer' in language means something that writes, but in technical terminology it means a human who writes. And if it is said: Suppose that is so, but it is problematic by our saying 'Some human is not a scholar' and 'Some scholar is not a human' for example; both are necessarily true. So we say: The truth in this and that is according to the negated-from being more general in such a form, not according to 'human' absolutely being more general than 'scholar' absolutely, nor 'scholar' absolutely being more general than 'human' absolutely. That cannot be; rather, 'human' is more general than the 'scholar' that is under it, and 'scholar' is more general than the 'human' that is under it likewise. And someone might say: 'Some human is not a scholar' is a human, and 'Some scholar is not a human' is a scholar; this is part of what has been discussed before, so consider what you know, and with God is success.
Source pages p. 465

Commentary
This passage defines the conversion rule for particular negative propositions. The author states that particular negatives do not convert at all, regardless of matter or mode. The reason is that one can negate the more specific term from the more general (e.g., 'Not every human is a writer') but never the reverse: you cannot negate the more general from the more specific. For instance, 'Not every writer is a human' is false because every writer is necessarily human. The author then addresses an objection: 'Some human is not a scholar' and 'Some scholar is not a human' both seem true. The response is that in these statements the negated term is considered only within a restricted sense: 'human' is more general than 'scholar' in the first, and 'scholar' more general than 'human' in the second, not absolutely. The passage uses examples from genus-species relations in the Islamic intellectual tradition, showing that these logical rules apply to natural kinds like human and writer, scholar, etc.

Key Terms
السالبة الجزئية
الانعكاس
الأعم
الأخص

Particular negative propositions never convert because the general term cannot be negated from the specific term in a valid converse.

Conversion of Universal Negatives and Accidental Attributes
Author refutes the view that universal negatives convert as themselves.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a universal negative proposition converts as itself and identify cases where conversion fails due to accidental attributes.

Core Idea
A universal negative does not always convert; accidental attributes block valid conversion.

In this passage, the author challenges the common assumption that 'No J is B' automatically converts to 'No B is J'. He shows that when the subject has accidental attributes that the predicate lacks, conversion leads to falsehood. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correct logical reasoning in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Original Proposition
Find the universal negative proposition in the form 'No J is B'. For example, 'No human is a laugher'.
The proposition must be absolute universal negative (سالبة كلية مطلقة عامة).
2
State the Proposed Converse
The converse would be 'No B is J'. Test whether this is true in all cases.
The earlier logicians claimed this always holds.
3
Check for Accidental Attributes
Examine whether the predicate (B) has an accidental attribute that is separable from the subject (J). If B is an accidental attribute of J, then 'No B is J' may be false.
For example, laughter is accidental to humans; a laugher is necessarily human, so 'No laugher is a human' is false.
4
Test with Examples
Apply the test to the given examples: 'No human is a laugher' (true) does not convert to 'No laugher is a human' (false). Similarly, 'No human is a respirator' fails conversion.
The author uses these to refute the earlier view.
5
Conclude on Conversion Validity
A universal negative converts as itself only if the predicate is not an accidental attribute that necessarily applies to the subject. Otherwise, conversion is invalid.
The necessary universal negative (e.g., 'No human is a stone') may convert, but non-necessary ones may not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذه كلها ما ذهب المتقدمون في السوالب الكلية والجزئية وانعكاسهما. وأما المتقدمون من الحكماء فعن الجمهور منهم أن السالبة الكلية المطلقة العامة تنعكس كنفسها، فإنه لا يصدق قولنا لا شيء من ج ب إلا ويصدق لا شيء من ب ج، فإنه إذا لم يصدق هذا فقد صدق نقيضه؛ فيفرض شيء واحد هو ب و ج فيكون ذلك الجيم ب وقد كان لا شيء من ج ب، هذا خلف. غير أنه ليس بخلف فإن قولنا بعض ج ب لا يناقضه لا شيء من ج ب على الإطلاق كما مر. والحق أنها لا تنعكس كنفسها فإن الموضوع الذي له أعراض مفارقة يمكن سلبها عنه ولا يمكن سلبها عنها فيقال مثلا لا شيء من الإنسان بضاحك بالإطلاق العام، ولا يقال لا شيء من الضاحك بإنسان بالإطلاق العام؛ إذ كل ضاحك إنسان بالضرورة. وكذلك إذا قلت لا شيء من الإنسان بمتنفس بالإطلاق العام فإنه لا يمكن أن يقال لا شيء من المتنفس بإنسان بالإطلاق العام، وقد كان المتنفس من الإنسان إنسانا بالضرورة. ثم عن البعض منهم أن السالبة الكلية الضرورية لما كانت منعكسة كنفسها كان من الواجب أن تكون السالبة الكلية اللا ضرورية منعكسة كنفسها؛ لأنه إذا
All this is what the earlier ones held regarding universal and particular negatives and their conversion. As for the earlier ones among the philosophers, the majority held that the absolute universal negative converts as itself: for our saying 'No J is B' is not true unless 'No B is J' is true. For if this is not true, then its contradictory is true; then one assumes a single thing that is B and J, so that J would be B, yet there was 'No J is B' – this is a contradiction. But it is not a contradiction, for our saying 'Some J is B' does not contradict 'No J is B' absolutely, as has passed. The truth is that it does not convert as itself, because the subject may have accidental attributes that can be negated from it but cannot be negated from them: for example, one says 'No human is a laugher' absolutely universally, but one does not say 'No laugher is a human' absolutely universally, for every laugher is necessarily human. Likewise if you say 'No human is a respirator' absolutely universally, one cannot say 'No respirator is a human' absolutely universally, for respirator from human is necessarily human. Then some of them held that since the necessary universal negative converts as itself, it must be that the non-necessary universal negative also converts as itself, because if...
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage discusses the conversion of universal negative propositions. The author first reports the view of the majority of earlier philosophers (al-mutaqaddimūn min al-ḥukamā') that an absolute universal negative (e.g., 'No J is B') converts as itself to 'No B is J'. They argue by contradiction: if it did not convert, then the contradictory 'Some J is B' would be true, leading to a contradiction. The author rebuts this, saying it is not a valid contradiction because 'Some J is B' does not contradict 'No J is B' (since the latter is universal and the former particular). The correct view, the author asserts, is that the universal negative does not convert as itself. The reason is that the subject may have accidental attributes (a‘rāḍ mufāriqa) that can be separated from it but not from the predicate. For example, 'No human is a laugher' is true universally because laughter is accidental to humans, but 'No laugher is a human' is false because every laugher is essentially human. Similarly, 'No human is a respirator' is true, but 'No respirator is a human' is false. Finally, some philosophers argue that since necessary universal negatives convert, non-necessary ones must also convert, but the passage cuts off before completing the argument.

Objection and response on partial negative propositions
Author refutes an objection about the truth of partial negatives like 'some man is not learned'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the truth conditions of partial negative propositions and explain why both 'some A is not B' and 'some B is not A' can be true simultaneously using the concept of relative generality.

Core Idea
Partial negatives can both be true when each subject is considered in its own context of generality, not absolutely.

This passage tackles a common objection against the conversion rules for partial negatives. The objector presents two seemingly contradictory propositions that are both true. The author's response reveals a subtle but crucial distinction about generality that resolves the puzzle. By understanding this, you'll sharpen your ability to handle immediate inferences in logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector presents two partial negatives: 'some man is not learned' and 'some learned is not man'. Both appear necessarily true, yet they seem to conflict with the general rule that partial negatives cannot be converted.
2
Understand the author's response
The author argues that truth in such cases depends on the subject being more general in that specific context, not on absolute generality between the terms.
3
Distinguish absolute vs contextual generality
Absolute generality means one term is universally broader (e.g., man is absolutely more general than learned only if all learned are men). Contextual generality means in a particular proposition, the subject is taken in a sense that makes it more general than the predicate.
4
Apply the distinction
In 'some man is not learned', 'man' is taken generally, so it is more general than 'learned' in that context. In 'some learned is not man', 'learned' is taken generally (including non-human learned beings?), so it is more general. Thus both can be true without contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قيل هب أنه كذلك لكنه يشكل بقولنا بعض الإنسان ليس بعالم بعض العالم ليس بإنسان مثلا؛ وقد صدق كل واحد منهما بالضرورة. فنقول: الصدق في هذا وذلك بحسب أن يكون المسلوب عنه أعم في مثل هذه الصورة لا بحسب أن يكون الإنسان مطلقا أعم من العالم مطلقا، والعالم مطلقا أعم من الإنسان مطلقًا؛ وذلك لا يمكن بل الإنسان أعم من العالم الذي هو تحته، والعالم من الإنسان الذي هو تحته كذلك. وكان لقائل أن يقول بعض الإنسان ليس بعالم هو إنسان، وبعض العالم ليس بإنسان هو عالم؛ وهذا من جملة ما قد مرَّ الكلام فيه فاعتبر بما عرفت وبالله التوفيق.
And if it is said: Assume it is so, but it is problematic by our statement 'Some man is not learned' and 'Some learned is not man' for example; and each of them is necessarily true. Then we say: Truth in this and that is according to the subject from which something is negated being more general in such a form, not according to man being absolutely more general than learned, and learned being absolutely more general than man; and that is not possible, rather man is more general than the learned that falls under him, and learned is more general than the man that falls under it likewise. And someone could say: 'Some man is not learned' is man, and 'Some learned is not man' is learned; and this is among what has already been discussed, so consider what you know, and with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection against a previous claim about the conversion of negatives. The objection presents two seemingly contradictory true partial negatives: 'Some man is not learned' and 'Some learned is not man'. Both appear to be true necessarily, yet if one were to convert them, they would not preserve truth in the same way. The author explains that the truth of these propositions depends on the relative generality of the subject in each particular case, not on absolute generality. Man is more general than learned only in a particular sense (when learned is a species under man), and learned is more general than man only when man is a species under learned. Thus, the truth of 'some man is not learned' is about man in its general sense, while 'some learned is not man' is about learned in a specific sense. The author then notes that someone might argue that the subject of 'some man is not learned' is man, and of 'some learned is not man' is learned, but this has been addressed earlier. The final phrase instructs the reader to reflect on what has been explained.

Why Universal Negative Absolute Propositions Do Not Convert Simply
Author critiques earlier philosophers on converting universal negatives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a universal negative absolute proposition fails to convert to itself, using the criterion of accidental predicates.

Core Idea
A universal negative absolute proposition does not convert if the predicate is an accidental attribute of the subject.

In this passage, the author examines the conversion of universal negative absolute propositions, such as 'No man is laughable'. The earlier philosophers argued that such a proposition always converts to itself, i.e., 'No laughable is man'. But the author shows this is false when the predicate is an accidental attribute that can be absent from the subject, while every instance of the predicate necessarily shares the subject. This insight is crucial for correctly applying conversion in logical arguments, especially in theological and legal reasoning where definitions and attributes matter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition
Find a universal negative absolute proposition (No S is P). Note that it is absolute (non-modal) and general in scope. For example, 'No man is laughable' (لا شيء من الإنسان بضاحك بالإطلاق العام).
The proposition must be true in the absolute sense, not necessary or temporal.
2
Understand the earlier philosophers' claim
They held that such a proposition converts to itself: if No J is B, then No B is J. Their proof was by contradiction: if No B is J is false, then Some B is J is true, implying a contradiction.
They assumed that Some B is J directly contradicts No J is B, but the author points out that is not a strict contradiction.
3
Spot the flaw in their reasoning
The contradictory of 'No J is B' is 'Some J is B', not 'Some B is J'. Their assumption that 'Some B is J' contradicts the original is invalid. The conversion does not follow by contradiction.
This is a formal logical error.
4
Apply the author's counterexample with accidental predicates
Consider 'No man is laughable' (absolute universal negative). This is true because laughable is an accidental attribute that can be absent. If we convert to 'No laughable is man', it is false because every laughable is necessarily a man. Hence conversion fails.
The key is that the predicate (laughable) is a separable accident (عرض مفارق).
5
Generalize the condition for failure
A universal negative absolute proposition fails to convert when the predicate is an accidental attribute that can be truly negated from the subject, but the subject cannot be truly negated from the predicate because every instance of the predicate necessarily implies the subject.
If the predicate is not accidental (e.g., necessary), conversion may be valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذه كلها ما ذهب المتقدمون في السوالب الكلية والجزئية وانعكاسهما. وأما المتقدمون من الحكماء فعن الجمهور منهم أن السالبة الكلية المطلقة العامة تنعكس كنفسها، فإنه لا يصدق قولنا لا شيء من ج ب» إلا ويصدق لا شيء من «بج»، فإنه إذا لم يصدق هذا فقد صدق نقيضه؛ فيفرض شيء واحد هو «ب» و «ج» فيكون ذلك الجيم «ب» وقد كان لا شيء من «ج ب»، هذا خلف. غير أنه ليس بخلف فإن قولنا بعض ج ب لا يناقضه لا شيء من «ج ب على الإطلاق كما مر . والحق أنها لا تنعكس كنفسها فإن الموضوع الذي له أعراض مفارقة يمكن سلبها عنه ولا يمكن سلبها عنها فيقال مثلا لا شيء من الإنسان بضاحك بالإطلاق العام، ولا يقال لا شيء من الضاحك بإنسان بالإطلاق العام؛ إذ كل ضاحك إنسان بالضرورة. وكذلك إذا قلت لا شيء من الإنسان بمتنفس بالإطلاق العام فإنه لا يمكن أن يقال لا شيء من المتنفس بإنسان بالإطلاق العام، وقد كان المتنفس من الإنسان إنسانًا بالضرورة.
These are all the views of the earlier scholars regarding universal and partial negatives and their conversion. As for the earlier philosophers, the majority of them held that the universal negative absolute general converts to itself, for ‘No J is B’ is not true unless ‘No B is J’ is true. For if this is not true, then its contradictory is true; then a single thing is assumed to be B and J, so that J is B, but it was ‘No J is B’ — this is a contradiction. But it is not a contradiction, for ‘Some J is B’ does not contradict ‘No J is B’ absolutely as has passed. The truth is that it does not convert to itself, for a subject that has accidental attributes can have those attributes negated from it but cannot have them negated from the attribute. For example, one says ‘No man is laughable’ with absolute generality, but one does not say ‘No laughable is man’ with absolute generality, for every laughable is necessarily man. Similarly, if you say ‘No man is breathing’ with absolute generality, it cannot be said ‘No breathing is man’ with absolute generality, while breathing from man is necessarily man.
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage discusses the conversion of the universal negative absolute proposition (e.g., 'No J is B'). The author first reports the view of the majority of earlier philosophers: that such a proposition converts to itself (i.e., 'No B is J'). They argued by contradiction: if 'No B is J' is false, then its contradictory 'Some B is J' is true, which would imply that a thing is both B and J, contradicting the original 'No J is B'. The author points out a flaw: 'Some B is J' is not the direct contradictory of 'No J is B' in all respects, as previously explained. Then the author states the correct view: the universal negative absolute does not convert to itself. The reason is that the subject may have accidental predicates that can be truly negated from the subject but not from the predicate. For example, 'No man is laughable' is true in an absolute sense (since laughable is accidental to man and can be absent), but 'No laughable is man' is false because every laughable is necessarily a man. Similarly, 'No man is breathing' (since some men do not breathe, e.g., dead) is true absolutely, but 'No breathing is man' is false. Thus, conversion fails.

Key Terms
سالبة كلية مطلقة عامة
عرض مفارق

D) Because the original proposition is false.

Key Takeaway
A universal negative absolute proposition does not convert when the predicate is an accidental attribute that can truly be denied of the subject but is always true of the subject when affirmed.

Why Non-Necessary Negatives Do Not Convert
Author argues that non-necessary negatives do not convert to themselves or others.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a non-necessary negative proposition can be validly converted, using the contradiction argument from this passage.

Core Idea
Non-necessary negations cannot convert because their truth does not force the converse to be necessary.

In modal logic, not all propositions behave the same under conversion. This passage tackles a subtle case: universal negatives that hold contingently. You will learn why such propositions are stubborn: they cannot be flipped without losing truth, even to themselves. The author's argument is a clean reductio supported by everyday examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition
Start with a universal negative proposition where the negation is not necessary: e.g., 'No A is B, but not necessarily so.' In Arabic: 'salb al-shay' 'an ghayrihi la bi-al-darurah.'
The modality 'not necessary' is key.
2
Assume conversion
Suppose this proposition does convert: its converse 'No B is A' would also be not necessary. The author tests this by assuming the converse were necessary instead.
3
Derive a contradiction
If the converse were necessary, then by contraposition the original would also be necessary. But we started with it being not necessary. That is a contradiction (خلف). Therefore the converse cannot be necessary.
This is a reductio proof that the converse is also not necessary.
4
Check conversion to itself
Now consider whether the proposition can convert to itself (i.e., the same form with swapped terms). The author says no, because many counterexamples exist where A can be denied of B but B cannot be denied of A.
Example: 'No stone is human' is necessary; but if it were non-necessary, one could imagine a stone turning into a human? Actually better: 'No animal is a plant' is necessary; non-necessary examples require contingent situations.
5
Conclude impossibility
Since both types of conversion fail, non-necessary negatives are not convertible at all. This is the standard view of the majority of logicians.
The passage ends by affirming that this is well-known among al-jumhur.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كان سلب الشيء عن غيره لا بالضرورة كان سلب ذلك الغير عنه لا بالضرورة أيضًا؛ إذ لو كان كذلك بالضرورة كان سلب الشيء عنه بالضرورة كذلك؛ وقد كان لا بالضرورة، هذا خلف. غير أنه وإن كان يتوهم أنها تكون منعكسة كنفسها فلا يمكن أن تكون منعكسة لا كنفسها / ولا كغير نفسها. فإنها لا تكون منعكسة كنفسها إلا وأن تكون منعكسة في نفسها، ولا يمكن أن تكون منعكسة في نفسها لا هي ولا ما هي بمثلها لما أمكن انسلاب الشيء عن غيره من غير أن يمكن انسلاب ذلك الغير عنه، وقد أمكن مثل هذا الانسلاب في كثير من الصور على ما عرف من قبل فلا يمكن انعكاسها إذن فهذه كلها من جملة ما هو المشهور عند الجمهور.
If the negation of a thing from another is not necessary, then the negation of that other from it is also not necessary; for if it were necessary, then the negation of the thing from it would also be necessary; but it was not necessary, which is a contradiction. However, even if it is imagined that they are convertible as themselves, it is impossible for them to be convertible as themselves or as other than themselves. For they are not convertible as themselves unless they are convertible in themselves, and it is impossible for them to be convertible in themselves, neither they nor those like them, because it is possible for a thing to be stripped from another without it being possible to strip that other from it. Such stripping has been possible in many cases as known before, so their conversion is impossible. Thus all these are among what is well-known among the majority.
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage discusses the conversion of negative propositions that are not necessary. The author begins with a logical argument: if the negation of A from B is not necessary, then the negation of B from A is also not necessary; otherwise, a contradiction arises. Then the author addresses a misconception: one might think such propositions could convert to themselves (i.e., the same form with swapped terms). The author argues that this is impossible because for a proposition to be convertible as itself, it must be convertible in itself, which is not the case here. The reason is that it is possible for A to be denied of B without it being possible to deny B of A, as seen in many examples. Therefore, these non-necessary negatives do not convert. This is the established view among the majority of scholars (al-jumhūr).

Contraposition of Negatives: Conditions and Challenges
Marginal note explains contraposition of particular negatives and different views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the conditions for contraposition of negative categorical propositions, distinguishing between external and essential propositions.

Core Idea
Contraposition of negatives is not uniform; it depends on quantity, modality, and whether the proposition is khārijiyya or ḥaqīqiyya.

This marginal note from a medieval advanced logic text delves into the nuanced rules of contraposition for negative propositions. It shows that the simple rule does not apply uniformly: the quantifier (universal vs. particular) and the modality (external vs. essential) affect whether and how the contrapositive holds. We will unpack the arguments and the writer's detailed classification.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two negative propositions
The note begins with a universal negative formulated as 'Some of what is not B is not J' (i.e., a particular negative) and a particular negative formulated as 'Not every what is not B is not J'. Notice the unusual phrasing; these are the contrapositives of standard negatives.
The notation uses 'ب' and 'ج' as logical variables for subject and predicate.
2
Understand the reductio argument for the first proposition
If the particular negative is false, its contradictory (universal affirmative) becomes true. Converting that yields 'Every J is B', which contradicts the original falsehood. This shows the contrapositive must be true.
This is a standard logical proof by contradiction.
3
Note the modality condition for the converse
The author says the converse must be absolute even if the original is modal, unless the original allows general possibility. This ensures the proof holds for all cases.
Modality affects the validity of conversion.
4
Classify propositions as external (khārijiyya) or essential (ḥaqīqiyya)
External propositions depend on existence; essential ones do not. This classification is crucial because it determines whether a contrapositive converts to an affirmative that requires existence.
The writer enumerates conversion rules for each type.
5
Apply the classification to specific cases
For the eleven propositions (excluding the two particulars), external negatives do not convert to more general affirmatives; essential negatives do not convert to affirmatives requiring existence. Various conversion outcomes are listed for different considerations.
The detailed rules are referenced from earlier sections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The universal negative is our saying 'Some of what is not B is not J' and the other is the particular negative mentioned in the contrapositive of the particular negative, which is our saying 'Not every what is not B is not J'. The case of the negative in contraposition does not differ by quantity except in things specific to universals similar to it in its subject. They argued for the first: if 'Some of what is not B is not J' is not true, then 'Nothing of what is not B is J' is true, and its converse is 'Nothing of J is not B', and it follows that 'Every J is B', which contradicts the original proposition. And you know that the modality of the converse must be absolute even if the proposition is with its cause, otherwise it is with general possibility, so that the proof for the second is complete: if it were not true, then 'Every what is not B is not J' would be true always or necessarily, and then 'Every J is B' would follow; this is a contradiction. As for us, we prefer the statement according to what appeared to us from the detailed statement on the contrapositive of negatives, and we say: As for the eleven propositions, meaning except for the two particular ones, that if they are external, they do not convert to the affirmative that is more general than their affirmatives for every consideration from its perspective, so the matter is more apparent therein. And if they are true in themselves (ḥaqīqiyyatān), they do not convert to what depends for its truth on the existence of the essence in the external world among affirmatives, because truth in the original does not depend on that, and they are with a negated subject according to the first three considerations, and negative according to the first and third; and they convert to what does not depend on that by way of coincidence, and they are with a negated subject according to the fourth consideration, and negative according to the second. And from negatives they convert to a negative of the subject according to the first or third consideration generally in the original, and to the second existentially, and to the fourth as itself, and to a negated subject according to the first consideration generally in the original, and to the second as itself, and to the third by way of coincidence necessarily. The reason for all that is known from what preceded; and thereby the whole concludes in affirmatives. For the Shaykh mentioned in its contrapositive two propositions, and the Imām transmitted them from him in al-Mulakhkhaṣ: one of them is the affirmative that he mentioned in the contrapositive of the negative.
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This marginal note is a detailed exposition on the contrapositive (ʿaks al-naqīḍ) of negative propositions. It distinguishes between two types: a universal negative expressed as 'Some of what is not B is not J' (which is actually a particular negative) and a particular negative expressed as 'Not every what is not B is not J'. The writer notes that the contrapositive behavior differs by quantity only in certain universal cases. The first argument is presented: if the particular negative 'Some of what is not B is not J' is false, then its contradictory 'Nothing of what is not B is J' must be true, and its converse 'Nothing of J is not B' yields 'Every J is B', contradicting the original falsity. The modality condition is then discussed: the converse must be absolute even if the original is necessitated, unless the original allows general possibility. The writer then gives their own preferred view: for the 'eleven propositions' (the usual 12 categoricals minus the two particulars?), if they are external (khārijiyya) they do not convert to a more general affirmative; if they are essential (ḥaqīqiyya), they do not convert to afirmatives that require existence in the external world. Various cases are enumerated for how they convert to other forms with negated subjects. The passage ends by referencing the Shaykh (Ibn Sīnā) who mentioned two propositions regarding contrapositive in al-Mulakhkhaṣ.

Conversion of Necessary Universal Negatives
Author discusses conversion of necessary universal negatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a necessary universal negative proposition converts as itself or to a perpetual negative, based on modal distinctions.

Core Idea
Necessary universal negatives convert as themselves because incompatibility is symmetric.

In modal logic, not all propositions convert the same way. This passage from the Mantiq Kabir examines the conversion of necessary universal negatives, presenting three different views. By analyzing the majority opinion and its alternatives, you will learn how to handle modal conversion in Islamic logical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Recognize the 'universal necessary negative' (sāliba kulliyya ḍarūriyya): a proposition stating that no member of the subject class is a member of the predicate class, with necessity. Understand that its meaning is the essential incompatibility of subject and predicate.
2
Understand the majority argument
The majority says it converts as itself because if J and B are essentially impossible to coexist, then B and J are also essentially impossible; incompatibility is symmetric.
3
Examine the alternative views
Some early scholars say it converts to a perpetual negative (dāʾima): necessary implies perpetual, and perpetual converts as itself, so the necessary indirectly converts to perpetual. Others distinguish essential (ḥaqīqiyya) vs external (khārijiyya): essential converts as itself, external does not.
4
Analyze the author's example
The author gives: 'Nothing that enters this house at this time is a human necessarily' is true if only non-humans enter, but its converse 'No human enters this house at that time necessarily' is false because a human could enter. This shows the failure of conversion to a necessary in external cases.
5
Apply modal distinctions
Distinguish essential necessity (based on essence) from external necessity (based on time-bound existence). Use this to decide which conversion view applies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: فيما يتعلق بالسوالب. واعلم أن السالبة الكلية الضرورية تنعكس كنفسها كما وجهةً عند الجمهور منهم «حقيقية» كانت أو خارجية» لما مر من قبل. ولأن المعنى من قولنا لا شيء من ج) (ب) بالضرورة أن الجيم والباء يستحيل اجتماعهما لذاتهما. ولما استحال اجتماع الجيم مع الباء استحال اجتماع الباء مع الجيم لما أن المنافاة بين الشيئين لا تكون مختصة بأحد الجانبين. وعند قوم من المتقدمين أنها لا تنعكس كنفسها في الجهة لكنها تنعكس سالبة كلية دائمة لاستلزام السالبة الكلية الضرورية السالبة الكلية الدائمة والسالبة الكلية الدائمة تنعكس كنفسها لما مر ؛ وقد كان من الواجب أن يكون المستلزم للمستلزم للشيء مستلزما لذلك الشيء ومنهم من قال: إنها إذا كانت حقيقية تنعكس كنفسها كما ذهب إليه الجمهور. فأما إذا كانت خارجية فلا تنعكس كنفسها لما مر في الموجبة من الممكنات أنه لا يمتنع أن يكون كل واحد من النوعين المتباينين متصفّا بصفة وكانت تلك الصفة لأحدهما بالفعل دون الثاني. والحق من جملة هذه الأقوال هو الأول، فإنه لا دليل على الثاني والثالث كما ينبغي؛ أما على الثاني فلأنه يتوقف في الأول، وأما على الثالث فلأنه يتكلف فيه؛ فنقول يصدق قولنا لا شيء من الداخل في هذا البيت في هذا الزمان بإنسان بالضرورة إذا فرضنا أن الداخل في ذلك الزمان غير الإنسان لا غير، ولا يصدق في عكسه لا شيء من الإنسان بداخل في ذلك البيت في ذلك الزمان بالضرورة؛ إذ هو في حيز المنع لما كان الأصل صادقًا. ولا يمتنع أ ع أن يكون الشيء ضروريًا بحسب الغير وهو ممكن بحسب ذاته.
Section: On what pertains to negatives. Know that the universal necessary negative converts as itself and in modality according to the majority, whether it is essential or external, as previously mentioned. Because the meaning of our saying 'Nothing of J is B necessarily' is that J and B are impossible to coexist by their essences. And since the coexistence of J with B is impossible, the coexistence of B with J is also impossible, because incompatibility between two things is not confined to one side. But according to some early scholars, it does not convert as itself in modality, but it converts to a universal perpetual negative, because the universal necessary negative implies the universal perpetual negative, and the universal perpetual negative converts as itself as previously mentioned; and it is necessary that the implicant of the implicand of a thing is itself an implicant of that thing. And some said: if it is essential, it converts as itself as the majority held; but if it is external, it does not convert as itself because, as previously mentioned in the affirmative of possibles, it is not impossible for each of two mutually exclusive types to be qualified by a quality, and that quality belongs to one actually and not to the other. The truth among these opinions is the first, because there is no proper proof for the second or third; as for the second, it depends on the first, and as for the third, it is forced. So we say: our statement 'Nothing that enters this house at this time is a human necessarily' is true if we assume that the one who enters at that time is nothing but a non-human, but its converse 'No human enters this house at that time necessarily' is not true, because it is in the realm of impossibility since the original was true. And it is not impossible that a thing is necessary with respect to something else while being possible in itself.
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This section deals with the conversion of necessary universal negatives. The author begins by stating the majority view: a necessary universal negative converts to itself in both form and modality, regardless of whether the proposition is essential (ḥaqīqiyya) or external (khārijiyya). The reason is that the meaning of 'No J is B necessarily' is that J and B are essentially incompatible, and incompatibility is symmetric. Then the author presents two alternative views: (1) Some early scholars say it converts to a perpetual (dāʾima) negative, not a necessary one, because the necessary implies the perpetual, and the perpetual converts to itself, so the necessary indirectly converts to a perpetual. (2) Others distinguish between essential and external: essential converts as itself, external does not. The author judges that the first view (majority) is correct, because the other views lack proper evidence. The second view depends on the first, and the third is forced. The author provides an example: 'Nothing that enters this house at this time is a human necessarily' is true if only non-humans enter, but its converse 'No human enters this house at that time necessarily' is false, because a human could have entered. The example shows that the converse is not necessary, so the original fails to convert necessarily. However, the author notes that a thing can be necessary relative to something else while being possible in itself, hinting at a modal distinction.

Key Terms
سالبة كلية ضرورية
دائمة
منافاة

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because the converse is always false.

Key Takeaway
The conversion of necessary universal negatives to themselves is valid when the necessity is essential, but the passage also discusses alternative views and an example showing exceptions for external necessity.

Conversion of Universal Necessary Negatives
Author discusses conversion of universal necessary negatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct conversion rule for universal necessary negatives and distinguish it from two alternative views.

Core Idea
A universal necessary negative converts as itself because mutual opposition is symmetric.

In this passage, the author addresses a specific point of controversy: whether a universal necessary negative proposition retains its necessity when converted. The majority holds that it does, but two alternative views have been proposed. By analyzing the arguments, you will learn to apply the correct conversion rule and avoid common fallacies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Recognize that the passage deals with universal necessary negatives: propositions of the form 'No S is P necessarily' (e.g., 'No intoxicant is permissible permanently').
2
Recall the definition of conversion
Conversion (in'ikas) swaps subject and predicate. For universal negatives, the standard non-modal rule is that they convert simply: 'No S is P' becomes 'No P is S'. Here the question is whether the modality (necessity) is also transferred.
3
Examine the majority argument for symmetric necessity
The author states that the impossibility of J and B coexisting in essence is mutual: if J cannot be B, then B cannot be J. Therefore the converse is also a necessary negative. This relies on the symmetry of mutual opposition.
The argument assumes necessity is grounded in the essences of the terms.
4
Contrast with the two alternative views
First alternative: some say the necessary negative converts only to a perpetual negative (not necessary), because necessary entails perpetual. Second alternative: some say conversion depends on whether the proposition is haqiqiyya (veridical) or kharijiyya (external); only haqiqiyya preserves necessity.
5
Evaluate the author's conclusion
The author rejects both alternatives as lacking proof or being far-fetched. He confirms the majority view with a concrete example: 'Nothing that enters this house at this time is a human being necessarily' is true, but the converse 'No human being is an entrant at this time necessarily' is false. The necessity of the converse is preserved only if the original denies possibility in essence, not merely in actuality.
The example shows that a proposition can be necessary relative to one context but not another; the correct conversion rule requires the stronger, essence-based necessity.

فصل: فيما يتعلق بالسوالب ] واعلم أن السالبة الكلية الضرورية تنعكس كنفسها كما وجهةً عند الجمهور منهم «حقيقية» كانت أو خارجية» لما مر من قبل. ولأن المعنى من قولنا لا شيء من ج) (ب) بالضرورة أن الجيم والباء يستحيل اجتماعهما لذاتهما. ولما استحال اجتماع الجيم مع الباء استحال اجتماع الباء مع الجيم لما أن المنافاة بين الشيئين لا تكون مختصة بأحد الجانبين. وعند قوم من المتقدمين أنها لا تنعكس كنفسها في الجهة لكنها تنعكس سالبة كلية دائمة لاستلزام السالبة الكلية الضرورية السالبة الكلية الدائمة والسالبة الكلية الدائمة تنعكس كنفسها لما مر ؛ وقد كان من الواجب أن يكون المستلزم للمستلزم للشيء مستلزما لذلك الشيء ومنهم من قال: إنها إذا كانت حقيقية تنعكس كنفسها كما ذهب إليه الجمهور. فأما إذا كانت خارجية فلا تنعكس كنفسها لما مر في الموجبة من الممكنات أنه لا يمتنع أن يكون كل واحد من النوعين المتباينين متصفّا بصفة وكانت تلك الصفة لأحدهما بالفعل دون الثاني. والحق من جملة هذه الأقوال هو الأول، فإنه لا دليل على الثاني والثالث كما ينبغي؛ أما على الثاني فلأنه يتوقف في الأول، وأما على الثالث فلأنه يتكلف فيه؛ فنقول يصدق قولنا لا شيء من الداخل في هذا البيت في هذا الزمان بإنسان بالضرورة إذا فرضنا أن الداخل في ذلك الزمان غير الإنسان لا غير، ولا يصدق في عكسه لا شيء من الإنسان بداخل في ذلك البيت في ذلك الزمان بالضرورة؛ إذ هو في حيز المنع لما كان الأصل صادقًا. ولا يمتنع أ ع أن يكون الشيء ضروريًا بحسب الغير وهو ممكن بحسب ذاته.
Chapter: Concerning negatives. Know that the universal necessary negative converts as itself, both in respect of modality and in respect of truth, according to the majority, whether it is a 'veridical' (haqiqiyya) or 'external' (kharijiyya) proposition, for the reason mentioned before. And because the meaning of our statement 'Nothing from J is B necessarily' is that J and B are impossible to come together in their essences. And when it is impossible for J to come together with B, it is impossible for B to come together with J, because the mutual opposition between two things is not confined to one side. But according to a group of the earlier scholars, it does not convert as itself in respect of modality, but rather it converts as a universal perpetual negative, because the universal necessary negative entails the universal perpetual negative, and the universal perpetual negative converts as itself, as previously mentioned. And it was necessary that whatever entails the entailer of a thing also entails that thing. And among them is one who said: If it is a veridical (haqiqiyya) proposition, it converts as itself, as the majority held. But if it is external (kharijjiyya), it does not convert as itself, for the reason mentioned in the case of affirmative possible propositions, that it is not impossible for each of two distinct types to be characterized by a property, and that property is actual for one of them and not the other. The correct among these opinions is the first, for there is no proof for the second and third as there should be. As for the second, it depends on the first, and as for the third, it is far-fetched. So we say: Our statement 'Nothing that enters this house at this time is a human being necessarily' is true if we assume that the one who enters at that time is other than a human being, and nothing else. But its converse, 'No human being is an entrant into this house at that time necessarily' is not true, because it is in the realm of impossibility since the original was true. And it is not impossible for something to be necessary with respect to something else while being possible in itself.
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This passage deals with the conversion of universal necessary negative propositions. The author begins by stating the majority view: a universal necessary negative converts as itself, meaning the converse is also a universal necessary negative, both in truth and modality. The reason given is that the impossibility of coexistence between two terms is mutual—if J cannot coexist with B, then B cannot coexist with J. The author then presents two alternative views. One group of earlier scholars held that the necessary negative converts not as a necessary but as a perpetual negative, because the necessary entails the perpetual, and the perpetual negative converts as itself. The author critiques this by noting that if A entails B, and B converts to C, it does not automatically follow that A converts to C; they overlook the need for a chain of entailment. Another group held that the conversion depends on whether the proposition is veridical (judging about actual existents in all times) or external (judging about actual existents at a specific time). For veridical, it converts; for external, it does not. The author rejects both alternatives, stating the first view (majority) is correct. He provides an example: 'Nothing that enters this house at this time is a human being necessarily' is true if the entrant is indeed non-human. But its converse 'No human being is an entrant at this time necessarily' is false, because it could be that a human could have entered but did not. The author concludes that a thing can be necessary with respect to another thing while being possible in itself.

A universal necessary negative converts as itself because the impossibility of coexistence between terms is symmetric, and this holds regardless of whether the proposition is haqiqiyya or kharijiyya.
