Conversion of Universal Perpetual Negatives: Majority View and al-Razi's Refinement
Discussion of conversion of perpetual negatives and objections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the conversion of universal perpetual negatives and evaluate the objections raised against al-Razi's position.

Core Idea
A perpetual negative converts as itself, but al-Razi shows it fails when the subject is veridical.

We have learned about immediate inference. This passage deepens that by examining a specific case: the conversion of universal perpetual negatives. The majority holds it converts as itself, but Fakhr al-Din al-Razi challenges this with a subtle condition. We will see his counterexample and then explore the objections raised against him and their refutations. This will sharpen your ability to handle modal conversion in logical debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Recognize that a universal perpetual negative states that no S is P at any time. Example: 'No human is a writer perpetually' means at no time is a human a writer.
This is a modal proposition with temporal universality.
2
Apply the majority conversion rule
According to the majority, a universal perpetual negative converts as itself: 'No S is P perpetually' yields 'No P is S perpetually'. For external subjects, this holds.
External subjects are those that actually exist.
3
Understand al-Razi's condition
Al-Razi argues that if the subject is veridical (haqiqiyya) — including all possible instances — the conversion fails. The predicate may not be necessary for the subject.
Haqiqiyya subject includes non-existent but possible instances.
4
Test with al-Razi's example
Take 'No human is a writer perpetually'. This is true because writing is not essential to humans. Its converse 'No writer is a human perpetually' is false, since many writers are human. So the converse does not hold.
This demonstrates the failure of conversion as itself.
5
Examine the first objection
Critics argue: if the original is true, either the converse is true or false. If false, its contradictory 'Some writer is human' is true. Combining with the original yields 'Some writer is not a writer', a contradiction. Hence the original must be false.
This objection aims to show al-Razi's assumption leads to absurdity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السالبة الدائمة الكلية فإنها تنعكس كنفسها عند الجمهور كما إذا كانت خارجية، وعند الإمام الكامل الفاضل فخر الدين الرازي لا تنعكس كنفسها إذا كانت حقيقية الموضوع لما أن الكتابة غير ضرورية للإنسان في شيء من الأوقات، وحينئذ يمكن أن يكون سلبها عنه في جميع الأوقات (۱). فلو صدق عكسها كنفسها لصدق لا شيء من الكاتب بإنسان دائما، وذلك محال ; ولأنها إذا انعكست كنفسها انعكست الموجبة الضرورية مطلقة عامة لتمام البرهان، وليس كذلك لما مر . ثم من الناس من قدح دعواه بناء / على ما يدل على انتفاء صدق الأصل [١٣٤ و] دون صدق العكس كما يقال إن الأصل لا يكون صادقًا، فإنه إذا صدق. فإما أن يصدق عليه أو لا يصدق وأيهما لزم يلزم انتفاء المجموع: أما إذا لزم الأول فظاهر، وأما إذا لزم الثاني فكذلك فإنه يصدق نقيضه وهو قولنا بعض الكاتب إنسان بالإطلاق العام. وأنه مع الأصل ينتج قولنا بعض الكاتب ليس بكاتب؛ وهو محال ولأنه إذا صدق فإما أن يثبت الكتابة لشيء من الذوات في شيء من الأوقات؛ وحينئذ يلزم أن تكون الكتابة لغير الإنسان مع أنها لا تكون للإنسان، أو لا يثبت؛ وحينئذ يلزم أن يصدق قولنا لا شيء من الكاتب بإنسان دائما ضرورة عدم الموضوع في جميع الأوقات. غير أنه إذا لم يكن سلب الشيء عن الشيء دائما مع أنه لا يمكن سلب ذلك الآخر عنه فلا يظهر القدح بمثل هذه الدلائل في القدح فيها أظهر من القدح في ذلك يعرف بالتأمل. ومنهم من قدح في دعواه بناء على القدح في الدليل الذي مرَّ ذكره وهو أن الكتابة إذا لم تكن ضرورية الإنسان في وقت ما يلزم أن لا تكون ضرورية له أصلا فإنه يمكن تكون ضروية في وقت من الأوقات بحسب الغير. غير أنه فاسد فإنه لا يلزم من فساد الدليل فساد المدلول على الخصوص إذا كانت الدلائل متعددة.
As for the universal perpetual negative, it converts as itself according to the majority, for example when it is external. But according to the perfect and virtuous Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, it does not convert as itself if its subject is veridical (haqiqiyya), because writing is not necessary for a human being at any time, and then its negation from him in all times is possible. So if its converse as itself were true, then 'No writer is a human being perpetually' would be true, which is absurd. And because if it converts as itself, then the universal necessary affirmative would convert as a general absolute, for the completion of the demonstration, but that is not the case, as previously mentioned. Then among people are those who attacked his claim based on what indicates the falsehood of the original without the falsehood of the converse, as it is said that the original is not true. For if it were true, then either its converse is true or not, and whichever follows, the sum is negated. If the first follows, it is obvious; if the second follows, then its contradictory is true, namely 'Some writer is a human being in the general absolute sense'. And that, together with the original, yields 'Some writer is not a writer', which is absurd. And because if it is true, then either writing is affirmed for some essence at some time—then writing would be for a non-human, while it is not for a human—or it is not affirmed, then 'No writer is a human being perpetually' would be true due to the non-existence of the subject in all times. However, if the negation of a thing from a thing is not perpetual while the negation of that other from it is impossible, then the attack with such arguments is not evident, and the attack on them is more evident than the attack on that, as known by reflection. And among them are those who attacked his claim based on the attack on the previously mentioned argument, namely that if writing is not necessary for a human being at some time, then it follows that it is not necessary for him at all, but it is possible for it to be necessary at some time with respect to something else. However, this is invalid, for the invalidity of the proof does not necessitate the invalidity of the conclusion, especially when there are multiple proofs.
Source pages p. 469

Commentary
This passage addresses the conversion of universal perpetual negative propositions. The author first states the majority view: a universal perpetual negative converts as itself (i.e., 'No S is P perpetually' yields 'No P is S perpetually'), citing the external case as an example. He then presents Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's dissenting view: if the subject is veridical (i.e., includes all possible instances), the converse does not hold. Al-Razi's example: 'No human is a writer perpetually' is true because writing is not essential to humans, but its converse 'No writer is a human perpetually' is false because some writers are humans. The author then discusses objections raised against al-Razi's position. Some critics argue that if the original is true, its converse must be false or true; if true, all is well; if false, its contradictory ('Some writer is human') is true, which combined with the original yields absurdities like 'Some writer is not a writer'. Others object based on the notion of necessity, claiming that if writing is not necessary for a human at some time, it is not necessary at all—but this is refuted by noting that necessity can be relative. The author ultimately defends the majority view, noting that attacking the proof does not invalidate the conclusion if multiple proofs exist.

حقيقية الموضوع
الإطلاق العام

B) It does not convert at all.
C) It does not convert as itself; the converse may be false even if the original is true.
D) It converts only if the predicate is also perpetual.

Key Takeaway
The conversion of universal perpetual negatives is not absolute; the veridicality of the subject can block the conversion, and objections based on absurd consequences can be refuted by careful analysis.

Parsing Conversion Rules for General, Particular, and Actual Propositions
Author enumerates conversion rules for general, particular, and actual propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conversion behaviour of each proposition type (general conditional, particular conditional, actual) and distinguish the majority view from the minority view.

Core Idea
Conversion is not uniform; proposition type determines if and how it converts.

This passage unravels the confusion surrounding conversion (al-‘aks) by categorising propositions. The author explicitly lists rules for general conditional, general conventional, particular conditional, particular conventional, and actual propositions. Understanding these rules is essential for correctly transforming premises in logical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition types listed
Read the Arabic enumeration: [3] general conditional (al-mashruta al-‘amma), [4] general conventional (al-‘urfiyya al-‘amma), [5] particular conditional, [6] particular conventional, [7] actuals (alfi‘liyyat) including temporal, existential, absolute general.
Note the article markers [٣] etc. indicate enumeration.
2
Extract the conversion rule for each type
For each type, note whether it converts identically, to its general form, or not at all. The general conditional converts as itself; general conventional likewise. Particular conditional and particular conventional convert to their general form according to the majority. Actuals do not convert according to the majority, but some earlier scholars hold they convert to a perpetual particular negative.
Look for key phrases: 'تنعكس كنفسها', 'تنعكسان إلى عامتهما', 'لا تنعكس أصلا'.
3
Differentiate the majority vs minority positions
The text marks two disputes: (a) for particulars, the majority says always convert to general, while a minority says always in some cases but not always in others; author says the minority is correct. (b) for actuals, majority says no conversion, minority says convert to perpetual particular negative; author does not explicitly side here.
Pay attention to phrases like 'عند الجمهور' and 'وعند قوم'.
4
Relate the rules to the underlying logic
The impossibility of conversion for temporal propositions is a key reason given for actuals not converting. The absolute general is more general than everything, hence conversion to a particular negative is possible for some. This shows how specific features (like temporality) affect conversion.
This connects to the broader theory of immediate inference in Islamic logic.

وبالجملة فالكلام في انعكاس هذه القضية كلام مشوّش غير مضبوط. ولو كان كذلك لكان تحرير المبحث من اللوازم بأن الموضوع إذا كانت السالبة حقيقية الموضوع ما هو، وكيف هو ؟ وكذلك المحمول وهو الكاتب، فإن الكاتب بحسب اللغة وهو شيء به الكتابة لا يلزم أن يكون إنسانًا كما أن الإنسان من حيث هو الإنسان لا يلزم أن يكون كاتبًا.
[٣] وأما المشروطة العامة فإنها تنعكس كنفسها كما إذا كانت خارجية لما مر ، [٤] وكذلك العرفية العامة. [٥] وأما المشروطة الخاصة، [٦] والعرفية [ الخاصة ] فإنهما تنعكسان إلى عامتهما عند الجمهور في جميع الأحوال. وعند قوم أنهما تنعكسان دائما في البعض لا دائمًا في البعض كما مرَّ ؛ وهذا هو الأصح لما مر من قبل.

In summary, the discussion on the conversion of this proposition is confused and unregulated. If it were otherwise, the investigation would have to consider the necessities: if the subject is a true negative, what is the subject and how is it? Likewise the predicate, which is 'writer', for the writer according to language is a thing with writing, and it is not necessary that it be a human, just as a human qua human is not necessarily a writer. [3] As for the general conditional, it converts as itself, like when it is extrinsic as previously mentioned. [4] And likewise the general conventional. [5] As for the particular conditional, [6] and the particular conventional, they both convert to their general form according to the majority in all cases. According to some, they always convert in some cases but not always in others, as previously mentioned; and this is the correct view based on what preceded. [7] As for the remaining propositions, if they are among actuals such as the temporal, the existential, the absolute general, and others, they do not convert at all according to the majority, due to the impossibility of conversion in the more specific among them, namely the temporal, as previously mentioned. According to some of the earlier scholars, they convert to a perpetual particular negative, because the absolute general is more general than everything.
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage defines the conversion rules for various types of propositions. The author begins by criticizing the confused state of prior discussions on this topic. Then, in an enumerated list, he specifies how different propositional forms behave under conversion. [3] The general conditional (al-mashruta al-‘amma) converts identically, like an extrinsic proposition. [4] Similarly, the general conventional (al-‘urfiyya al-‘amma) also converts as itself. [5]-[6] The particular conditional and particular conventional both convert to their general form according to the majority (al-jumhur); a minority holds that they convert always in some cases but not always in others, and the author considers this minority view correct. [7] The remaining types, which are actual (fi‘liyya) propositions — like temporal (waqtitiyya), existential (wujudiyya), absolute general (mutlaqa ‘amma) — do not convert at all in the majority view, because the most specific among them (the temporal) cannot convert. However, some earlier scholars (qawm min al-mutaqaddimin) hold that they do convert to a perpetual particular negative, arguing that the absolute general is more general than everything. This sets up the need for a proof, which follows in the next lesson.

Key Takeaway
The conversion rule for a proposition depends on its type; general conditionals convert identically, particular conditionals convert to their general form, and actual propositions generally do not convert.

Conversion of General Possibility to Perpetual Particular Negative
Proof that general possibility converts to perpetual particular negative

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert a general possibility proposition into its perpetual particular negative converse and demonstrate the proof using the third figure.

Core Idea
General possibility converts to a perpetual particular negative, not a universal.

In this lesson, we examine a key rule in modal logic: the conversion of general possibility propositions. The author shows that a general possibility negative converts to a perpetual particular negative, a result that relies on the third figure of the syllogism. We will walk through the proof step by step, clarifying the logical machinery involved.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conversion claim
The author states that if 'No J is B' is true with general possibility (al-mumkina al-amma), then it converts to 'Some B is not J perpetually' (a perpetual particular negative). What is being claimed?
General possibility is the widest sense of possibility, not restricted by time or condition.
2
Extract the two premises used in the proof
The proof uses two premises: (1) 'Everything that is B necessarily is B with general possibility' (a logical truth) and (2) 'No B necessarily is J perpetually' (derived from the original conversion). Identify these in the text.
Premise (2) comes from a prior rule about conversion of necessary propositions.
3
Apply the third figure
The two premises share the middle term 'B' (subject in both). In the third figure, the middle term is subject in both premises. The conclusion follows from these two premises via a third-figure syllogism: Some B is not J perpetually.
The third figure yields a particular conclusion.
4
Verify the converse matches
The conclusion 'Some B is not J perpetually' is exactly the required converse of 'No J is B with general possibility'. Check that subject and predicate are swapped and the quantity changed from universal to particular.
Conversion of a universal negative (general possibility) yields a particular negative (perpetual).

وأما إذا كانت الممكنات فكذلك؛ إذ الممكنة العامة أعم من الكل وإنها تنعكس إلى سالبة جزئية دائمة أيضًا؛ وذلك لأنه إذا صدق لا شيء من «ج ب» بالإمكان العام صدق مجموع قضيتين: أحدهما كل ما هو «ب» بالضرورة فهو (ب) بالإمكان العام، والثانية لا شيء من (ب) بالضرورة ب ج دائما لما مر من قبل. ويلزم من لزوم هاتين المقدمتين صدق المدعى من الثالث. وأما أنه يلزم من انعكاس المطلقة العامة والممكنة العامة انعكاس ما هو الأخص منهما فذلك هذا البرهان أيضًا وبما مرَّ من قبل، وهو أن لازم الأعم لازم الأخص. وأما المراتب في كون البعض منها أخص من البعض فقد مر ، ولو اقتصروا على بيان انعكاس الممكنة العامة فقد حصل لهم المرام من أن تعرضوا لبيان انعكاس المطلقة العامة لما أن الممكنة العامة أعم من الكل. والبحث الذي مرَّ ذكره في الفعليات فذلك لا يكون مختصا بها، فإنه يصدق أن يقال لا شيء من القمر بمنخسف بالإمكان الخاص ولا يصدق بعض المنخسف ليس بقمر دائما لما أن المنخسف لا يكون إلا وأن يكون قمرًا عند الجمهور. ثم لقائل أن يقول: هذه السوالب إذا كانت منعكسة إلى سالبة جزئية كانت منعكسة إلى سالبة جزئية ضرورية بعين ما ذكرتم من البرهان، فإنه إذا صدق لا شيء من «ج ب» بالإمكان العام صدق لا شيء من «ب» بالضرورة «ب ج» بالضرورة وإلا يصدق نقيضه وإنه مع الأصل ينتج قولنا بعض «ب» بالضرورة ليس هو «ب» بالإمكان العام وذلك [محال]. وهذه القضية مع قولنا كل ما هو «ب» بالضرورة فهو «ب» بالإطلاق العام ينتج بعض «ب» ليس «ج» بالضرورة [١٣٥ و] من الثالث وإن قيل إنما يكون كذلك إذا لم / تكن الصغرى الممكنة متقاعدة في الشكل؛ إنها لا تكون متقاعدة تعرف من بعد.
And as for when the [propositions] are possibility [propositions], likewise; for the general possibility is more general than all, and it converts to a perpetual particular negative as well; that is because if 'No J is B' is true with general possibility, then two propositions together are true: one is 'Everything that is B necessarily is B with general possibility', and the second is 'No B necessarily is B J perpetually' as previously mentioned. And from the necessity of these two premises the truth of the claim follows from the third [figure]. And as for that from the conversion of the general absolute and the general possibility follows the conversion of what is more specific than them, that is this proof as well and by what preceded, which is that the necessary concomitant of the more general is necessary concomitant of the more specific. And as for the degrees in some being more specific than others, it has passed. And if they confined themselves to explaining the conversion of the general possibility, they would have attained their aim without addressing the conversion of the general absolute, because the general possibility is more general than all. And the investigation that was mentioned regarding actual propositions is not specific to them; for it is true to say 'No moon is eclipsed with special possibility' and it is not true that 'Some eclipsed is not a moon perpetually', because the eclipsed is only and is a moon according to the majority. Then someone may say: These negatives, if they are converted to particular negatives, are converted to necessary particular negatives by the same proof you mentioned; for if 'No J is B' is true with general possibility, then 'No B necessarily is B J necessarily' is true, otherwise its contradictory would be true and it together with the original produces 'Some B necessarily is not B with general possibility' and that is impossible. And this proposition together with our statement 'Everything that is B necessarily is B with absolute generality' yields 'Some B is not J necessarily' from the third [figure], and if it is said that it is only so when the minor possibility is not irregular in the figure; that it is not irregular will be known later.
Source pages p. 471

Commentary
This passage deals with the conversion of propositions of possibility, specifically the general possibility (al-mumkina al-amma). The author begins by stating that the general possibility is the most universal type, and it converts to a perpetual particular negative. The proof is given by combining two premises: first, everything that is B necessarily is B with general possibility (which is an identity), and second, no B necessarily is J perpetually. From these two, using the third figure (since the middle term B appears in both premises), we derive that some B is not J perpetually, which is the converse. The author then notes that this proof also shows the conversion of more specific types, since the necessary concomitant of the more general is also necessary for the more specific. He then addresses an objection: if the negative general possibility propositions convert to necessary particular negatives, then via another proof using necessity and absolute generality, we again get a necessary particular negative. The author concludes by noting a condition about the minor premise's regularity, which will be explained later.

Objection: Does Conversion Yield Necessary Particular Negatives?
Objection and response regarding necessary particular conversion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct and evaluate the objection that conversion of general possibility yields necessary particulars, and identify the condition regarding the minor premise.

Core Idea
An objector claims conversion yields necessary particulars, but the author notes a condition about the minor premise's regularity.

After presenting the main conversion rule, the author considers an objection: if general possibility negatives convert to perpetual particular negatives, then by a similar argument they might convert to necessary particular negatives. The author responds by noting a condition about the minor premise's role in the third figure, which will be clarified later. This lesson unpacks that objection and the author's counterpoint.

Guided Path
1
State the objection
The objector argues: If 'No J is B with general possibility' converts to a perpetual particular negative, then by the same proof, it should also convert to a necessary particular negative. Find this reasoning in the text.
The objector uses a parallel syllogism involving necessity and absolute generality.
2
Reconstruct the objector's syllogism
The objector says: if the original is true, then 'No B necessarily is J necessarily' (by some prior rule). Then combining with 'Everything that is B necessarily is B with absolute generality', we get 'Some B is not J necessarily' from the third figure. Identify the premises and conclusion.
This is a new syllogism, not the original conversion proof.
3
Identify the author's response
The author counters that this conclusion follows only if the minor premise (the one involving absolute generality) is 'not irregular' (mut qa'ida) in the third figure. What does 'irregular' mean here? It refers to the figure's conditions.
The author postpones the explanation of regularity to a later section.
4
Evaluate the logical force of the objection
The objection suggests that conversion yields necessary particulars, which would be a stronger claim. The author's condition effectively blocks the objection unless certain conditions are met, preserving the original conversion rule as given.
This shows the precision required in modal logic.

Do Particular Negatives Convert? The Rule and Its Exception
Author explains that particular negative propositions do not convert, with an exception for special conditional/conventional types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a particular negative proposition can and cannot convert, and evaluate the exception for specially conditioned/conventional types using the author's criteria.

Core Idea
Particular negatives never convert in themselves, even if a special condition makes them appear to.

In categorical logic, conversion is a powerful tool, but not all propositions play by the same rules. The particular negative ('Some S is not P') is especially stubborn: the majority of logicians hold that it never converts. However, some scholars allow conversion under a special temporal condition. In this passage, the author explains that exception and then shows why it does not hold universally, using a vivid example from biology.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the majority rule
The author begins by stating the majority view: the particular negative does not convert at all, whether it is a real (haqiqiyya) or external (kharijiyya) proposition. Note that 'real' means the subject exists in reality, while 'external' means the subject is considered externally.
2
2. Understand the exception and its condition
Some logicians claim conversion is possible if the proposition is 'specially conditioned' (mashruta khassa) or 'specially conventional' (urfiya khassa). This means the negation holds only as long as the subject exists. For example, 'Some J is not B' is true not perpetually but as long as J exists. The claim is that it converts to 'Some B is not J' as long as B exists, because the two terms must meet in one essence.
This condition restricts the truth of the proposition to the duration of the subject's existence.
3
3. Analyze the author's counterexample
The author tests the exception with the proposition 'Some animal is not human, as long as it is animal' (a specially conventional type). He argues it cannot convert to 'Some human is not animal' because it is impossible for any human to not be an animal. The negation of 'animal' from 'human' is absurd. This shows that even with the temporal condition, conversion fails.
Even though the original proposition is true (there are animals that are not human), the converse is false.
4
4. Grasp the author's conclusion
The author concludes that if a particular negative does not convert in one form, it does not convert in itself. Any apparent conversion is due to a special condition, not the nature of the proposition. Therefore, the particular negative as such does not convert. The debate is about the proposition in its essence, not about accidental features.
The author reinforces the majority view while acknowledging the logical nuance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السالبة الجزئية فإنها لا تنعكس أصلا عند الجمهور حقيقية كانت أو خارجية كما مر . وعند قوم إنها تنعكس إذا كانت مشروطة خاصة أو عرفية خاصة فإنه إذا صدق قولنا بعض (ج) ليس «ب» لا دائما بل ما دام ج) صدق قولنا بعض «ب» ليس «ج» لا دائما بل ما دام «ب» لأنه لا بد من اجتماع الوصفين أعني الجيم والباء في ذاتٍ واحدة حينئذ ضرورة لا دوام سلب الباء لبعض أفراد الجيم وحصول المنافاة بينهما في تلك الذات؛ وذلك مستلزم لصدق المدعى في هذه الصورة. غير أنه في حيز المنع لما أنها لا تكون منعكسة في سائر الصور كما في قولنا بعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان لا دائما بل ما دام حيوانا، فإنها عرفية خاصة. ولا يمكن أن تكون منعكسة لامتناع سلب الحيوان عن الإنسان. ولا يظن أن سلب الدوام يخرجه عن كونه صادقًا فإن الحيوان الذي ليس بإنسان يمكن أن لا يبقى حيوانا ثمَّ يصير إنسانًا. ألا ترى أن النطفة كانت دما، والدم كان غذاء، والغذاء كان لحما، واللحم كان دجاجا أو غير ذلك؟ ولأنها إذا لم تكن منعكسة في صورة ما فلا تكون منعكسة من حيث هي هي بل إذا كانت منعكسة فكانت منعكسة من حيث كذا ولا كلام في ذلك المجموع بل الكلام في السالبة الجزئية من حيث هي سالبة جزئية.
And as for the particular negative, it does not convert at all according to the majority, whether it is real or external as previously mentioned. And according to some, it converts if it is specially conditioned or specially conventional: for if our statement 'Some J is not B' is true, not perpetually but as long as J [exists], then our statement 'Some B is not J' is true, not perpetually but as long as B [exists], because it is necessary for the two attributes, namely J and B, to meet in one essence at that time, necessarily; due to the non-permanence of the negation of B from some individuals of J and the occurrence of mutual exclusion between them in that essence; and that entails the truth of the claim in this form. However, it is subject to objection because it is not converted in other forms, as in our statement 'Some animal is not human', not perpetually but as long as it is an animal, for it is specially conventional. And it cannot be converted because of the impossibility of negating 'animal' from 'human'. And do not think that the negation of perpetuity removes it from being true, for the animal that is not human can cease to be an animal and then become human. Do you not see that the sperm was blood, the blood was food, the food was flesh, and the flesh was chicken or something else? And because if it is not converted in one form, then it is not converted in itself; rather, if it is converted, it is converted insofar as it is such and such, and there is no argument about that totality; rather, the argument is about the particular negative insofar as it is a particular negative.
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage addresses the conversion of particular negative propositions. The author states that, according to the majority (jumhur), a particular negative does not convert at all, whether it is a real or external proposition. However, some logicians hold that it does convert if it is specially conditioned (mashruta khassa) or specially conventional (urfiya khassa). The condition is that the negation holds only as long as the subject exists. For example, 'Some animal is not human' is true as long as it is animal (since there are animals that are not human, e.g., a horse), but if that animal changes and becomes human, the proposition may become false. The author then provides a counterexample: 'Some animal is not human' cannot convert to 'Some human is not animal' because it is impossible for a human to not be an animal. He then explains that even though the negation is not perpetual, it is still true because the animal could transform into a human (like sperm to chicken). Therefore, the particular negative does not convert universally; if it does convert, it is due to a specific condition, not in itself. The conclusion is that the particular negative, considered merely as a particular negative, does not convert.

Key Terms
السالبة الجزئية (al-saliba al-juz'iyya): A proposition that denies something of some individuals of the subject (e.g., 'Some J is not B'). [Note: Stable term across Islamic logic.]
مشروطة خاصة (mashruta khassa): A conditional proposition with a special condition, here meaning that the negation holds only as long as the subject exists. [Note: This appears to be a technical term in the theory of conditional propositions; may vary among logicians.]
عرفية خاصة (urfiya khassa): A conventional proposition with a special condition, similar to 'mashruta khassa' but based on customary usage. [Note: Not a standard term in all works; seems to be used here as a type of conditional.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that 'Some animal is not human' does not convert to 'Some human is not animal'?
A) Because 'human' is not a species of 'animal'
B) Because it is impossible to negate 'animal' from 'human'
C) Because the original proposition is not perpetual
D) Because conversion only works for universal propositions

Key Takeaway
A particular negative proposition does not convert in itself; any apparent conversion is due to an accidental temporal condition, not the nature of the proposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Consider the proposition: 'Some intoxicants are not wine' (since nabidh is an intoxicant but not wine). Determine whether this proposition converts to 'Some wine is not an intoxicant'. Use the rule taught in this lesson to justify your answer.

Two Kinds of Truth and Their Logical Power in Conversion
Author defines two types of truth and argues both imply truth in the converse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between truth by reality and truth by assumption, and explain why both entail truth in the converse.

Core Idea
Truth by assumption is as logically potent as truth by reality for conversion.

In this passage, the author teaches that truth is not monolithic: a proposition can be true by actually matching reality, or it can be assumed true for the sake of argument. Understanding this distinction is essential for evaluating dialectical reasoning, where participants often grant premises they know are false. The author then proves a pivotal property: both kinds of truth guarantee that the converse proposition is also true in the same mode.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two kinds of truth
Locate where the author distinguishes الصدق بالحقيقة (truth by reality) and الصدق بالفرض (truth by assumption). Note that both can occur in affirmative or negative propositions.
The examples given are clear: 'Every human is an animal' is truth by reality; 'Every human is a horse' is only truth by assumption.
2
Parse the core claim
The author asserts that just as truth by reality implies truth in the converse, so does truth by assumption. This is a strong claim: it means that the mode of truth is preserved under conversion.
For example, if 'No human is a horse' is true by reality, then 'No horse is a human' is also true by reality. If 'No human is an animal' is assumed true, then 'No animal is a human' is assumed true.
3
Examine the proof strategy
The author uses a proof by contradiction. Assume the original is true but the converse is not true (in the same mode). Then the contradictory of the converse would be true, leading to an absurdity.
The proof is presented in two cases: either the original and its contradictory are both true (impossible self-negation) or the implication relation fails (absurd).
4
Consider the objection about impossibility
The author anticipates that assuming an impossible proposition (like 'No human is an animal') might seem to invalidate demonstrations. He clarifies that under the assumption, the impossible is treated as not impossible, so the proof still holds.
This is a key point for dialectical contexts: even false assumptions can be used in logical reasoning.
5
Reflect on the incomplete exception
The passage ends while discussing a possible impediment to the implication, specifically if the impediment covers all necessary and non-necessary universal negatives. This suggests the author acknowledges a nuance, but the argument is cut off.
The main lesson is that the implication generally holds; the exception is left for deeper study.

فصل : [في الصدق] ومن جملة ما يجب أن تعلم هو أن الصدق في الأصل قد يكون بالحقيقة، وقد يكون بالفرض؛ موجبة كانت القضية أو سالبة كما في قولنا كل إنسان حيوان، لا شيء من الإنسان بفرس، فإن الصدق فيهما بالحقيقة على خلاف قولنا كل إنسان فرس، لا شيء من الإنسان بحيوان، فإن الصدق فيهما لا يمكن أن يكون إلا بالفرض . وكما أن الصدق بالحقيقة مما يلزمه الصدق في العكس فكذلك الصدق بالفرض فإنه من جملة ما يلزمه الصدق في العكس كذلك. ولا يظن أن ذلك الفرض إذا كان فرض محال فلا يمكن إقامة البرهان عليه لما أن المحال على التقدير المحال غير محال؛ إذ المحال في إقامة البرهان لا يلزم من ذلك التقدير فقط بل يلزم من التقدير الآخر وإن كان ذلك التقدير من جملة ما له مدخل في التقدير الآخر وهو أن الصدق في الأصل إذا لم يكن مستلزما للصدق في العكس فيقال مثلا إذا صدق قولنا بالضرورة لا واحد من الإنسان بحيوان صدق قولنا بالضرورة لا واحد من الحيوان بإنسان كذلك وإلا لصدق نقیضه؛ وإنه يفضي إلى المحال بانعكاس النقيض تارةً وبانضمامه إلى الأصل أخرى كما مر . ولأن الصدق في الأصل مستلزم / للصدق في العكس، فإنه إذا لم يكن مستلزمًا فلا يخلو من أن يتحقق هذا المجموع وهو صدقه مع صدق النقيض في الجملة أو لا يتحقق ؛ فإن تحقق فظاهر فإنه يلزم منه انسلاب الشيء عن نفسه وهو إن الحيوان ليس بحيوان في هذه الصورة. وإن لم يتحقق فكذلك فإنه يلزم منه أن يكون المستلزم للشيء لا يكون مستلزما لذلك الشيء؛ وذلك محال . ولا يبعد أن يقال في مثل هذه الصورة إن الصدق في الأصل لا يكون مستلزما للصدق في العكس؛ إذ المانع عن الاستلزام لا يخلو من أن يكون شاملًا للضرورية واللاضرورية من السوالب الكلية أو لا يكون، فإن كان
Chapter on Truth. Among what must be known is that truth in the original proposition may be by reality or by assumption; whether the proposition is affirmative or negative, as in our statements 'Every human is an animal' and 'No human is a horse', the truth in both is by reality, contrary to our statements 'Every human is a horse' and 'No human is an animal', for truth in these can only be by assumption. And just as truth by reality entails truth in the converse, so too truth by assumption entails truth in the converse likewise. And do not think that if that assumption is an impossible assumption, then a demonstration cannot be built upon it, because the impossible under an impossible supposition is not impossible; indeed, the impossibility in building a demonstration does not follow from that supposition alone but from another supposition, even if that supposition is among what has input in the other supposition. And that is: if the truth in the original does not imply truth in the converse, then for example, if our statement 'No human is an animal' is true by necessity, then our statement 'No animal is a human' is true likewise; otherwise its contradictory would be true, and that leads to impossibility by conversion of the contradictory sometimes and by appending it to the original other times, as previously. And because truth in the original implies truth in the converse, if it does not imply, then either this composite is realized—namely its truth together with the truth of its contradictory in some respect—or not; if it is realized, then obviously it entails the thing stripping itself of itself, i.e., 'the animal is not an animal' in this form. And if it is not realized, likewise, then it entails that the implied of something is not implied by that thing; and that is impossible. And it is not improbable to say in such a form that truth in the original does not imply truth in the converse, for the impediment to implication is either universal covering necessary and non-necessary universal negatives or not; if it is...
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and argumentative text on the nature of truth and its relation to conversion. It opens by stating that truth in a proposition can be of two types: truth by reality (where the proposition corresponds to actual fact) and truth by assumption (where the proposition is taken as true for the sake of argument, even if false in reality). Examples are given: 'Every human is an animal' is true by reality, while 'Every human is a horse' can only be true by assumption. The author then asserts that both types of truth entail the truth of the converse proposition (i.e., swapping subject and predicate). He anticipates an objection: if the assumption is an impossible one (like 'No human is an animal'), how can a demonstration be built on it? He replies that under the assumption, the impossible becomes permissible; the impossibility of building a demonstration arises only when combining assumptions, not from the assumption alone. He then provides a rigorous argument: if truth in the original does not imply truth in the converse, then consider a necessary universal negative like 'No human is an animal'—its converse 'No animal is a human' must also be true; otherwise the contradictory would be true, leading to contradiction. He then presents a proof by cases: either the original is true together with its contradictory (which leads to self-negation like 'animal is not animal') or not (which leads to the absurdity of an implication failing). The text ends mid-sentence discussing a possible exception where the implication may not hold due to a universal impediment covering all negatives, but the argument is cut off.

Key Terms
الصدق بالحقيقة
الصدق بالفرض
العكس
المستلزم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is true about the relationship between truth in the original proposition and truth in its converse?
A) Only truth by reality entails truth in the converse.
B) Only truth by assumption entails truth in the converse.
C) Both truth by reality and truth by assumption entail truth in the converse.
D) Neither type of truth entails truth in the converse.

Key Takeaway
Truth, whether by reality or by assumption, guarantees the truth of the converse; this principle is essential for logical conversion in demonstrations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the proposition 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (true by reality in Islamic law). State its converse and explain whether it is true by reality or by assumption. (Hint: the converse of an A proposition is an I proposition.)
?
In a debate, a disputant assumes 'No animal is rational' (false in reality). Show that its converse 'No rational being is an animal' must be true by assumption. Then explain why this matters for the debate's logical structure.

Truth in Conversion is a Necessary Concomitant
Author argues that truth in converse is necessary concomitant of truth in original

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the converse of a necessary universal negative proposition and justify its necessity using the three-case framework.

Core Idea
Truth in the converse necessarily follows from truth in the original, in reality or on any presupposition.

The author defends the principle that when a proposition is true, its converse must also be true—otherwise a contradiction follows. This is not merely a dialectical claim but a demonstrative one. He explains three ways this concomitance holds: in reality, on a presupposition that itself requires it, or on a presupposition where an obstacle is absent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition
The passage uses the universal negative: 'No J is B'. Note its modality: necessary (ḍarūrī).
In Arabic: بالضرورة لا شيء من ج ب
2
State its converse
The converse swaps subject and predicate: 'No B is J'.
In Arabic: بالضرورة لا شيء من ب ج
3
Apply the three-case framework
Determine whether the necessity of the converse follows (a) in reality itself, (b) on a presupposition that makes the concomitance in reality, or (c) on a presupposition that does not necessitate in reality. The author argues that in all three cases the converse must be true on pain of impossibility.
4
Test with a reductio
Assume the converse is false; then its contradictory is true. Show that this leads to an impossibility given the truth of the original. This proves the necessity of the converse.
The reductio is a demonstrative proof, not dialectical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شاملًا فلا مجال للاستلزام في الضرورة ، وإن لم يكن شاملا فلا مجال لعدم الاستلزام في اللا ضرورية. فإن من المحال أن يكون عدم الاستلزام من لوازم عدم الشمول، ولا يمكن أن يكون الشمول من لوازم الاستلزام فيها البتة. لكنا نقول ما ذكرتم يتوقف على إثبات الملازمة من العدمين؛ وفيه من الكلام بخلاف ما ذكرناه من قبل على كون الصدق في الأصل مما يستلزم الصدق في العكس فإنه لا يتوقف على إثبات شيء كان الكلام في ذلك الإثبات نحو الكلام في هذا. ولأن ما ذكرتم من البراهين الجدلية دون ما ذكرناه فإنه من البرهانية، والبرهانية فوق الجدلية لما نبينه من بعد.
ثم من جملة ما يكون من اللوازم معرفته في هذا المقام هو أن تعرف بأن الصدق في العكس من لوازم الصدق في الأصل؛ والشيء لا يكون لازما للشيء إلا وأن يكون لازما له في نفس الأمر ، أو على تقدير يلزم من كونه لازما على ذلك التقدير أن يكون لازما له في نفس الأمر ، أو على تقدير لا يلزم من كونه لازما على ذلك التقدير أن يكون لازما له في نفس الأمر. ولو كان كذلك لكان من اللوازم أن يكون إقامته البرهان على كونه لازماً لذلك الشيء إما في نفس الأمر، وإما على تقدير يلزم، وإما على تقدير لا يلزم كما مرَّ.
وبالجملة وكما أن الصدق في العكس من لوازم الصدق في الأصل في نفس الأمر فكذلك من اللوازم على جميع التقادير إما في نفس الأمر فكما يقال إذا صدق قولنا بالضرورة لا شيء من (ج) ب) صدق قولنا بالضرورة لا شيء من بج» وإلا لصدق نقيضه ؛ وذلك يفضي إلى المحال كما مر . وإما على التقدير الذي يلزم من كونه لازما على ذلك التقدير أن يكون لازما في نفس الأمر فكما يقال إذا صدق قولنا بالضرورة لا شيء من ج ب على تقدير أن لا يتحقق في نفس الأمر ما يحقق اللزوم في نفس الأمر صدق قولنا بالضرورة لا شيء من «بج» وإلا نقيضه على ذلك التقدير ؛ وذلك يفضي إلى المحال على ذلك التقدير. وإما على التقدير الذي لا يلزم وكما يقال إذا صدق قولنا بالضرورة لا شيء من ج ب على تقدير أن لا يتحقق المانع عن اللزوم في نفس الأمر صدق قولنا بالضرورة لا شيء من بج» وإلا لصدق نقيضه على ذلك التقدير ; وذلك يفضي إلى المحال على ذلك التقدير. ثم المحال وإن كان يلزم على ذلك التقدير فلا يلزم من ذلك التقدير بل يلزم منه ومن غيره كما مر؛ فما يلزم على التقدير إذن فقد يكون من لوازم التقدير، وقد لا يكون. فلو كان من اللوازم لكان لازما في نفس الأمر إذا كان ذلك التقدير لازما في نفس الأمر. وإن لم يكن من اللوازم فكذلك بالنسبة إلى ما يكون من لوازمه وهو التقدير مع الغير فيما نحن بصدده؛ وهذا ظاهر.
Comprehensive, so there is no room for entailment in necessity; and if it is not comprehensive, there is no room for non-entailment in the non-necessary. For it is impossible that non-entailment be a concomitant of non-comprehensiveness, and it cannot be that comprehensiveness be a concomitant of entailment therein at all. But we say: what you mentioned depends on proving the concomitance from the two negations; and therein is discourse contrary to what we mentioned earlier that truth in the original entails truth in the converse, for it does not depend on proving anything; the discourse regarding that proof is similar to the discourse regarding this. And because what you mentioned of dialectical proofs is inferior to what we mentioned, for it is demonstrative, and the demonstrative is superior to the dialectical, as we will explain later.
Then among the things whose knowledge is necessary in this context is that you know that truth in the converse is a concomitant of truth in the original; and a thing is not a concomitant of a thing unless it is a concomitant of it in reality, or on a presupposition such that from its being a concomitant on that presupposition it follows that it is a concomitant in reality, or on a presupposition such that from its being a concomitant on that presupposition it does not follow that it is a concomitant in reality. And if it were so, then it would be among the concomitants that establishing the proof that it is a concomitant of that thing is either in reality, or on a presupposition that necessitates, or on a presupposition that does not necessitate, as has passed.
In summary, and just as truth in the converse is among the concomitants of truth in the original in reality, so too it is among the concomitants on all presuppositions: either in reality, as it is said if the statement 'necessarily, no J is B' is true, then the statement 'necessarily, no B is J' is true; otherwise its contradictory would be true, and that leads to an impossibility as has passed. Or on the presupposition that necessitates its being a concomitant on that presupposition that it is a concomitant in reality, as it is said if the statement 'necessarily, no J is B' is true on the presupposition that what realizes the concomitance in reality does not obtain in reality, then the statement 'necessarily, no B is J' is true; otherwise its contradictory on that presupposition; and that leads to an impossibility on that presupposition. Or on the presupposition that does not necessitate, as it is said if the statement 'necessarily, no J is B' is true on the presupposition that the obstacle to the concomitance does not obtain in reality, then the statement 'necessarily, no B is J' is true; otherwise its contradictory would be true on that presupposition; and that leads to an impossibility on that presupposition. Then the impossibility, even if it is entailed on that presupposition, is not entailed from that presupposition alone, but from it and from something else, as has passed; so what is entailed on the presupposition may be among the concomitants of the presupposition, and may not be. So if it is among the concomitants, it would be a concomitant in reality if that presupposition is a concomitant in reality. And if it is not among the concomitants, then similarly with respect to what is among its concomitants, namely the presupposition along with something else in what we are discussing; and this is evident.
Source pages p. 474

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement responding to an objection regarding logical conversion (al-'aks). The author first distinguishes between comprehensive and non-comprehensive rules of entailment, then contrasts his own demonstrative (burhani) proof with the opponent's dialectical (jadali) proof. He then establishes that truth in the converse is a necessary concomitant (lazim) of truth in the original, and this relationship can be considered in three ways: (1) necessary in reality (fi nafs al-amr), (2) necessary on a presupposition that itself necessitates the concomitance in reality, and (3) necessary on a presupposition that does not necessitate it in reality. He illustrates each case with the universal negative proposition 'No J is B' and its converse 'No B is J', showing that if the original is necessarily true, the converse must also be necessarily true on pain of contradiction (reductio). The author emphasizes that his proof is demonstrative, thus superior to dialectical arguments. The overall aim is to defend the validity of immediate inference through conversion.

Key Terms
العكس
اللازم
البرهان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if the original proposition 'No intoxicant (khamr) is halal' is necessarily true, what is the status of its converse 'No halal is an intoxicant'?
A) It is necessarily false.
B) It is necessarily true.
C) It is true only if an obstacle to conversion is removed.
D) It is true on a presupposition that necessitated it.

Key Takeaway
Truth in the converse is a necessary concomitant of truth in the original, and the proof is demonstrative, not merely dialectical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the proposition 'Every fajr prayer is two rak'ahs' (صلاة الفجر ركعتان). Is its converse 'Everything that is two rak'ahs is fajr prayer' necessarily true? Apply the three-case framework from this lesson to determine whether it follows in reality or on a presupposition.
?
Construct a necessary universal negative proposition from fiqh (e.g., 'No riba transaction is permissible') and derive its converse. Explain using the author's reductio why the converse must also be necessary.

Demonstrative vs Dialectical Proof
Author distinguishes demonstrative from dialectical proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish demonstrative (burhani) proof from dialectical (jadali) proof based on the author's criteria.

Core Idea
Demonstrative proof is superior because it derives from necessary principles, not merely accepted premises.

The author contrasts his own demonstrative proof of conversion with the opponent's dialectical proof. He asserts that demonstrative proof yields certainty, while dialectical proof relies on premises that may be merely accepted. This distinction is crucial for evaluating the strength of arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of proof mentioned
The author uses 'burhani' (demonstrative) and 'jadali' (dialectical).
In Arabic: البرهانية والجدلية
2
Locate the author's claim about superiority
He says 'what you mentioned of dialectical proofs is inferior to what we mentioned, for it is demonstrative, and the demonstrative is superior to the dialectical, as we will explain later.'
3
Understand the basis of the distinction
The author implies that his proof does not depend on proving anything else; it is self-evident. The opponent's proof depends on proving a concomitance from two negations, which is less certain.
Demonstrative proof is based on necessary principles; dialectical proof is based on accepted opinions.
4
Apply the distinction to an Islamic argument
For instance, in kalam, some proofs for God's existence are burhani (relying on necessary truths), while others are jadali (based on widely accepted premises).

This passage is a critical refinement responding to an objection regarding logical conversion (al-'aks). The author first distinguishes between comprehensive and non-comprehensive rules of entailment, then contrasts his own demonstrative (burhani) proof with the opponent's dialectical (jadali) proof. He then establishes that truth in the converse is a necessary concomitant (lazim) of truth in the original, and this relationship can be considered in three ways: (1) necessary in reality (fi nafs al-amr), (2) necessary on a presupposition that itself necessitates the concomitance in reality, and (3) necessary on a presupposition that does not necessitate it in reality. He illustrates each case with the universal negative proposition 'No J is B' and its converse 'No B is J', showing that if the original is necessarily true, the converse must also be necessarily true on pain of contradiction (reductio). The author emphasizes that his proof is demonstrative, thus superior to dialectical arguments. The overall aim is to defend the validity of immediate inference through conversion.

العكس
اللازم
البرهان

D) It is longer.

Key Takeaway
Demonstrative proof is superior to dialectical proof because it relies on necessary truths, not merely accepted opinions.

Simple Conversion of Affirmative Conditional Propositions
Definition and proof of simple conversion for affirmative conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule of simple conversion to affirmative conditional propositions and verify the validity using the reductio method.

Core Idea
An affirmative conditional converts to a particular proposition, not a universal one.

In categorical propositions, simple conversion swaps subject and predicate while preserving truth. But for conditionals, the conversion is more restricted. This lesson explains how to convert an affirmative conditional (muttasila) and proves the rule using a reductio argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original conditional
Find an affirmative conjunctive conditional (if-then) statement. Note its quantity (universal/particular) and modality (necessary/possible/actual).
Example: 'Whenever A is B, then C is D' is universal and necessary if stated with 'necessarily'.
2
Perform simple conversion
Swap the antecedent and consequent while keeping the quality (affirmation) unchanged. The result is 'If C is D, then A is B'.
No conversion of negation here; we only deal with affirmatives.
3
Determine the resulting modality
The converse is not universal or necessary. If the original is actual (fi'li), the converse is a particular absolute (juz'iyya muṭlaqa). If the original is possible (mumkin), the converse is a particular possible (juz'iyya mumkina).
Particular means 'in some cases', not 'in all cases'.
4
Verify using reductio
Assume the converse is false. Then its contradictory is true. Show that this leads to a contradiction either by converting the contradictory or by conjoining it with the original premise. This proves the converse must be true in at least some cases.
The text only completes the first method (conversion of the contradictory); the second method is cut off.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في الموجبات من الشرطية ]

Section on the affirmatives of conditionals.
As for conditionals, the simple conversion therein is the swapping of antecedent to consequent and consequent to antecedent while preserving negation and affirmation, as is the case in categoricals. As for conjunctives (muttasilat), if the conjunctive is affirmative, whether universal or particular, necessary or non-necessary, then it converts to a particular absolute universal if it is among actuals, and a particular possible universal if it is among possibles. So it is said in the necessary [conditional] 'necessarily, whenever A is B, then C is D', then it may be that 'whenever C is D, then A is B' is true in the absolute general [sense]; otherwise its contradictory would be true. And this leads to an impossibility either by conversion of the contradictory, or by conjoining it with the original. By way of conversion: it is said if its contradictory is true, namely our statement 'not at all whenever C is D, then A is B'. And it converts universally, namely our statement 'not at all whenever A is B, then C is D'; and it was [true] necessarily 'whenever A is B, then C is D' – this is a contradiction. Or by way of conjoining: it is said if its contradictory is true, namely our statement 'not at all if' – [text cuts off]

Commentary
This passage defines the simple conversion (al-'aks al-mustawi) for conditional propositions (al-shartiyyat). The author explains that, like categoricals, conversion in conditionals involves swapping the antecedent and consequent while keeping the quality (affirmation/negation) unchanged. Affirmative conditionals (conjunctives) that are universal or particular, necessary or non-necessary, convert to a particular absolute proposition if the conditional is actual (fi'liyya) or a particular possible proposition if it is potential (mumkina). An example is given: if 'Whenever A is B, then C is D' is necessarily true, then it may be that 'Whenever C is D, then A is B' is true in an absolute sense. The proof is by reductio: if the converse is false, its negation leads to a contradiction either by converting the contradictory (which yields the negation of the original) or by conjoining the contradictory with the original premise. The author illustrates both methods, though the text cuts off before completing the second method.

Conversion of Universal Affirmative Conditionals
Author defines conversion for conditionals and provides two proofs for universal affirmative conjunctives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define simple conversion for conditional propositions, state the conversion rule for universal affirmative conjunctives, and reproduce one of the two proofs that justify the change in quantity.

Core Idea
Universal conditionals convert to particular conditionals—a key difference from categorical conversion.

Just as categorical propositions can be converted by swapping subject and predicate, conditional propositions (if-then statements) can also be converted by swapping antecedent and consequent. However, the conversion rule for conditionals is not identical to that for categoricals. This lesson introduces the definition of simple conversion for conditionals and the specific rule for universal affirmative conjunctives. The author explains that the quantity always becomes particular, and the modality depends on whether the conditional concerns actuals or possibles. He then provides two reductions ad absurdum to establish this rule.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of conversion for conditionals
Conversion in conditionals means swapping the antecedent (مقدم) and consequent (تالي) while preserving the quality (affirmation or negation). This is analogous to simple conversion (عكس مستوي) in categoricals.
The term 'عكس مستوي' is used here as the standard post-Avicennan term.
2
Identify the conversion rule for universal affirmative conjunctives
The author states: any universal affirmative conjunctive (whether necessary or not) converts to a particular affirmative. If the conditional is about actuals, the converse is a particular absolute general (جزئية مطلقة عامة). If about possibles, it is a particular possible general (جزئية ممكنة عامة).
The quantity changes from universal to particular; the quality remains affirmative.
3
Trace the first proof: by inverse conversion
Assume the contradictory of the converse: 'Absolutely never when B, A'. By inverse conversion (عكس النقيض), this becomes 'Absolutely never when A, B', which contradicts the original universal affirmative. Therefore the converse must hold as a particular.
Inverse conversion is a different operation: it converts 'Not all' to 'Not all' by contraposition.
4
Trace the second proof: by conjunction
Again assume the contradictory of the converse: 'Absolutely never when B, A'. Conjoin it with the original 'Whenever A, B'. From these two, derive 'Absolutely never when A, B' by modus tollens or similar, contradicting the original.
This proof does not require a separate inverse conversion step; it directly shows inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Chapter on the affirmative conditionals. As for conditionals, the simple conversion in them is replacing the antecedent with the consequent and the consequent with the antecedent while preserving negation and affirmation, as in categoricals. For conjunctives: when a conjunctive is universal affirmative, whether particular, necessary or not, it converts to a particular absolute general if it is from actuals, and to a particular possible general if from possibles. It is said in the implicational: necessarily whenever A is B, it is J D. Then it may be that whenever J is D, it is A B with absolute generality; otherwise its contradictory would be true. And that leads to absurdity by inverse conversion sometimes, and by conjunction with the original another time. Either by the method of conversion: it is said if its contradictory is true, that is our statement: 'Absolutely not: whenever J is D, it is A B.' And it converts universally, that is our statement: 'Absolutely not: whenever A is B, it is J D'; while it was necessarily 'Whenever A is B, it is J D'; this is a contradiction. Or by the method of conjunction: it is said if its contradictory is true, that is our statement: 'Absolutely not: whenever J is D, it is A B.' Then it is conjoined with the original and it is said: necessarily whenever A is B, it is J D, and absolutely not whenever J is D, it is A B. So absolutely not whenever A is B, it is J D; this is also a contradiction.
Source pages p. 475

Commentary
This passage defines the conversion of conditional propositions. The author begins by explaining that conversion in conditionals is simply swapping the antecedent and consequent while preserving the quality (affirmation/negation), just like in categorical propositions. Then he focuses on conjunctive conditionals (mutatsilat). He states that any universal affirmative conjunctive, whether necessary or not, converts to a particular affirmative. Specifically, if the conditional is about actual matters, the converse is a particular absolute general proposition; if about possibles, it is a particular possible general proposition. For example, the necessary implication 'Whenever it is A, it is B' converts to a particular 'Sometimes when it is B, it is A' with absolute generality. He then provides two proofs. The first is by inverse conversion (عكس النقيض): assume the contradictory of the converse, which would be 'Absolutely never when B, A'. By inverse conversion, this becomes 'Absolutely never when A, B', which contradicts the original necessary universal. The second proof is by conjunction (انضمام): again assume the contradictory of the converse, but here it is conjoined with the original to derive a contradiction directly. Both proofs establish the validity of the conversion.

Proof That Original Conditional Implies Converse
Author provides a proof based on separation to show truth of original implies truth of converse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the proof that truth in a conditional entails truth in its converse, using the concept of separation (iftiraq).

Core Idea
The truth of 'if A then B' forces the truth of 'if B then A' by eliminating separation.

This passage presents a remarkable proof that if a conditional statement is true, its converse must also be true. The proof hinges on analyzing whether there exists any instance where the antecedent and consequent diverge in truth value—a situation called 'separation' (iftiraq). Work through the two-case argument to see how the logician arrives at this conclusion.

Guided Path
1
State the Claim
The author asserts that the truth of the original conditional (if A then B) implies the truth of its converse (if B then A). He also notes that the original cannot be true simultaneously with the contradictory of the converse.
2
Define Separation (Iftiraq)
Separation refers to an instance where the antecedent is true while the consequent is false. If such an instance exists, the original conditional is false.
3
Case 1: Separation Exists
If there is at least one instance of separation, then the original conditional cannot be true, because in that instance the antecedent does not imply the consequent. Thus, this case is incompatible with the truth of the original.
4
Case 2: No Separation Exists
If no separation exists, then the original is true. The author argues that in this case, the contradictory of the converse (which asserts that there is an instance where B is true and A false) cannot be true, because that would imply a separation in the original. Therefore, the converse must be true.
The passage states: 'if it is not realized at all, then the truth in the contradictory of the converse cannot be realized, nor can the separation between antecedent and consequent be realized in the state of non-separation between them.'
5
Alternative Proof
The author adds that the truth of the original means that in every state where the antecedent holds, the consequent holds. Hence, they are always together, so swapping them yields a true converse.
This is the core idea: in every state, the truth values coincide, so the converse holds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثمَّ من البراهين ما يبرهن به الصدق في العكس بعد الصدق في الأصل لما كان الصدق في الأصل لا يمكن أن يكون بدون الصدق في العكس بل لا يمكن أن يكون مع الصدق في النقيض. وذلك مثل ما يقال: إن الافتراق بين المقدم والتالي في الأصل مثلا لا يخلو من أن يكون متحققا في الجملة، أو لا يكون، فإن كان متحققاً فلا يمكن أن يتحقق الصدق في الأصل وإلا لكان المقدم حالة الافتراق بينه وبين التالي مستلزما للتالي. ومن المحال أن يكون كذلك وإنه مستلزم للنقيض في تلك الحالة، وإن لم يكن أصلا فلا يمكن أن يتحقق الصدق في النقيض أعني نقيض العكس ولا يمكن أن يتحقق الافتراق بين المقدم والتالي حالة عدم الافتراق بينهما. وقد يقال أيضًا إن الصدق في الأصل لا يتحقق إلا وأن يتحقق كل واحد من المقدم والتالي في حالة وهي حالة تحقق المقدم. ولما تحقق كل واحد منهما في حالة فلا يمكن أن يتحقق هذا دون ذلك في تلك الحالة، ولا ذلك دون هذا كذلك: وهذا هو المطلوب ليس إلا.
Then among the proofs is that which proves the truth in the converse after the truth in the original, because the truth in the original cannot be without the truth in the converse, nor can it be with the truth in the contradictory. For example, it is said: the separation between the antecedent and consequent in the original cannot but be either realized in general or not. If it is realized, then the truth in the original cannot be realized, otherwise the antecedent in the state of separation between it and the consequent would necessitate the consequent. And it is impossible that it be so, and it necessitates the contradictory in that state. And if it is not realized at all, then the truth in the contradictory (i.e., the contradictory of the converse) cannot be realized, nor can the separation between antecedent and consequent be realized in the state of non-separation between them. And it is also said that the truth in the original is realized only when each of the antecedent and consequent is realized in a state, which is the state of the realization of the antecedent. And when each of them is realized in a state, it is impossible that this one be realized without that one in that state, nor that one without this one. This is exactly what is sought.
Source pages p. 476

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that the truth of the original conditional entails the truth of its converse. The author first states the goal: to show that truth in the original cannot exist without truth in the converse, nor with truth in the contradictory of the converse. The proof proceeds by considering the separation (افتراق) between the antecedent and consequent. There are two cases: either separation occurs in some instance or it does not. If separation occurs, then the original conditional cannot be true, because in the state of separation the antecedent would imply the consequent, which is impossible. If separation does not occur at all, then the contradictory of the converse (which asserts that sometimes when the consequent holds, the antecedent does not) cannot be true, because that would require separation in the state of non-separation. Alternatively, the author notes that the truth of the original means that in every instance where the antecedent holds, the consequent also holds. Thus, in that state, they are always together, so swapping them yields a true converse. This is the core idea.

Key Takeaway
The truth of a conditional forces the truth of its converse by eliminating the possibility of separation in either direction.

Analysis of an Objection to Universal-Particular Conversion
Objector argues against universal-to-particular conversion rule

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the objector's argument against universal-to-particular conversion and identify the key logical premise that the author targets in his reply.

Core Idea
Conversion from universal to particular risks making the particular more general, which the objector claims blocks conversion.

In this passage, a logical objector challenges the rule that a universal affirmative conditional converts to a particular affirmative. The objector argues that if conversion holds, the particular would be more general than the universal, and this greater generality itself prevents conversion. The author begins a rebuttal but is cut off. This lesson unpacks the objector's reasoning and sets the stage for understanding the full dialectic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's starting assumption
The objector assumes the logicians' claim: a universal affirmative conditional converts to a particular affirmative (i.e., 'If A then B' becomes 'Some B is A' when conditional? Actually, this is about categorical propositions: 'All A is B' converts to 'Some B is A'). Take note of the conditional phrasing in Arabic: 'law kāna kamā dhakartum...' meaning 'if it were as you mentioned'.
The passage is about categorical conversion, not conditional syllogisms despite the word 'conditional' in translation.
2
Extract the objector's conditional reasoning
The objector uses a conditional: If universal converts to particular, then the particular must be more general (a'amm) than the universal. Otherwise (i.e., if it were not more general), the particular would not convert. This is a logical necessity.
The objector is analyzing the relationship between original and converse in terms of generality.
3
Understand the prevention claim
The objector asserts that the particular being more general is a preventer (māni') of conversion. Because for conversion to be valid, the original and converse must have the same truth range. If the converse is more general, there can be cases where the original is false but the converse true, violating equivalence.
This is based on the earlier rule that conversion requires that whenever the original is true, the converse is true, but the converse being more general would allow false originals with true converses.
4
Analyze the separation argument
The objector invokes a dilemma: either there is a separation between the truth of the original and the truth of the converse (i.e., cases where original is true but converse false or vice versa), or there is no separation. If separation exists, conversion fails because the truth conditions differ. If no separation exists, then the converse is not more general, contradicting the assumption.
This is a classic logical dilemma forcing the logician to accept that conversion is impossible.
5
Observe the author's response opening
The author begins: 'But we say: This is among the things that contradict the truth in the original if the consequent...' The response is cut off, but we can infer he plans to argue that the objector's reasoning actually undermines the truth of the original proposition, not the conversion rule.
This incomplete sentence hints at a reductio ad absurdum: if the objector's objection holds, then the original universal statement would be false, which is unacceptable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لقائل أن يقول في هذا المقام لو كان كما ذكرتم وهو أن تكون الموجبة الكلية تنعكس موجبة جزئية لكان من اللوازم أن تكون الجزئية أعم من الكلية وإلا لكانت الجزئية تنعكس، وكونها أعم من جملة ما يكون مانعا عن الانعكاس. وذلك لأن الافتراق بين الصدق في الأصل، والصدق في العكس لا يخلو من أن يكون متحققاً في الجملة، أو لا يكون؛ فإن كان متحققاً فلا مجال للانعكاس لما ذكرتم من قبل، وإن لم يكن متحققا البتة فلا مجال للعمومية وهذا ظاهر. لكنا نقول: هذا من جملة ما ينافي الصدق في الأصل إذا كان التالي
Then someone may say in this place: If it were as you mentioned, that the universal affirmative converts to a particular affirmative, then it would be necessary that the particular is more general than the universal, otherwise the particular would not convert, and its being more general is something that prevents conversion. That is because the separation between the truth in the original and the truth in the converse can only be either realized in general or not; if it is realized, there is no room for conversion for the reason you mentioned earlier; and if it is not realized at all, there is no room for generality, and this is apparent. But we say: This is among the things that contradict the truth in the original if the consequent...
Source pages p. 476

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the previously established conversion rule. The objector argues: If a universal affirmative conditional converts to a particular affirmative, then the particular converse must be more general than the universal original (since particular is less restrictive). Otherwise, if the particular were not more general, it would not be a valid conversion (because it would be equivalent or weaker). But the particular being more general would prevent conversion, because for conversion to hold, the original and converse should have the same range of truth. The objector then uses the same separation argument: if there is separation between the truth of the original and the truth of the converse, then conversion fails; if there is no separation, then there is no generality. The author begins a response with 'But we say', arguing that the objector's point actually contradicts the truth of the original if the consequent... The response is cut off at the page boundary. The likely continuation would refute the objection by showing that the particular is indeed more general but that does not prevent conversion.
