Proof of the Conversion of the Universal Affirmative Proposition
Proof of conversion and objection response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the proof that a universal affirmative conditional converts to a particular affirmative and analyze the logical structure of the objection and response.

Core Idea
A universal affirmative conditional proves its converse, but only in a weaker form.

The author establishes that a universal affirmative conditional (e.g., 'Whenever A is B, then C is D') necessarily implies a particular affirmative converse (sometimes when C is D, then A is B). This is proven by considering the relation of separation between antecedent and consequent. An objector argues that this would make the particular more general, but the author refutes this by appealing to the necessary implication.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition
The original is a universal affirmative conditional: 'Whenever the antecedent holds, the consequent holds.' The proof must show that this implies the converse is true at least sometimes.
2
Understand the proof's key idea: separation
The proof considers whether the antecedent and consequent can be 'separated' (i.e., one true without the other). If separation is possible, the original cannot be universally true. If impossible, the converse must hold at least partially.
3
Follow the two-case argument
Case 1: If separation is possible in some instance, then the original universal claim is false. Case 2: If separation is never possible, then whenever the antecedent holds, the consequent holds; and in the state where the antecedent holds, both are true together, so the converse is true in that instance.
4
Analyze the objection
The objector claims that if the universal converts to a particular, the particular would be more general than the universal, which would prevent conversion. This is based on a misunderstanding of the relationship between the original and its converse when the consequent is broader.
5
See how the author responds
The author replies that the objection assumes the original is false (since the consequent being more general would contradict the universal truth). But the discussion assumes the original is true, so the objection is irrelevant. Moreover, the particular converse follows necessarily from the universal original.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثمَّ من البراهين ما يبرهن به الصدق في العكس بعد الصدق في الأصل لما كان الصدق في الأصل لا يمكن أن يكون بدون الصدق في العكس بل لا يمكن أن يكون مع الصدق في النقيض. وذلك مثل ما يقال: إن الافتراق بين المقدم والتالي في الأصل مثلا لا يخلو من أن يكون متحققا في الجملة، أو لا يكون، فإن كان متحققاً فلا يمكن أن يتحقق الصدق في الأصل وإلا لكان المقدم حالة الافتراق بينه وبين التالي مستلزما للتالي. ومن المحال أن يكون كذلك وإنه مستلزم للنقيض في تلك الحالة، وإن لم يكن أصلا فلا يمكن أن يتحقق الصدق في النقيض أعني نقيض العكس ولا يمكن أن يتحقق الافتراق بين المقدم والتالي حالة عدم الافتراق بينهما. وقد يقال أيضًا إن الصدق في الأصل لا يتحقق إلا وأن يتحقق كل واحد من المقدم والتالي في حالة وهي حالة تحقق المقدم. ولما تحقق كل واحد منهما في حالة فلا يمكن أن يتحقق هذا دون ذلك في تلك الحالة، ولا ذلك دون هذا كذلك: وهذا هو المطلوب ليس إلا. ثم لقائل أن يقول في هذا المقام لو كان كما ذكرتم وهو أن تكون الموجبة الكلية تنعكس موجبة جزئية لكان من اللوازم أن تكون الجزئية أعم من الكلية وإلا لكانت الجزئية تنعكس، وكونها أعم من جملة ما يكون مانعا عن الانعكاس. وذلك لأن الافتراق بين الصدق في الأصل، والصدق في العكس لا يخلو من أن يكون متحققاً في الجملة، أو لا يكون؛ فإن كان متحققاً فلا مجال للانعكاس لما ذكرتم من قبل، وإن لم يكن متحققا البتة فلا مجال للعمومية وهذا ظاهر. لكنا نقول: هذا من جملة ما ينافي الصدق في الأصل إذا كان التالي أعم من المقدم، وكلامنا على تقدير الصدق فيه فلا نعتبره. ولأن الجزئية وهي العكس لا تكون أعم من الكلية إلا وأن تكون من اللوازم، ولو كان كذلك لكان من الممتنع أن يكون الصدق في الأصل بدون الصدق في العكس، هذا هو الكلام في الموجبة الكلية.
Then, from the proofs is that which proves the truth of the converse after the truth of the original, because the truth of the original cannot exist without the truth of the converse, nor can it exist with the truth of the contradictory. For example, it is said: The separation between the antecedent and consequent in the original, for instance, is either realized in some respect or not. If it is realized, then the truth of the original cannot be realized, for then the antecedent in the state of separation between it and the consequent would necessitate the consequent, which is impossible, and it would necessitate the contradictory in that state. And if it is not realized at all, then the truth of the contradictory, i.e., the contradictory of the converse, cannot be realized, nor can the separation between the antecedent and consequent be realized in the state of non-separation between them. And it is also said that the truth of the original is not realized unless both the antecedent and consequent are realized in a state, namely the state of the realization of the antecedent. And since each of them is realized in a state, it cannot be that this is realized without that in that state, nor that without this likewise. And this is exactly what is required. Then someone may object in this context: If it were as you mentioned, that the universal affirmative converts to a particular affirmative, then it would follow that the particular is more general than the universal; otherwise the particular would convert, and its being more general is a hindrance to conversion. This is because the separation between the truth of the original and the truth of the converse is either realized in some respect or not; if it is realized, there is no room for conversion for the reason you mentioned; and if it is not realized at all, there is no room for generality, and this is clear. But we say: This is something that contradicts the truth of the original when the consequent is more general than the antecedent, and our discussion assumes the truth of it, so we do not consider it. And because the particular, which is the converse, is not more general than the universal except that it is from the necessary implications, and if that were the case, it would be impossible for the truth of the original to be without the truth of the converse. This is the discussion of the universal affirmative.
Source pages p. 476

Commentary
This passage provides a proof and response regarding the conversion of the universal affirmative proposition (al-mūjiba al-kulliyya). The author first presents a proof that the universal affirmative converts to a particular affirmative. The proof uses the relationship between antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tālī) in the original proposition, arguing that if the original is true, the converse must also be true, and that the impossibility of the opposite leads to the desired conclusion. Then an objection is raised: if the universal converts to a particular, then the particular would be more general than the universal, which would prevent conversion because there would be a separation between the truths. The author responds by stating that this objection is based on assuming the consequent is more general, but the discussion assumes the truth of the original, so it is not considered. Furthermore, the particular as converse is not more general unless it is a necessary implication, which would make the original true impossible without the converse. Thus, the conversion holds.

Key Terms
عكس ('aks): Conversion: the operation of swapping the antecedent and consequent in a conditional proposition, or subject and predicate in a categorical, while maintaining truth conditions as per logical rules. [Note: In this text, 'aks is specifically about conditional propositions, but in categorical logic it applies to subject and predicate. No significant shift noted.]
موجبة كلية (mūjiba kulliyya): Universal affirmative proposition: a proposition that affirms a predicate of all subjects of a certain kind, here used in conditional form: 'Whenever A is B, then C is D'. [Note: In categorical logic, universal affirmative usually means 'All S are P'. Here it is in conditional context. No significant shift.]
موجبة جزئية (mūjiba juz'iyya): Particular affirmative proposition: a proposition that affirms a predicate of some subjects, here 'Sometimes when A is B, then C is D'. [Note: Same as above.]
مقدم (muqaddam): Antecedent: the part of a conditional proposition that precedes the 'then', representing the condition. [Note: Stable term.]
تالي (tālī): Consequent: the part of a conditional proposition that follows the 'then', representing the result. [Note: Stable term.]
لازم (lāzim): Necessary implication: the relationship where the truth of the antecedent necessitates the truth of the consequent. [Note: In logic, often used in the context of entailment. Stable.]
افتراق (iftirāq): Separation: the state where the antecedent and consequent are not related by implication, i.e., the proposition is not truly conditional. [Note: Specific to this text's terminology for conditional conversion proofs.]

Conversion of the Particular Affirmative
Author states the particular affirmative converts to a particular affirmative with brief proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert a particular affirmative proposition into its converse and prove the conversion using reductio ad absurdum.

Core Idea
The particular affirmative converts to itself — 'Some A is B' implies 'Some B is A'.

The author extends the conversion rule to the particular affirmative proposition. Just as the universal affirmative converts to a particular affirmative, the particular affirmative also converts to a particular affirmative. The proof uses the same reductio method: if the converse were false, its contradictory would be true, leading to impossibility. This lesson walks through the logic of that conversion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
The passage deals with the particular affirmative (mūjiba juz'iyya). Note that it is expressed in conditional form: 'Sometimes when antecedent, then consequent.'
2
State the conversion rule
The particular affirmative converts to a particular affirmative: from 'Sometimes when A is B, C is D' we infer 'Sometimes when C is D, A is B.'
3
Understand the reductio proof
The author argues: if the converse were false, its contradictory would be true. That contradictory would lead to an absurdity (as known from earlier discussion). Therefore, the converse must be true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٢] وعلى هذا في الموجبة الجزئية فإنها تنعكس موجبة جزئية كذلك كما يقال إذا صدق قولنا قد يكون إذا كان «أب» فـ ج د صدق قولنا قد يكون إذا كان (ج (د) فـ «أب» وإلا لصدق نقيضه ؛ وإنه يفضي إلى المحال على ما عرف.
[2] And similarly for the particular affirmative: it converts to a particular affirmative, as it is said: if our statement 'Sometimes when A is B, then C is D' is true, then our statement 'Sometimes when C is D, then A is B' is true; otherwise its contradictory would be true, and that leads to impossibility, as is known.
Source pages p. 477

Commentary
This short passage presents the conversion rule for the particular affirmative proposition (al-mūjiba al-juz'iyya). The author states that just like the universal affirmative, the particular affirmative converts to a particular affirmative. He provides an example in conditional form: if the proposition 'Sometimes when A is B, then C is D' is true, then the converse 'Sometimes when C is D, then A is B' is also true. The proof is by reductio: if the converse were false, its contradictory would be true, which would lead to an impossibility. The reasoning mirrors that given for the universal affirmative, but now the proposition is particular in quantity.

Key Terms

نقيض

Conversion of Accidental Conditionals: Modality Matters
Author explains conversion rules for accidental conditionals, distinguishing possible and impossible antecedents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether an accidental conditional proposition can be validly converted based on the modality (possibility or impossibility) of its antecedent.

Core Idea
Accidental conditionals convert only when the antecedent is possible; impossible antecedents block conversion.

In the previous lesson, we learned that implicational (luzūmiyya) conditionals convert. But what about accidental (ittifāqiyya) conditionals, where the connection is merely coincidental? This passage reveals a key distinction: conversion depends on the modality of the antecedent. Only when the antecedent is possible does the accidental conditional convert; if the antecedent is impossible, conversion fails. This rule prevents false inferences from vacuously true conditionals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of conditional
Look at the conditional proposition. Is the connection between antecedent and consequent necessary (luzūmiyya) or accidental (ittifāqiyya)? The passage focuses on accidental conditionals.
Accidental conditionals are those where the antecedent does not necessitate the consequent; the connection is coincidental.
2
Check the modality of the antecedent
Determine whether the antecedent is possible (mumkin) or impossible (mumtaniʿ). The passage states that if the antecedent is possible, the conditional converts; if impossible, it does not.
Examples of impossible antecedents from the passage: the existence of void (khalāʾ) in the physical world.
3
Follow the conversion rule for possible antecedents
For an accidental conditional with a possible antecedent, convert it just like an implicational conditional: a universal accidental conditional converts to a particular accidental conditional, and a particular converts to a particular.
The passage gives the example: 'Whenever man is rational in essence, the donkey is braying in essence' (universal) converts to 'Sometimes when the donkey is braying in essence, man is rational in essence' (particular).
4
Handle impossible antecedents: no conversion
If the antecedent is impossible, do not convert. The original conditional is true vacuously (since the antecedent never occurs), but the converse would imply the existence of the impossible antecedent, which is false.
Example: 'Whenever void exists, man exists' is true, but its converse 'Sometimes when man exists, void exists' is false because it would affirm the existence of void.
5
Test with a new example
Construct your own accidental conditional with a possible or impossible antecedent from Islamic sciences, and apply the rule to see if the conversion is valid.
For instance, consider 'Whenever the world is eternal, it is necessary in itself' (antecedent is impossible in Islamic theology).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٣] وأما في الاتفاقية؛ فالمشهور عند الجمهور منهم أنها تنعكس كاللزومية كلية كانت أو جزئية إذا كان المقدم فيها من الممكنات كما يقال مثلا كلَّما كان الإنسان ناطقا في ماهيته كان الحمار ناهقًا في ماهيته صدق قولنا قد يكون إذا كان الحمار ناهقًا في ماهيته كان الإنسان ناطقا في ماهيته وإلا لصدق نقيضه؛ وذلك يفضي إلى المحال . [٤] وعلى هذا في الجزئية فيقال مثلا قد يكون إذا كان الإنسان متحرّكًا بالعرض كان الفرس متحرّكا بالذات، قد يكون إذا كان الفرس متحركًا بالذات كان الإنسان متحركًا بالعرض. [٥] فأما إذا كان المقدم فيها من الممتنعات فلا تنعكس لما أنه يصدق أن يقال كلَّما كان الخلاء موجودًا كان الإنسان موجودًا، ولا يصدق أن يقال قد يكون إذا كان الإنسان موجودا كان الخلاء موجودًا والمعنى من الخلاء في هذا المقام هو أن يوجد جسمان لا يتلاقيان، ولا يكون بينهما ما يتلاقيانهما (١). وأما الفرق بين اللزومية والاتفاقية فقد مرَّ من قبل ان اللزومية مشتملة على شيء يمتنع بالنظر إلى ذلك الشيء أن يتحقق المقدم دون التالي حتى إذا [١٣٧] (۱) النسخة يتلاقيانه وما أثبتناه هو الصواب كما هو الظاهر من السياق.
[3] As for the accidental (conditional), the well-known view among the majority is that it converts like the implicational (conditional), whether universal or particular, when the antecedent in it is among possible things. For example, as it is said: 'Whenever man is rational in his essence, the donkey is braying in its essence' is true, then our statement 'Sometimes when the donkey is braying in its essence, man is rational in his essence' is true; otherwise its contradictory would be true, and that leads to impossibility. [4] And similarly for the particular, as it is said: 'Sometimes when man is accidentally moving, the horse is essentially moving' then 'Sometimes when the horse is essentially moving, man is accidentally moving.' [5] As for when the antecedent is among impossible things, then it does not convert, because it is true to say 'Whenever void exists, man exists', but it is not true to say 'Sometimes when man exists, void exists'; and the meaning of 'void' in this context is that two bodies exist that do not meet, and there is nothing between them that they meet. (1) And as for the difference between the implicational and accidental (conditional), it has passed before: the implicational includes something with respect to which it is impossible that the antecedent is realized without the consequent, so that... [137] (1) The copy has 'يتلاقيانه' and what we have established is correct, as is apparent from the context.
Source pages p. 477

Commentary
This passage addresses the conversion of accidental conditional propositions (al-ittifāqiyya). The author states the well-known view of the majority of logicians: accidental conditionals convert like implicational ones (al-luzūmiyya) when the antecedent is among possible things (mumkināt). He gives an example: the universal 'Whenever man is rational in essence, the donkey is braying in essence' (an accidental connection) converts to a particular 'Sometimes when the donkey is braying, man is rational'. The proof again uses reductio. Then for the particular accidental, he gives 'Sometimes when man is accidentally moving, the horse is essentially moving' converting similarly. However, when the antecedent is impossible (mumtani'āt), conversion fails. Example: 'Whenever void exists, man exists' is true (because void cannot exist, so the conditional is vacuously true), but the converse 'Sometimes when man exists, void exists' is false because it would imply the existence of void, which is impossible. The author then notes that the distinction between implicational and accidental conditionals was explained earlier: in an implicational conditional, there is a connection that makes it impossible for the antecedent to be true without the consequent.

لزومية
ممكن
ممتنع
خلاء

Key Takeaway
Accidental conditionals convert only when the antecedent is possible; impossible antecedents block conversion because the converse would affirm an impossibility.

Conversion of Necessary Affirmative Conditionals
Author explains conversion of necessary affirmative conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert universal and particular necessary affirmative conditionals correctly and verify their validity.

Core Idea
A necessary conditional always converts to an affirmative conditional of the same quantity.

The conversion of necessary conditionals is a foundational operation in Islamic logic, allowing us to derive new conditional statements from established ones. This passage explains how to convert both universal and particular necessary conditionals, ensuring that truth is preserved under the same modality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type and quantity
Determine whether the conditional is universal (كلية) or particular (جزئية) and whether it is necessary (لزومية). Necessary conditionals are those where the antecedent cannot be true without the consequent.
2
Perform the conversion
Swap the antecedent and consequent. For universal conditionals, the converse is particular; for particular conditionals, the converse remains particular.
The text states: 'الجزئية وهي العكس لا تكون أعم من الكلية' — the converse is particular, not more general.
3
Test for contradiction
Assume the converse is false and see if it leads to a contradiction. If so, the conversion is valid.
The author uses the method of contradiction (إلا لصدق نقيضه) to prove validity.
4
State the result
Conclude that the converted proposition is true under the same truth conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أعم من المقدم، وكلامنا على تقدير الصدق فيه فلا نعتبره. ولأن الجزئية وهي العكس لا تكون أعم من الكلية إلا وأن تكون من اللوازم، ولو كان كذلك لكان من الممتنع أن يكون الصدق في الأصل بدون الصدق في العكس، هذا هو الكلام في الموجبة الكلية. [٢] وعلى هذا في الموجبة الجزئية فإنها تنعكس موجبة جزئية كذلك كما يقال إذا صدق قولنا قد يكون إذا كان «أب» فـ ج د صدق قولنا قد يكون إذا كان (ج (د) فـ «أب» وإلا لصدق نقيضه ؛ وإنه يفضي إلى المحال على ما عرف. وأما في الاتفاقية؛ فالمشهور عند الجمهور منهم [٣] أنها تنعكس كاللزومية كلية كانت أو جزئية إذا كان المقدم فيها من الممكنات كما يقال مثلا كلَّما كان الإنسان ناطقا في ماهيته كان الحمار ناهقًا في ماهيته صدق قولنا قد يكون إذا كان الحمار ناهقًا في ماهيته كان الإنسان ناطقا في ماهيته وإلا لصدق نقيضه؛ وذلك يفضي إلى المحال . [٤] وعلى هذا في الجزئية فيقال مثلا قد يكون إذا كان الإنسان متحرّكًا بالعرض كان الفرس متحرّكا بالذات، قد يكون إذا كان الفرس متحركًا بالذات كان الإنسان متحركًا بالعرض. [٥] فأما إذا كان المقدم فيها من الممتنعات فلا تنعكس لما أنه يصدق أن يقال كلَّما كان الخلاء موجودًا كان الإنسان موجودًا، ولا يصدق أن يقال قد يكون إذا كان الإنسان موجودا كان الخلاء موجودًا والمعنى من الخلاء في هذا المقام هو أن يوجد جسمان لا يتلاقيان، ولا يكون بينهما ما يتلاقيانهما. وأما الفرق بين اللزومية والاتفاقية فقد مرَّ من قبل ان اللزومية مشتملة على شيء يمتنع بالنظر إلى ذلك الشيء أن يتحقق المقدم دون التالي حتى إذا تحقق هذا في الخارج تحقق ذلك، وإذا لم يتحقق ذلك فلا يمكن أن يتحقق هذا؛ والاتفاقية لا تكون كذلك.
More general than the premise, and our discussion assumes truth in it, so we do not consider it. And because the particular, which is the converse, is not more general than the universal unless it is among the necessary consequences, and if it were so, then it would be impossible that truth in the original occurs without truth in the converse. This is the discussion about the universal affirmative. [2] And likewise for the particular affirmative: it converts as a particular affirmative similarly, as it is said: if our statement 'it may be that when A is B, then C is D' is true, then our statement 'it may be that when C is D, then A is B' is true; otherwise its contradictory would be true, and that leads to absurdity as known. As for the accidental conditional, the well-known view among the majority of them [3] is that it converts like the necessary conditional, whether universal or particular, if the antecedent in it is among the possibles, as is said for example: 'Whenever man is rational in his essence, the donkey is braying in its essence' is true, then our statement 'it may be that when the donkey is braying in its essence, man is rational in his essence' is true; otherwise its contradictory would be true, and that leads to absurdity. [4] And likewise for the particular: it is said for example: 'It may be that when man is accidentally moving, the horse is essentially moving; it may be that when the horse is essentially moving, man is accidentally moving.' [5] As for when the antecedent in it is among the impossibles, then it does not convert, because it is true to say 'Whenever void exists, man exists', but it is not true to say 'It may be that when man exists, void exists'. The meaning of 'void' in this context is that two bodies exist that do not meet, and there is nothing between them that they meet. As for the difference between the necessary and the accidental, it has passed before: the necessary conditional contains something such that with respect to that thing, it is impossible for the antecedent to be realized without the consequent, so that if the antecedent is realized externally, the consequent is realized, and if the consequent is not realized, then it is impossible for the antecedent to be realized. The accidental is not like that.
Source pages p. 477

Commentary
This passage discusses the conversion (عكس) of affirmative conditional propositions. The author first addresses universal affirmative conditionals (موجبة كلية), explaining that their converse must also be true under the same truth conditions; otherwise it would lead to contradiction. Then particular affirmative conditionals (موجبة جزئية) are shown to convert as particular affirmatives, using an example with variables أ and ج. Next, the accidental conditional (اتفاقية) is treated: the majority opinion holds that it converts like the necessary conditional (لزومية) provided the antecedent is possible. Examples are given: one involving man and donkey (an absurd illustration) and another with accidental and essential motion of animals. An exception is made when the antecedent is impossible, e.g., 'void exists' — such conditionals do not convert because the converse would be false. Finally, the author reiterates the distinction between necessary and accidental conditionals: in a necessary conditional, the antecedent cannot be true without the consequent; in an accidental one, no such necessitation holds.

Key Terms

الموجبة الكلية
اللزومية
الاتفاقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Particular affirmative
C) Universal negative
D) Particular negative

Key Takeaway
A necessary affirmative conditional converts to an affirmative conditional of the same quantity, with the universal becoming particular.

Apply conversion to the necessary conditional: 'Whenever prayer is obligatory, purification is required.' State the converse and verify its validity using the method of contradiction.

Conversion of Accidental Affirmative Conditionals
Author explains conversion of accidental affirmative conditionals with condition on antecedent possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when an accidental conditional converts and identify the exception for impossible antecedents.

Core Idea
An accidental conditional converts only if the antecedent is possible; impossible antecedents block conversion.

Accidental conditionals, where the antecedent does not necessitate the consequent, can still be converted under certain conditions. This passage explains that the majority view allows conversion if the antecedent is possible, but denies it when the antecedent is impossible. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying conversion in Islamic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional as accidental
Determine whether the conditional is accidental (اتفاقية) by checking if the antecedent can be true without the consequent necessarily following.
Accidental conditionals lack the necessitation found in necessary conditionals.
2
Check the modality of the antecedent
Is the antecedent possible (ممكن) or impossible (ممتنع)? If possible, conversion may hold; if impossible, it does not convert.
The author explicitly states: 'إذا كان المقدم فيها من الممكنات' (if the antecedent is among the possibles) for conversion to hold.
3
Perform the conversion
If the antecedent is possible, convert by swapping the antecedent and consequent. The result is a particular affirmative conditional (or universal? The text says 'كاللزومية كلية كانت أو جزئية' – like the necessary, whether universal or particular.
The conversion pattern is the same as for necessary conditionals: universal converts to particular, particular remains particular.
4
Verify using the exception
Test with an impossible antecedent, e.g., 'Whenever void exists, man exists'. This conditional is true, but its converse 'If man exists, void exists' is false, so conversion fails.
The example of void illustrates why conversion fails for impossible antecedents.

Conversion of Universal Negative Conditionals
Author discusses conversion of negative conditionals, specifying required modalities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which universal negative conditionals can be validly converted and apply the proof method using contradiction.

Core Idea
Only necessary, permanent, generally conditioned, and generally conventional universal negatives convert as themselves.

Not all negative conditionals convert like affirmatives. In this lesson, we explore the specific modalities that allow a universal negative conditional to be converted into a converse with the same truth value. We will follow the author's proof using the method of conversion and the method of conjunction, both based on contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of conditional
Determine whether the conditional is affirmative or negative. The author focuses on universal negative conditionals (e.g., 'Necessarily not at all when A is B, C is D').
Negative conditionals have the form: 'Not (if p then q)' or equivalently 'p and not q' in standard logic, but here they are treated as a single proposition.
2
Determine the modality
Check if the conditional is necessary (ضرورية), permanent (دائمة), generally conditioned (مشروطة عامة), or generally conventional (عرفية عامة). Only these four modalities allow conversion as itself.
Other modalities (e.g., possible) do not convert at all.
3
Apply the conversion method
Assume the original is true. If the converse were false, its contradictory (a particular affirmative) would be true. Show that this leads to a contradiction, proving the converse must be true.
The author provides two methods: conversion (الانعكاس) and conjunction (الانضمام).
4
Check the specially conditioned case
Note that for المشروطة الخاصة there is disagreement: some say it converts generally, others only partially (text cuts off).
This indicates that not all modalities are settled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في السوالب من الشرطية ] هذا إذا كانت المتصلة من الموجبات، فأما إذا كانت من السوالب فإنها لا تنعكس إلا إذا كانت كلية؛ والكلية لا تنعكس كنفسها كما وجهة إلا إذا كانت ضرورية، أو دائمة، أو مشروطة عامة، أو عرفية عامة. فإن ما عداها لا تنعكس كنفسها بل لا تنعكس أصلا ؛ [۱] أما الضرورية فيقال بالضرورة ليس البتة إذا كان أب» كان ج د بالضرورة ليس البتة إذا كان «ج د» كان «أب» وإلا لصدق نقيضه وهو قولنا قد يكون إذا كان ج د كان «أب» فينعكس عكســا جزئيا وهو قولنا قد يكون إذا كان (أ ب) كان (ج) د بالإمكان العام، وقد كان بالضرورة ليس البتة إذا كان (أ ب) كان ( دا، هذا خلف؛ هذا بطريق الانعكاس. وأما بطريق الانضمام فيقال قد يكون إذا كان ج د كان «أب»، وليس البتة إذا كان «أب) كان (ج) د ، فقد يكون إذا كان جدا فلا يكون ج د، هذا خلف؛ [٢] وعلى هذا في الدائمة؛ إذ لا فرق بينهما إلا في الضرورة. [٣] وأما المشروطة العامة فيقال بالضرورة ليس البتة إذا كان «أب» كان «ج د ما دام «أب»، بالضرورة ليس البتة إذا كان ( د) كان أب» ما دام جد وإلا لصدق نقيضه وهو قولنا قد يكون إذا كان ج (د) كان «أب» ما دام (ج) د) بالإمكان العام فينعكس عكسا جزئيا؛ وذلك يفضي إلى المحال، [٤] وعلى هذا في العرفية العامة؛ إذ لا فرق بينهما إلا في الضرورة. [٥] وأما المشروطة الخاصة فالكلام فيها نحو الكلام فيما مر : [1] فإن منهم من قال إنها تنعكس عكسا عامتها»، [ب] ومنهم من قال إنها تنعكس
Section: [On negative conditionals]. This is when the conditional is affirmative. As for when it is negative, it does not convert unless it is universal; and the universal does not convert as itself in the same manner unless it is necessary, or permanent, or generally conditioned, or generally conventional. For what is other than these does not convert as itself, rather it does not convert at all. [1] As for the necessary: it is said 'necessarily not at all when A is B, C is D' then 'necessarily not at all when C is D, A is B'; otherwise its contradictory would be true, which is our statement 'it may be that when C is D, A is B', so it converts into a particular conversion, which is our statement 'it may be that when A is B, C is D by general possibility', and it was 'necessarily not at all when A is B, C is D' — this is a contradiction. This is by way of conversion. As for by way of conjunction: it is said 'it may be that when C is D, A is B', and 'not at all when A is B, C is D', so it may be that when C is D, then C is not D — this is a contradiction. [2] And likewise for the permanent condition, for there is no difference between them except in necessity. [3] As for the generally conditioned: it is said 'necessarily not at all when A is B, C is D as long as A is B', then 'necessarily not at all when C is D, A is B as long as C is D'; otherwise its contradictory would be true, which is our statement 'it may be that when C is D, A is B as long as C is D by general possibility', so it converts into a particular conversion; and that leads to absurdity. [4] And likewise for the generally conventional, for there is no difference between them except in necessity. [5] As for the specially conditioned, the discussion about it is similar to what has passed: [a] some say it converts as a general conversion, [b] and some say it converts...
Source pages p. 477

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on the conversion of negative conditional propositions (السوالب من الشرطية). The author states that only universal negative conditionals can convert, and even then, only under specific modalities: necessary (ضرورية), permanent (دائمة), generally conditioned (مشروطة عامة), or generally conventional (عرفية عامة). Other modalities do not convert at all. For necessary negatives, the author presents two methods of proof: conversion (الانعكاس) and conjunction (الانضمام). In the conversion method, assuming the original negative is true, if the converse were false, its contradictory (a particular affirmative) would be true, leading to contradiction. In the conjunction method, assuming the converse is true while the original is false leads to an impossible scenario. The same reasoning applies to the permanent condition, differing only in that 'permanent' does not include necessity. For generally conditioned conditionals (where the condition 'as long as A is B' is attached), the proof uses the same structure. The generally conventional is analogous. Finally, the specially conditioned (مشروطة خاصة) is addressed: there is disagreement — some say it converts generally, others... (text cuts off).

الضرورية

Conversion of Negative Conditionals
Rule for conversion of negative conditionals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which universal negative conditional propositions convert as themselves according to the rule.

Core Idea
Not all universal negatives convert; only four modal types preserve their form.

In this lesson, we examine a crucial nuance in the conversion (in'ikas) of conditional propositions. While affirmative conditionals have their own rules, negative conditionals are more restrictive. The author specifies that only universal negatives of certain modal types convert as themselves. Understanding this distinction is essential for sound logical inference in Islamic sciences, particularly when analyzing statements in kalam or usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish affirmative from negative conditionals
The passage begins by noting that the previous rule applied to affirmative conditionals. Now it addresses negatives. Identify whether your conditional is affirmative or negative; this determines which conversion rules apply.
2
Check universality
For negative conditionals, conversion only applies if the proposition is universal (kulliyya). A particular negative does not convert at all. Verify the quantity: is it 'every time' or 'some time'?
3
Recall the four modal types that convert as themselves
Even among universal negatives, only four modal types convert without change: necessary (daruriya), perpetual (da'ima), common conditional (mashruta 'amma), and common customary ('urfiyya 'amma). Memorize these four.
4
Apply the rule to a given proposition
Given a universal negative conditional, determine if its modality is one of the four listed. If yes, the converted proposition will have the same form. If not, either it converts to a different form or does not convert at all.
For example, a temporal (waqtiyya) universal negative does not convert as itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في السوالب من الشرطية ] هذا إذا كانت المتصلة من الموجبات، فأما إذا كانت من السوالب فإنها لا تنعكس إلا إذا كانت كلية؛ والكلية لا تنعكس كنفسها كما وجهة إلا إذا كانت ضرورية، أو دائمة، أو مشروطة عامة، أو عرفية عامة. فإن ما عداها لا تنعكس كنفسها بل لا تنعكس أصلا
Chapter: [On the negatives of conditionals] This is when the connected [conditional] is affirmative. But if it is from the negatives, then it does not convert unless it is universal. And the universal does not convert as itself in every aspect except if it is necessary, or perpetual, or common conditional, or common customary. For what is other than these does not convert as itself, rather it does not convert at all.
Source pages p. 478

Commentary
This passage lays down the foundational rule for the conversion (in'ikas) of negative conditional propositions (al-sawalib min al-shartiyya). The author specifies that conversion only applies to universal negatives, and even then, only certain modal types retain their form: necessary (daruriya), perpetual (da'ima), common conditional (mashruta 'amma), and common customary ('urfiyya 'amma). All other types either convert as a different type or not at all. This sets the stage for detailed proofs.

Key Takeaway

Proofs for Converting Necessary Negative Conditionals
Author provides proof that necessary negative conditionals convert as themselves.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reproduce and distinguish the two proofs (in'ikas and indimam) for the conversion of necessary negative conditionals and explain why perpetual negatives follow the same pattern.

Core Idea
Necessary negative conditionals convert as themselves; two proofs ensure certainty.

In this passage, the author demonstrates that a necessary negative conditional remains valid when its antecedent and consequent are swapped and the negation is maintained. Two distinct proof techniques are presented: conversion (in'ikas) and composition (indimam). Both rely on reductio ad absurdum and are foundational for immediate inference in modal logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition
Start with the necessary negative conditional: 'Necessarily, not at all, if A then B.' Recognize that 'not at all' indicates a universal negation.
This is the proposition whose converse we want to prove.
2
State the converse to be proved
The claim is that the converse 'Necessarily, not at all, if B then A' also holds. If false, its contradictory would be true.
The contradictory of a necessary negative is a possible affirmative.
3
Follow the proof by conversion (in'ikas)
Assume the converse is false, so 'Possibly if B then A' (partial conversion). Combine with the original: 'Necessarily not at all if A then B' and 'Possibly if B then A' lead to a situation where A implies not B and B possibly implies A, which is contradictory (since A implies not B implies not A).
This contradiction proves the converse must be true.
4
Follow the proof by composition (indimam)
Assume the converse is false, so 'Possibly if B then A' and 'Not at all if A then B' (the original). Then from 'Possibly if B then A' we have a case where B and A hold; but 'Not at all if A then B' means in that case A implies not B, so B implies not B, a contradiction.
This is a more direct reductio.
5
Extend to perpetual negatives
The author notes that the same reasoning applies to perpetual negative conditionals (where the necessity is dropped entirely). The only difference is the modal force; the logical structure is identical.
Perpetual negatives have the same conversion properties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الضرورية فيقال بالضرورة ليس البتة إذا كان أب» كان ج د بالضرورة ليس البتة إذا كان «ج د» كان «أب» وإلا لصدق نقيضه وهو قولنا قد يكون إذا كان ج د كان «أب» فينعكس عكســا جزئيا وهو قولنا قد يكون إذا كان (أ ب) كان (ج) د بالإمكان العام، وقد كان بالضرورة ليس البتة إذا كان (أ ب) كان ( دا، هذا خلف؛ هذا بطريق الانعكاس. وأما بطريق الانضمام فيقال قد يكون إذا كان ج د كان «أب»، وليس البتة إذا كان «أب) كان (ج) د ، فقد يكون إذا كان جدا فلا يكون ج د، هذا خلف؛ [٢] وعلى هذا في الدائمة؛ إذ لا فرق بينهما إلا في الضرورة.
As for the necessary [negative], it is said: necessarily, not at all, if 'ab' then 'j d'; [then] necessarily, not at all, if 'j d' then 'ab'; otherwise its contradictory would be true, which is our saying: it may be that if 'j d' then 'ab', so it converts partially, which is our saying: it may be that if 'ab' then 'j d' with general possibility, whereas [originally] it was necessarily, not at all, if 'ab' then 'j d' – this is a contradiction. This is by way of conversion. As for by way of composition, it is said: it may be that if 'j d' then 'ab', and not at all if 'ab' then 'j d', so it may be that if 'j d' then 'ab' and not 'j d' – this is a contradiction. [2] And likewise for the perpetual; there is no difference between them except in necessity.
Source pages p. 478

Commentary
This passage gives two proofs for the conversion of necessary negative conditionals. The first is by conversion (in'ikas): assume the original is 'necessarily not at all if A then B'. If the converse were false, its contradictory would hold: 'possibly if B then A' (a partial conversion). That combined with the original yields a contradiction. The second proof is by composition (indimam): assume the converse is false, so 'possibly if B then A' and 'not at all if A then B' – this leads to a situation where B implies A and A does not imply B, which is contradictory. The author then states that perpetual negatives follow the same pattern, differing only in necessity.

Key Terms
انعكاس
انضمام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Necessarily not at all if B then A
B) Possibly if B then A
C) Possibly if A then B
D) Necessarily if A then B

Key Takeaway
Necessary negative conditionals convert as themselves, and two independent proofs (in'ikas and indimam) confirm this using reductio ad absurdum.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Conversion of Common Conditional and Customary Negatives
Author proves common conditional and customary negatives convert as themselves.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to perform and verify the conversion of common conditional and common customary negative propositions, preserving the duration restriction.

Core Idea
Common conditional negatives convert as themselves because the duration restriction is essential to their truth.

So far, we have learned how necessary propositions convert. Now, the author extends the proof to conditional propositions that are restricted by the duration of their antecedent—common conditionals (mashruta 'amma) and common customary ('urfiyya 'amma). The proof shows that their negatives convert into themselves, with the same 'as long as' (ma dama) clause preserved. This is a subtle but crucial extension that deepens our understanding of immediate inference in conditional logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition structure
The common conditional negative consists of a modal (necessity or custom), a negated conditional ('not at all if A then B'), and a duration restriction ('as long as A'). Notice the 'as long as' restricts the truth to the duration of the antecedent.
The 'common' refers to the duration restriction, not the modal.
2
State the conjecture
The author claims that the proposition converts into itself: the same modal and negation, but with the antecedent and consequent swapped, and the duration condition now on the new antecedent (the original consequent).
This is a self-conversion: the converse has the same form.
3
Execute the proof by contradiction
Assume the converse is false. Then its contradictory—a partial converse with possibility—would be true. Show that this leads to a logical absurdity (contradiction), thereby proving the original conversion valid.
The proof mirrors that for necessary propositions but adds the duration clause.
4
Extend to common customary
Recognize that the same reasoning applies when the modal is replaced by customary custom ('urf), since the proof does not rely on the specific nature of necessity but only on the logical structure.
The only difference is the modality: absolute necessity vs. customary occurrence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المشروطة العامة فيقال بالضرورة ليس البتة إذا كان «أب» كان «ج د ما دام «أب»، بالضرورة ليس البتة إذا كان ( د) كان أب» ما دام جد وإلا لصدق نقيضه وهو قولنا قد يكون إذا كان ج (د) كان «أب» ما دام (ج) د بالإمكان العام فينعكس عكسا جزئيا؛ وذلك يفضي إلى المحال، [٤] وعلى هذا في العرفية العامة؛ إذ لا فرق بينهما إلا في الضرورة.
As for the common conditional [negative], it is said: necessarily, not at all, if 'ab' then 'j d' as long as 'ab'; [then] necessarily, not at all, if 'j d' then 'ab' as long as 'j d'; otherwise its contradictory would be true, which is our saying: it may be that if 'j d' then 'ab' as long as 'j d' with general possibility, so it converts partially; and that leads to absurdity. [4] And likewise for the common customary; there is no difference between them except in necessity.
Source pages p. 478

Commentary
This passage proves that common conditional negatives (where the antecedent's duration restricts the conditional) convert as themselves, with a similar duration condition. The proof mirrors that for necessary, but with the addition of 'as long as' (ma dama) clauses. If the converse were false, its contradictory (a partial conversion with possibility) would hold, leading to contradiction. The common customary follows the same pattern, differing only in the use of custom ('urf) instead of absolute necessity.

Three Opinions on Special Conditional Negative Conversion
Author presents three opinions on conversion of special conditional negatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the three scholarly opinions on the conversion of special conditional negatives, using the logical variables in the passage.

Core Idea
Three opinions: universal conversion, partial conversion, or no conversion due to material variability.

This passage presents a dialectical situation where logicians disagree on whether special conditional negatives convert. Understanding these positions helps you grapple with how temporal restrictions affect logical inference. We will parse each opinion and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Locate the three opinions
Find the three opinions in the text: the markers [1], [ب], [ت] and the sixth point [٦]. Opinion one says it converts universally; opinion two says it converts always partially; opinion three says it does not convert at all due to material differences.
The enumeration marks likely correspond to letters alif, ba, ta.
2
Analyse the example for the second opinion
The text uses variables 'ab' and 'j d' to illustrate the partial conversion. Write out the original proposition: 'necessarily, not at all, if ab then jd not always but as long as ab'. Then its conversion: 'necessarily, not at all, if jd then ab as long as jd always partially, not always fully'.
The conversion is partial because it holds only as long as the condition lasts.
3
Understand the third opinion's basis
The third opinion denies conversion because 'conversion varies with the materials (maddah)'. This means the truth of the conversion depends on the specific content, so no universal rule applies.
This highlights the role of subject matter in logical inference.
4
Relate to special customary
The same three opinions apply to 'special customary' (al-urfiyya al-khassa), another type of conditional. This shows the pattern holds across similar proposition types.

Why Temporal Negative Conditionals Do Not Convert
Author denies conversion for temporal and extended negatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a temporal negative conditional and explain why it cannot be validly converted.

Core Idea
Temporal negatives lock the conditional to a time, breaking the symmetry required for conversion.

In earlier lessons, we saw that some conditionals convert. However, temporal negatives resist conversion because the truth of the original does not guarantee the truth of the converse. In this passage, the author demonstrates that the consequent may depend on the antecedent, making the converted statement false. Let's unpack the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Notice that the temporal negative conditional is of the form: 'Necessarily, if A then not B, at a specific time t.' This means that during time t, whenever A occurs, B does not occur.
The time restriction is crucial—without it, the conditional would be absolute.
2
Attempt to convert formally
Write the converted form: 'Necessarily, if B then not A, at the same time t.' Is this statement always true? The author says no.
Conversion swaps the antecedent and consequent while keeping the negation and temporal restriction.
3
Find the counterexample
The author points out that some consequents cannot exist without the antecedent. If B implies A, then when B is true, A must be true, so 'not A' would be false. Hence the conversion fails.
The original conditional can still be true if A occurs without B, but the converse would require B to occur without A, which is impossible under the dependency.
4
Generalize to extended negatives
The same reasoning applies to the extended (muntashira) and other more specific propositions: as restrictions increase, conversion becomes impossible.
The author lists a hierarchy from extended to general possible, showing that only the most general propositions might convert.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القضايا الباقية فإنها لا تكون منعكسة لامتناع العكس في الأخص منها: وهي الوقتية فإنه يصدق أن يقال بالضرورة ليس البتة إذا كان «أب» كان (ج) د لا دائما بل وقت كذا، ولا يصدق أن يقال بالضرورة ليس البتة إذا كان (ج) د كان «أب» لا دائما بل وقت كذا، فإن من التوالي ما لا يمكن أن يوجد بدون المقدم البتة فلا يمكن أن يسلب عنه أصلا. [۸] وعلى هذا في المنتشرة التي هي أخص من الوجودية اللا دائمة / التي هي أخص من الوجودية اللا [١٣٧] ضرورية التي هي أخص من الممكنة الخاصة التي هي أخص من المطلقة العامة التي هي أخص من الممكنة العامة.
As for the remaining propositions, they are not convertable because conversion is impossible in the more specific among them: the temporal [negative], for it is true to say: necessarily, not at all, if 'ab' then 'j d' not always but at time such-and-such, but it is not true to say: necessarily, not at all, if 'j d' then 'ab' not always but at time such-and-such, for among consequents are those that cannot exist without the antecedent at all, so it cannot be negated from it at all. [8] And likewise for the extended [proposition] which is more specific than the existential non-perpetual / which is more specific than the existential non-necessary which is more specific than the special possible which is more specific than the absolute general which is more specific than the general possible.
Source pages p. 479

Commentary
This passage explains why temporal (waqtiyya) and extended (muntashira) negative conditionals do not convert. The temporal is a proposition that holds only at a specific time, not always. Its conversion would require the consequent to imply the antecedent at that same time, but often the consequent can exist without the antecedent, making the conversion false. The extended proposition is even more specific, and the author provides a hierarchy of specificity: extended < existential non-perpetual < existential non-necessary < special possible < absolute general < general possible. This hierarchy shows that as propositions become more specific, conversion becomes less possible.

Key Terms

منتشرة (muntashira)

Key Takeaway
Temporal and extended negative conditionals do not convert because the consequent may depend on the antecedent, breaking the symmetry required for valid conversion.

Refutation of Invalid Conversions for Some Negatives
Author refutes claims that temporal, existential, and particular negatives convert.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which negative proposition types convert and which do not, and explain the asymmetry that prevents conversion.

Core Idea
Conversion of negatives is not universal; temporal, existential, and particular negatives fail due to asymmetric relations.

Some logicians mistakenly claimed that temporal, extended, existential non-perpetual, and special possible negatives convert as themselves. The author refutes each, showing that conversion fails because the relation between terms is not symmetric. Understanding these restrictions is crucial for valid immediate inference in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claims being refuted
Find the four types of negatives that some scholars claimed convert: temporal negative (saliba waqtiyya), extended negative (saliba muntashira), existential non-perpetual negative (saliba wujudiyya la da'ima), and special possible negative (mumkina khassa).
These are treated in both categorical and conditional propositions.
2
Understand the refutation for temporal and extended
The author argues that it is possible to negate something from something else but not the reverse. For example, 'A is not B' does not imply 'B is not A' when A and B have a subject-predicate or antecedent-consequent asymmetry.
This applies whether the terms are subjects/predicates or antecedents/consequents.
3
Understand the refutation for existential and possible
These negatives include an affirmative component (e.g., 'existential non-perpetual' means something is not always existent, but occasionally is). Conversion would require the negation/affirmation to remain, but the temporal condition changes.
The condition of 'as long as' or 'always' cannot be preserved in conversion.
4
Understand the refutation for particular negatives
A particular negative (e.g., 'some A is not B') does not convert because the universal (B) does not depend on the particular (A). The existence of the universal can be prior to the particular in negation and affirmation, so conversion would be invalid.
The author gives an incomplete example about animal existence, likely illustrating that 'not if animal then something else' does not convert.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعن البعض منهم أن السالبة الوقتية والمنتشرة كل واحدة منهما في الحمليّات والشرطيات تنعكس كنفسها كما وجهة والسالبة الوجودية اللا دائمة، والممكنة الخاصة تنعكس كل واحدة منهما انعكاس الموجبة لاشتمالها عليها؛ والكل باطل . أما في الوقتية والمنتشرة فإنه يمكن أن يسلب الشيء عن غيره، ولا يمكن أن يسلب ذلك الغير عنه موضوعًا كان ذلك الشيء أو مقدما. وأما في الوجودية والممكنة الخاصة فلاشتراط بقاء السلب والإيجاب بحاله. وأما السالبة الجزئية فإنها لا تنعكس لما أن وجود العام يمكن أن لا يكون مقدما لوجود الحاصل في السلب والإيجاب، ولا يمكن أن لا يكون وجود الخاص مقدما لوجود العام فيقال مثلا قد لا يكون إذا كان الحيوان موجودا
And some [scholars] [say] that the temporal negative and the extended negative, each of them in categoricals and conditionals, convert as themselves in every aspect; and the existential non-perpetual negative, and the special possible negative, each of them converts like the affirmative because it includes it. But all this is false. As for the temporal and extended, it is possible to negate something from something else, but not possible to negate that other from it, whether the thing is a subject or an antecedent. As for the existential and special possible, [it is false] because of the condition that the negation and affirmation remain as they are. As for the particular negative, it does not convert because the existence of the universal can be not an antecedent for the existence of the resultant in negation and affirmation, and it is not possible that the existence of the particular is not an antecedent for the existence of the universal; so it is said, for example: it may be that if animal exists [then...]
Source pages p. 479

Commentary
This passage rebuts the opinion that temporal, extended, existential non-perpetual, and special possible negatives convert as themselves. The author argues that conversion fails because the relationship between antecedent and consequent is not symmetric. For temporal and extended, negation of one from the other is possible in one direction but not the reverse. For existential and special possible, the modal condition (e.g., 'as long as' or 'always') must remain. For particular negatives, conversion is impossible because the universal does not necessarily depend on the particular. The example of animal existence is cut off, but it likely intends to show that 'not if animal then something' does not convert.

Key Terms
سالبة وقتية
سالبة منتشرة
سالبة وجودية لا دائمة
ممكنة خاصة
سالبة جزئية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it is not universal
B) Because the universal may not be antecedent for the particular
C) Because it is not necessary
D) Because it is temporal

Key Takeaway
Conversion of negative propositions is not always valid; temporal, extended, existential, possible, and particular negatives have specific failures due to asymmetry and modal constraints.

Minority View on Conversion of Negative Conditionals
Author reports a minority view on conversion of negative conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the minority view on conversion of negative conditionals and explain why material dependence prevents uniform conversion.

Core Idea
Conversion rules for conditionals are not universal; they depend on the material content.

In this short passage, the author reports a minority position within Islamic logic that challenges the assumption that all conditionals can be converted. The key insight is that conversion (al-'aks) does not follow a fixed rule; it varies according to the 'matter' (al-mawadda) of the proposition. This lesson unpacks that claim and shows why it matters for rigorous reasoning, especially in disciplines where conditional propositions are common.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minority claim
The passage states that some scholars say negative conditionals do not convert at all. Find the phrase that states this claim.
The claim is explicitly 'la tan'akisu aslan'.
2
Understand the justification
The justification is that conversion differs according to the matter (al-mawadda). This means that the truth of the converted conditional depends on the specific content and modality, not on a fixed formal rule.
Matter refers to the actual content or modal status of the propositions.
3
Recognize the scope
The view is applied specifically to 'al-'urfiyya al-khassa' (the special customary conditional). This is a conditional where the implication holds customarily but not necessarily always.
This is a modal conditional type, adding nuance to the conversion debate.
4
Connect to conversion rules
Recall that conversion in categorical propositions (e.g., from 'Every A is B' to 'Some B is A') follows uniform rules. Here, the minority view highlights that conditionals are more complex and material-dependent.
This challenges the assumption that logical rules are purely formal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من قال إنها لا تنعكس أصلا لما أن العكس يختلف باختلاف المواد، وعلى هذا في العرفية الخاصة.
And some of them said that they do not convert at all because conversion differs according to the matter, and upon this in the special customary [proposition].
Source pages p. 479

Commentary
This passage reports a minority position within the logical tradition regarding the conversion ('aks) of negative conditional propositions. The claim is that these propositions do not convert at all. The justification given is that conversion (i.e., swapping antecedent and consequent while preserving truth) varies depending on the 'matter' (al-mawadda), meaning the specific content or modality of the proposition. Since no uniform rule holds, some scholars argue that negative conditionals are simply not convertible. The author adds that this view applies specifically to what is called the 'special customary' (al-'urfiyya al-khassa) type of conditional, a term referring to a conditional where the implication holds as long as the antecedent exists customarily but not necessarily always. This is a technical nuance in the broader theory of immediate inferences in conditional logic.

Key Terms
العكس (al-'aks): Conversion: swapping the antecedent and consequent (or subject and predicate in categorical) while preserving truth value under certain conditions. [Note: In later texts, 'aks is sometimes restricted to categorical propositions; here it applies to conditionals.]
العرفية الخاصة (al-'urfiyya al-khassa): A conditional proposition where the implication holds as long as the antecedent exists according to customary duration, not necessarily always. [Note: This is a specific modal conditional type; its classification varies among logicians.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the minority view reported in the passage, why do negative conditionals not convert?
A) Because the consequent is always false
B) Because conversion varies according to the matter
C) Because they are impossible
D) Because the antecedent is always true

Key Takeaway
The conversion of conditionals is not a purely formal operation; it depends on the specific content and modality of the propositions involved.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Non-Convertible Negative Conditionals: Temporal and Specific Propositions
Author enumerates types of non-convertible negative conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which types of negative conditional propositions are not convertible and explain why using the temporal conditional as an example.

Core Idea
Not all negative conditionals convert; temporal and other specific ones do not.

In this passage, the author explains that certain specific negative conditional propositions do not obey conversion. He illustrates with the temporal conditional and provides a hierarchy of more specific propositions. Understanding these exceptions is crucial for correctly applying conversion in modal logic.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the claim about non-conversion
The author states that the remaining propositions (beyond universal affirmative) are not convertible, especially the more specific ones.
2
Examine the temporal (al-waqtiyya) example
The temporal conditional says 'necessarily, never when A is B then J is D, but only at a certain time'. The converse is false.
3
Understand the reason
Some consequents cannot exist without the antecedent at all, so the negation cannot be transferred to the converse.
This is because the negation is temporally restricted.
4
Trace the hierarchy of specific propositions
The author lists a chain: widespread < non-permanent existential < non-necessary existential < special possible < general absolute < general possible. Each is more specific than the next.
The more specific a proposition, the less likely it converts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القضايا الباقية فإنها لا تكون منعكسة لامتناع العكس في الأخص منها: وهي الوقتية فإنه يصدق أن يقال بالضرورة ليس البتة إذا كان «أب» كان (ج) د لا دائما بل وقت كذا، ولا يصدق أن يقال بالضرورة ليس البتة إذا كان (ج) د كان «أب» لا دائما بل وقت كذا، فإن من التوالي ما لا يمكن أن يوجد بدون المقدم البتة فلا يمكن أن يسلب عنه أصلا. وعلى هذا في المنتشرة التي هي أخص من الوجودية اللا دائمة التي هي أخص من الوجودية اللا ضرورية التي هي أخص من الممكنة الخاصة التي هي أخص من المطلقة العامة التي هي أخص من الممكنة العامة.
As for the remaining propositions, they are not convertible due to the impossibility of conversion in the more specific of them: namely the temporal [proposition], for it is true to say 'necessarily, not at all when A is B then J is D, not always but at such a time', and it is not true to say 'necessarily, not at all when J is D then A is B, not always but at such a time', because some consequents cannot exist without the antecedent at all, so it cannot be negated from it absolutely. And likewise for the widespread [proposition] which is more specific than the non-permanent existential, which is more specific than the non-necessary existential, which is more specific than the special possible, which is more specific than the general absolute, which is more specific than the general possible.
Source pages p. 479

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of types of negative conditional propositions that do not convert. The author explains that the more specific types among the remaining (i.e., besides the universal affirmative conditional) are not convertible. The first example is the 'temporal' (al-waqtiyya) conditional: it is true that necessarily, never when A is B then J is D, but not always, only at a certain time; but the converse is false. The reason given is that some consequents cannot exist without the antecedent at all, so the negation cannot be transferred. This applies similarly to a hierarchy of more specific propositions: the 'widespread' (al-muntashira), which is more specific than the 'non-permanent existential' (al-wujudiyya la da'ima), which is more specific than the 'non-necessary existential' (al-wujudiyya la daruriyya), which is more specific than the 'special possible' (al-mumkina al-khassa), which is more specific than the 'general absolute' (al-mutlaqa al-'amma), which is more specific than the 'general possible' (al-mumkina al-'amma). This hierarchical list shows the author's classification of modal conditional propositions.

Key Terms
الوقتية
المنتشرة
الممكنة الخاصة

Key Takeaway
Temporal and other more specific negative conditionals do not convert because the consequent may be inherent to the antecedent's time or nature.

Refutation of Conversion Claims for Negative Conditionals
Author refutes scholars who claim temporal and other negative conditionals convert.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to refute false claims about conversion of negative conditionals using the author's counterexamples and the principle that conversion must hold in all materials.

Core Idea
Conversion of negative conditionals must hold for all materials; particular negatives never convert, as shown by the animal-man example.

In this passage, the author takes on a group of logicians who mistakenly claim that certain negative conditionals convert like affirmatives. He systematically dismantles each claim, showing why temporal, widespread, existential, and special possible negatives do not convert, and why particular negatives never do. Through clear counterexamples, he establishes the proper rules of conversion for negative conditionals, crucial for sound inference in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opposing claim
Locate the initial assertion: some scholars claim that temporal and widespread negatives, as well as non-permanent existential and special possible negatives, convert as themselves. Note that they claim this for both categoricals and conditionals.
The author introduces the claim with 'وعن البعض منهم أن السالبة الوقتية والمنتشرة...'
2
Understand the refutation strategy
The author declares all such claims false ('والكل باطل') and then addresses each type separately. Notice the pattern: for each type, he provides a specific logical reason why conversion fails, often based on asymmetry or persistence conditions.
The refutation relies on testing whether the converse holds in all cases, not just some.
3
Examine the counterexample for temporal/widespread negatives
The author states: 'it is possible to negate a thing from another, but it is not possible to negate that other from it'. Apply this to a concrete example: 'animal' and 'man'. You can say 'it may be that when animal exists, man does not exist', but not 'when man exists, animal does not exist'.
This asymmetry shows conversion fails because the original is true but the converse is false.
4
Apply the universal condition for conversion
The author argues that for the particular negative (e.g., 'it may be that when animal exists, man does not exist'), conversion fails because the universal (animal) can exist without the particular (man), but the particular cannot exist without the universal. This is a general principle: for conversion to hold, the relationship must be symmetric, which it is not.
The author emphasizes that conversion must be valid in all materials and all respects, not just in some specific instances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعن البعض منهم أن السالبة الوقتية والمنتشرة كل واحدة منهما في الحمليّات والشرطيات تنعكس كنفسها كما وجهة والسالبة الوجودية اللا دائمة، والممكنة الخاصة تنعكس كل واحدة منهما انعكاس الموجبة لاشتمالها عليها؛ والكل باطل. أما في الوقتية والمنتشرة فإنه يمكن أن يسلب الشيء عن غيره، ولا يمكن أن يسلب ذلك الغير عنه موضوعًا كان ذلك الشيء أو مقدما. وأما في الوجودية والممكنة الخاصة فلاشتراط بقاء السلب والإيجاب بحاله. وأما السالبة الجزئية فإنها لا تنعكس لما أن وجود العام يمكن أن لا يكون مقدما لوجود الحاصل في السلب والإيجاب، ولا يمكن أن لا يكون وجود الخاص مقدما لوجود العام فيقال مثلا قد لا يكون إذا كان الحيوان موجودا كان الإنسان موجودًا. ولا يقال قد لا يكون إذا كان الإنسان موجودا كان الحيوان موجودا. وأما قول من قال: «إنها إذا كانت خاصة من المشروطتين والعرفيتين كانت منعكسة كنفسها»؛ فذلك من جملة ما لا يلزم منه أن تكون السالبة الجزئية منعكسة وإن كان متحققا فإن ما يكون منعكسة فهو منعكس في جميع المواد وبجميع الجهات؛ والسالبة الجزئية لا تكون كذلك: هذا هو الكلام في اللزومية.
And from some of them [it is claimed] that the temporal and widespread negative [proposition], each of them in categoricals and conditionals, convert as themselves, and likewise the non-permanent existential negative, and the special possible convert each of them as the conversion of the affirmative because they include it; but all is false. As for the temporal and widespread, it is possible to negate a thing from another, but it is not possible to negate that other from it, whether that thing is a subject or an antecedent. As for the existential and special possible, because of the condition of the persistence of negation and affirmation as they are. As for the particular negative, it does not convert because the existence of the universal can be not a premise for the existence of the obtained in negation and affirmation, but the existence of the particular cannot be not a premise for the existence of the universal; for example, it is said: 'it may be that when animal exists, man does not exist', but it is not said 'it may be that when man exists, animal does not exist'. And as for the saying of one who said: 'If they are particular from the two conditional [types] and the customary, they convert as themselves' – that is among what does not entail that the particular negative is convertible, even if it is actualized, for what is convertible is convertible in all matters and in all respects, and the particular negative is not so. This is the discussion concerning the implicative [conditionals].
Source pages p. 479

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of a group of scholars who claimed that certain negative conditionals do convert. The author systematically rebuts these claims. First, he states the opposing position: that temporal and widespread negatives, as well as non-permanent existential and special possible negatives, convert like affirmatives because they 'include' the affirmative. The author declares all this false. He then provides reasons: For temporal and widespread, it is possible to negate a thing from another, but not vice versa, so conversion fails. For existential and special possible, the condition requires persistence of negation/affirmation in the same state, which does not hold upon conversion. Next, he addresses the particular negative (al-saliba al-juz'iyya), arguing it does not convert because the universal can exist without the particular, but not vice versa, giving the example: 'it may be that when animal exists, man does not exist' is true, but 'when man exists, animal does not exist' is false. Finally, he refutes a specific objection: even if some particular negatives from the two conditional types (the conditional and the customary) convert in some cases, conversion must hold for all materials and respects, which is not true for particular negatives. The passage concludes by noting this discussion concerns implicative (luzumiyya) conditionals.

Key Terms
السالبة الجزئية
اللزومية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Negative conditionals do not convert unless the negation is symmetrical, and particular negatives never convert because the universal can exist without the particular.

Conversion of Accidental Conditionals
Conversion rules for accidental conditionals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether an accidental conditional can be converted and produce the correct converse when valid.

Core Idea
An accidental universal conditional converts only if its consequent is possible; an accidental particular never converts.

Accidental conditionals are propositions where the connection between antecedent and consequent is not necessary but coincidental. The logician must know when such a conditional may be validly converted and when it may not. The passage gives clear rules for universal and particular accidental conditionals, with examples that illustrate the pitfalls of invalid conversion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the quantity
Determine whether the accidental conditional is universal ('never when A then B') or particular ('it may be that when A then not B'). The rule differs for each.
Universal accidental conditionals are like 'ليس البتة إذا كان الإنسان موجودًا كان الخلاء موجودًا'.
2
Check the consequent's modality
If the conditional is universal, examine whether the consequent is logically possible (mumkin) or impossible (mumtani'). Only if it is possible can the conversion be considered.
If the consequent is impossible, as in the void example, conversion is impossible.
3
Apply the conversion
If the universal accidental conditional has a possible consequent, produce the converse by swapping the antecedent and consequent while keeping the same universal negation. For particular accidentals, do not convert at all.
The converse must maintain the same modal status.
4
Test with examples
Take the author's examples and apply the rules: 'Never when man exists does void exist' has an impossible consequent → no conversion. 'It may be that when a man is stationary, he is not sleeping' is particular → no conversion.
Invent your own examples from Islamic sciences to practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما في الاتفاقية فالكلية منها تنعكس إذا كان التالي فيها ممكنًا، فإنه إذا كان ممتنعا كان العكس ممتنعًا فيقال مثلا ليس البتة إذا كان الإنسان موجودًا كان الخلاء موجودًا، ولا يمكن أن يقال ليس البتة إذا كان الخلاء موجودًا كان الإنسان موجودًا. وأما الجزئية منها فإنها لا تنعكس أيضًا، فإنه يقال قد لا يكون إذا كان الإنسان ساكنا كان نائما، ولا يقال قد لا يكون إذا كان الإنسان نائمًا كان ساكنا.
As for the accidental [conditional], the universal of it converts if the consequent in it is possible; for if it is impossible, then the conversion is impossible. For example, it is said: 'Never when man exists does void exist', and it cannot be said: 'Never when void exists does man exist'. And as for the particular of it, it also does not convert, for it is said: 'It may be that when man is stationary, he is not sleeping', and it is not said: 'It may be that when man is sleeping, he is not stationary'.
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage specifies the conversion rules for accidental (ittifaqiyya) conditionals, which are conditionals where the connection between antecedent and consequent is not necessary but accidental. For universal accidental conditionals, conversion is valid only if the consequent is possible (mumkin). If the consequent is impossible (mumtani'), conversion fails. The author gives an example: 'Never when man exists does void exist' is true because void is impossible, but its converse 'Never when void exists does man exist' is false because void cannot exist at all. For particular accidental conditionals, they do not convert at all. Example: 'It may be that when a man is stationary, he is not sleeping' is true, but its converse 'It may be that when a man is sleeping, he is not stationary' is false because when sleeping, he must be stationary? Actually, the example shows that the converse does not necessarily hold.

Key Terms
