Conversion Applies Only to Conjunctive Conditionals
Author delimits the scope of conversion rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish conjunctive from disjunctive conditionals and explain why conversion is valid only for the former.

Core Idea
Conversion requires a natural order between parts; disjunctives have no such order.

In the previous lessons, you learned about conversion ('aks) of categorical propositions. Now the author expands that discussion to conditional propositions. But not all conditionals can be converted. This passage draws a crucial boundary: only conjunctive conditionals — those of the 'if...then' form — admit logical conversion. Disjunctive conditionals — 'either...or' — are excluded because their parts are symmetric. Understanding this distinction prevents a common error in logical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of conditionals
Conjunctive conditionals (mutasilat) express a connection where the antecedent naturally precedes the consequent (e.g., 'If the sun rises, then it is day'). Disjunctive conditionals (munfasilat) express alternatives where the parts are interchangeable (e.g., 'Either Zayd is in the house or he is in the market').
2
Recall the definition of conversion
Conversion means swapping the parts of a proposition while preserving truth. For a conditional, conversion would mean taking 'If P then Q' and deriving 'If Q then P'.
Conversion is an immediate inference.
3
Check the natural order condition
In a conjunctive, there is a natural order: the antecedent is prior to the consequent. In a disjunctive, there is no such order; the parts are symmetric. Therefore, converting a disjunctive merely yields a verbal rearrangement, not a logical consequence.
The author says: 'المقدم فيها لا يتميز عن التالي بالطبع' — the antecedent is not distinguished from the consequent by nature.
4
Apply the rule to a concrete example
Take the conjunctive 'If it rains, the ground is wet'. Its converse 'If the ground is wet, it rained' is not always true (other causes). But conversion in logic requires truth preservation, so this conversion fails. Yet the author's point is about the possibility of conversion as a formal operation: in conjunctives, the order matters; in disjunctives, it does not, so conversion is meaningless.
The author is not saying all conjunctive conversions are valid, but that conversion is only applicable to conjunctives as a formal operation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الكلام في العكس وما يتعلق به إنه من جملة ما يكون مختصا بالمتصلات؛ إذ المنفصلات بمعزل عنه، وقد كان المقدم فيها لا يتميز عن التالي بالطبع على ما عرف فلا يمكن أن يحكم بالعكس إلا بحسب اللفظ. وذلك لا يعتبر فيما نحن فيه وهو بيان القواعد العقلية التي هي من الشرائط في تحقيق العلوم الإلهية وغيرها.
Then the discussion of conversion and what pertains to it is that it is among what is specific to conjunctives; for disjunctives are separate from it, and the antecedent in them is not distinguished from the consequent by nature, as is known, so conversion cannot be judged except verbally. And that is not considered in what we are discussing, which is the exposition of intellectual rules that are among the conditions for attaining the divine sciences and others.
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage draws a clear boundary: the rules of conversion ('aks) apply only to conjunctive (mutasil) conditionals (i.e., if-then statements). Disjunctive (munfasil) conditionals (either-or statements) are excluded because in a disjunction, the antecedent and consequent are not naturally distinguished; they are symmetric. Therefore, any 'conversion' of a disjunctive is merely verbal, not a genuine logical operation. The author emphasizes that such verbal conversion is irrelevant to the present work, which aims to establish the rational rules necessary for the divine sciences (al-'ulum al-ilahiyya) and other sciences.

Key Terms
المتصلات
المنفصلات
العكس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because the antecedent is not distinguished from the consequent by nature
C) Because they are too complex
D) Because they are not used in divine sciences

Key Takeaway
Conversion ('aks) is a logical operation that only applies to conjunctive conditionals, where the parts have a natural order, not to symmetric disjunctives.

The General Definition of Contraposition: Beyond Categoricals
Author defines contraposition and argues for a general definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to articulate the general definition of contraposition that applies to both categorical and conditional propositions.

Core Idea
Contraposition is not limited to categoricals; its true definition must capture both categorical and conditional forms.

In this passage, the author points out that the commonly given definition of contraposition — exchanging the contradictory of the subject and predicate — only works for categorical propositions. Since contraposition also applies to conditionals (like 'If A then B'), a more general definition is needed. The author then provides that definition in terms of 'judged-upon' and 'judged-by'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the standard definition
Find the majority definition of contraposition in the Arabic. What operation does it describe? It says: take the contradictory of the predicate and make it the subject, and take the contradictory of the subject and make it the predicate.
2
Recognize its limitation
The author says this definition is 'مخصوص بالحمليات' (specific to categoricals). Why? Because it only makes sense for subject-predicate propositions.
3
Understand the need for generality
Contraposition also applies to conditionals (shartiyyat). For example, in 'If A then B', its contrapositive is 'If not B then not A'. A general definition must cover both types.
4
Extract the general definition
The author's revised definition: exchange the opposite (muqabil) of the 'judged-by' (al-mahkum bihi) to become the 'judged-upon' (al-mahkum 'alayh), and exchange the opposite of the judged-upon to become the judged-by. Note: 'judged-by' is the predicate/consequent; 'judged-upon' is the subject/antecedent.
5
Compare the two definitions
How does the general definition encompass both categoricals and conditionals? In categoricals, 'judged-by' is the predicate and 'judged-upon' is the subject. In conditionals, 'judged-by' is the consequent and 'judged-upon' is the antecedent. The same operation works for both.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثالث من المباحث فإنه في عكس النقيض: أما عكس النقيض فالجمهور منهم عرفوه بهذه العبارة، وهو أن يؤخذ ما يناقض المحمول فيجعل موضوعا، وما يناقض الموضوع فيجعل محمولا، وهذا هو التعريف المخصوص بالحمليّات. وعكس النقيض لا يكون مخصوصا بها. فالأولى أن يعرف بتعريف يكون عاما يعم الحمليَّات والشرطيَّات؛ وذلك أن يقال بتبديل مقابل المحكوم به بالسلب والإيجاب محكوما عليه، ومقابل المحكوم عليه محكوما به.
And as for the third of the investigations, it is concerning contraposition: as for contraposition, the majority among them defined it with this expression: that one takes what contradicts the predicate and makes it a subject, and what contradicts the subject and makes it a predicate, and this is the definition specific to categoricals. But contraposition is not specific to them. So it is better to define it with a definition that is general, encompassing categoricals and conditionals; and that is to say: by exchanging the opposite of the judged-by [i.e., predicate/consequent] in terms of negation and affirmation to be the judged-upon [subject/antecedent], and the opposite of the judged-upon to be the judged-by.
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage introduces the third topic of the section: contraposition ('aks al-naqid). The author notes that the majority of logicians define contraposition as taking the contradictory of the predicate and making it the subject, and the contradictory of the subject and making it the predicate. However, this definition is specific to categorical propositions (hamliyyat). Since contraposition applies also to conditionals (shartiyyat), a more general definition is needed. The author proposes a general definition: exchange the opposite (muqabil) of the 'judged-by' (al-mahkum bihi) — which in categoricals is the predicate, in conditionals the consequent — in terms of negation and affirmation, making it the 'judged-upon' (al-mahkum 'alayh) — subject/antecedent; and exchange the opposite of the judged-upon to become the judged-by. This general formulation captures contraposition for both types of propositions.

Key Terms
عكس النقيض
الحمليات
الشرطيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the problem with the majority definition of contraposition?
A) It is incorrect
B) It is specific to categoricals
C) It is too general
D) It applies only to conditionals

Key Takeaway
Contraposition must be defined generally to cover both categorical and conditional propositions, using the exchange of the opposite of the judged-by and judged-upon.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the categorical proposition 'Every intoxicant is haram', write its contrapositive using the standard definition (i.e., exchange contradictory of predicate and subject).

Conversion Rules for Conditional Propositions
Author explains conversion rules for conditional propositions (luzumiyya and ittifaqiyya).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the conversion rules for implicative and coincidental conditionals and determine whether a given conditional converts validly.

Core Idea
Conversion of conditionals is not uniform—it depends on type and modality.

This passage extends the concept of conversion (al-ʿaks) from categorical propositions to conditional propositions. Since conditionals can be implicative (necessary connection) or coincidental (accidental connection), their conversion behaves differently. Understanding these rules is crucial for valid immediate inference in theological and legal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of conditional
Determine whether the conditional is luzumiyya (the antecedent necessarily implies the consequent) or ittifaqiyya (merely accidental). For example, 'If man exists, then animal exists' is luzumiyya; 'If man exists, then void exists' is ittifaqiyya (with impossible consequent).
The type affects conversion validity.
2
Check the quantifier and modality
Note whether it is universal or particular. For ittifaqiyya, also check the modality of the consequent (possible or impossible). The passage states that a universal ittifaqiyya converts only if the consequent is possible.
Modality is key for coincidentals.
3
Apply the conversion rule
For luzumiyya: a particular negative does not convert universally. For ittifaqiyya universal: convert only if consequent is possible; particular does not convert. Example: 'Not always if man exists, then void exists' does not convert because void's existence is impossible.
Conversion must hold in all materials.
4
Test with examples
Use the examples given: 'If man exists, then animal exists' (luzumiyya) – does it convert? 'Not if void exists, then man exists' ittifaqiyya – no. 'If man is sitting, then he is sleeping' – particular does not convert.
Verify understanding by applying rules.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كان الإنسان موجودًا . ولا يقال قد لا يكون إذا كان الإنسان موجودا كان الحيوان موجودا. وأما قول من قال: «إنها إذا كانت خاصة من المشروطتين والعرفيتين كانت منعكسة كنفسها»؛ فذلك من جملة ما لا يلزم منه أن تكون السالبة الجزئية منعكسة وإن كان متحققا فإن ما يكون منعكسا فهو منعكس في جميع المواد وبجميع الجهات؛ والسالبة الجزئية لا تكون كذلك: هذا هو الكلام في اللزومية. وأما في الاتفاقية فالكلية منها تنعكس إذا كان التالي فيها ممكنًا ، فإنه إذا كان ممتنعا كان العكس ممتنعًا فيقال مثلا ليس البتة إذا كان الإنسان موجودًا كان الخلاء موجودًا، ولا يمكن أن يقال ليس البتة إذا كان الخلاء موجودًا كان الإنسان موجودًا . وأما الجزئية منها فإنها لا تنعكس أيضًا، فإنه يقال قد لا يكون إذا كان الإنسان ساكنا كان نائما، ولا يقال قد لا يكون إذا كان الإنسان نائمًا كان ساكنا. ثم الكلام في العكس وما يتعلق به إنه من جملة ما يكون مختصا بالمتصلات؛ إذ المنفصلات بمعزل عنه ، وقد كان المقدم فيها لا يتميز عن التالي بالطبع على ما عرف فلا يمكن أن يحكم بالعكس إلا بحسب اللفظ. وذلك لا يعتبر فيما نحن فيه وهو بيان القواعد العقلية التي هي من الشرائط في تحقيق العلوم الإلهية وغيرها.
It is not said 'it may not be that if man exists, then animal exists.' As for the statement of one who said: 'If it [the proposition] is particular, from the conditional and the customary, then it converts like itself'—that is among the things from which it does not follow that the particular negative is converted, even if it is actual. For whatever is converted, it is converted in all matters and in all respects; and the particular negative is not like that. This is the discussion concerning the implicative [conditional]. As for the coincidental [conditional], the universal of it converts if the consequent is possible. For if it is impossible, then the conversion is impossible. For example, it is said: 'Absolutely not, if man exists, then void exists'; and it cannot be said: 'Absolutely not, if void exists, then man exists.' As for the particular of it, it also does not convert. For it is said: 'It may not be that if man is sitting, then he is sleeping'; and it is not said: 'It may not be that if man is sleeping, then he is sitting.' Then the discussion concerning conversion and what pertains to it—it is among the things that are specific to conditionals, since disjunctives are apart from it. For the antecedent in them is not distinguished from the consequent by nature, as is known, so judgment of conversion can only be according to wording. And that is not considered in our subject, which is the exposition of the rational rules that are among the conditions in the verification of the divine sciences and others.
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage discusses the rules of conversion (al-ʿaks) for conditional propositions (al-sharṭiyyāt). The author begins with examples from implicative conditionals (al-luzūmiyya) and coincidental conditionals (al-ittifāqiyya). For implicative conditionals, a universal affirmative conditional does not convert to a particular affirmative? Actually, the text states that a particular negative conditional does not convert universally. In the example, if man exists, animal exists is true, but one cannot say 'it may not be that if man exists, animal exists' (that would be false). Then the author refutes a claim that particular conditionals convert like themselves. He argues that conversion must hold in all materials and respects, which particular negatives do not satisfy. For coincidental conditionals, the universal converts only if the consequent is possible; if impossible, conversion is impossible. Example: 'Absolutely not, if man exists, then void exists' does not convert because void does not exist necessarily. For particular coincidentals, 'it may not be that if man is sitting, then he is sleeping' does not convert to 'it may not be that if man is sleeping, then he is sitting' because sleeping does not imply sitting. Finally, the author notes that conversion is specific to conditionals (al-muttaṣilāt), not disjunctives (al-munfaṣilāt), because in disjunctives the antecedent is not naturally prior to the consequent, so conversion is merely verbal and not rational. This discussion is part of establishing rational rules for the divine sciences.

The Correct Definition of Contraposition
Author defines contraposition and discusses the condition of preserving quality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the majority definition from the author's general definition of contraposition, and identify why preserving negation/affirmation is a necessary condition.

Core Idea
Contraposition must be defined generally to include conditionals, and the quality must remain unchanged.

In this lesson, we learn the definition of contraposition (ʿaks al-naqīḍ). The author points out that the majority definition only applies to categoricals, but a better definition encompasses conditionals. He insists that preserving the negation/affirmation (quality) is a condition for valid contraposition.

Guided Path
1
Find the two definitions
Locate the majority definition and the author's general definition in the Arabic passage. The majority definition uses 'subject' and 'predicate' (specific to categoricals), while the author's uses 'what is judged upon' and 'what is judged by' (general for both categoricals and conditionals).
The author's general definition is: 'by exchanging the opposite of what is judged upon with negation and affirmation as the thing judged upon, and the opposite of what is judged upon with negation and affirmation as the thing judged by, while preserving the negation and affirmation as they are.'
2
Compare their scope
Notice that the majority definition uses 'subject' and 'predicate' (only categoricals), while the author's uses 'what is judged upon' (maḥkūm ʿalayhi) and 'what is judged by' (maḥkūm bihi) which applies to conditionals as well.
This shows the author's concern for a definition that covers all proposition types.
3
Identify the condition
The author explicitly states that preserving negation and affirmation (quality) is a condition for contraposition, following Ibn Sīnā. Find the sentence: 'The truth is that it is a condition.'
This condition distinguishes contraposition from other transformations like conversion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثالث من المباحث فإنه في عكس النقيض : أما عكس النقيض فالجمهور منهم عرفوه بهذه العبارة، وهو أن يؤخذ ما يناقض المحمول فيجعل موضوعا، وما يناقض الموضوع فيجعل محمولا، وهذا هو التعريف المخصوص بالحمليّات. وعكس النقيض لا يكون مخصوصا بها. فالأولى أن يعرف بتعريف يكون عاما يعم الحمليَّات والشرطيَّات؛ وذلك أن يقال بتبديل مقابل المحكوم به بالسلب والإيجاب محكوما عليه، ومقابل المحكوم عليه بالسلب والإيجاب محكوما به مع بقاء السلب والإيجاب بحاله، والصدق والكذب كذلك. ثم المتقدمون اختلفوا في شرطية بقاء السلب والإيجاب، والجمهور منهم ذهبوا إلى أنه ليس بشرط ; والحق فيه أن يكون شرطًا، وهذا هو مذهب الشيخ لما أن العكس عنده بل في الحقيقية هو التصرف في جميع أجزاء القضية. ولا يبعد أن يقال في كل واحد من هذين التعريفين إنه لا يصح : [1] أما في الأول فيقال الشيخ كان يقول في عكس نقيض السالبة الكلية إن قولنا لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر يلزمه بعض ما ليس بحجر إنسان فيجعل عين الموضوع محمولاً». لكنا نقول : إن قوله يلزمه كذا لا يدل على أن ذلك عكس النقيض؛ وإن كان عكس النقيض من جملة ما يلزمه بل يدل على أنه يلزمه لما أنه من لوازم عكس النقيض؛ وهو قولنا بعض ما ليس بحجر ليس بلا إنسان. ولأن الشيخ عرفه بالتعريف المذكور؛ وذلك لا يكون عكس النقيض بالنسبة إلى هذا التعريف فكيف يعتقده عكس النقيض. [ب] وأما في الثاني فيقال إنه كان يقول في عكس نقيض الموجبة الكلية إذا قلنا كل ج ب لزمه أن كل ما ليس (ب) ليس (ج)، وهذه القضية إن كانت سالبة كانت مخالفة للأصل في الكيفية ; وإن كانت موجبة معدولة من جملة ما لأ برهان عليه». فإن اللازم لصدق النقيض لا يناقض الأصل فنقول بل من جملة ما عليه من البراهين التي مرَّ ذكرها، وقد أقام عليه الشيخ من البراهين أيضًا فقال لما صدق قولنا كل جب صدق قولنا كل ما ليس «ب» ليس (ج) وإلا لصدق نقيضه وهو قولنا ليس بعض ما ليس «ب» ليس «ج»؛ أي بعض ما ليس بج) فينعكس بعض (ج) ليس «ب»؛ وقد كان كل ج ب ، هذا خلف.
As for the third topic, it is concerning contraposition. As for contraposition, the majority of them defined it with this expression: that what contradicts the predicate is taken and made the subject, and what contradicts the subject is taken and made the predicate. And this is the definition specific to categoricals. But contraposition is not specific to them. So it is better to define it with a definition that is general, encompassing categoricals and conditionals. That is to say: by exchanging the opposite of what is judged upon with negation and affirmation as the thing judged upon, and the opposite of what is judged upon with negation and affirmation as the thing judged by, while preserving the negation and affirmation as they are, and truth and falsity likewise. Then the earlier scholars differed on whether preserving negation and affirmation is a condition. The majority of them held that it is not a condition. But the truth is that it is a condition, and this is the doctrine of the Shaykh, because conversion in his view—rather in reality—is the manipulation of all parts of the proposition. And it is not unlikely that one might say of each of these two definitions that it is invalid: [1] As for the first, it is said that the Shaykh used to say concerning the contraposition of the universal negative: 'Our statement 'No human is a stone' entails 'Some non-stone is a human'—so he makes the very subject the predicate.' But we say: His statement 'it entails such' does not prove that that is contraposition, even if contraposition is among what it entails. Rather, it proves that it entails it because it is among the necessary concomitants of contraposition—and that is our statement 'Some non-stone is not a human.' And because the Shaykh defined it with the mentioned definition; and that is not contraposition with respect to this definition, so how can he believe it to be contraposition? [B] As for the second, it is said that he used to say concerning the contraposition of the universal affirmative: If we say 'Every J is B', it entails that 'Every non-B is non-J'—and this proposition, if it is negative, contradicts the original in quality; but if it is affirmative with privative terms, it is among things for which there is no proof. For what is necessary for the truth of the contrapositive does not contradict the original. So we say: Rather, it is among what there are proofs for, which have been mentioned. And the Shaykh also established proofs for it, saying: When our statement 'Every J is B' is true, our statement 'Every non-B is non-J' is true, otherwise its contradictory would be true—which is our statement 'Not some non-B is non-J', i.e., 'Some non-B is J'. Then it converts to 'Some J is non-B', but it was 'Every J is B'—this is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage defines contraposition (ʿaks al-naqīḍ) and discusses its proper formulation. The majority defined it specifically for categorical propositions: take the contradictory of the predicate and make it the subject, and take the contradictory of the subject and make it the predicate. However, the author argues this definition is too narrow—contraposition applies to conditionals as well. A better definition is: exchange the opposite (with respect to negation and affirmation) of the thing judged upon to become the thing judged by, and the opposite of the thing judged by to become the thing judged upon, while preserving the original negation/affirmation and truth value. The author then notes that earlier scholars disagreed on whether preserving the quality (negation/affirmation) is a condition. The majority said no, but the correct view is that it is a condition, following the Shaykh (Ibn Sīnā), because conversion in reality is manipulation of all parts of the proposition. The author then addresses two objections against the Shaykh's definitions: First, regarding universal negative contraposition (e.g., 'No human is a stone' entails 'Some non-stone is human'), critics claimed the Shaykh made the very subject the predicate. The author defends that this is a necessary concomitant of contraposition, not the contraposition itself—the actual contrapositive is 'Some non-stone is not a human', which preserves the negative quality. Second, regarding universal affirmative contraposition (e.g., 'Every J is B' to 'Every non-B is non-J'), critics argued either it changes quality (if taken as negative) or uses privative terms (if taken as affirmative) and lacks proof. The author counters that the Shaykh provided a proof by contradiction: if the contrapositive were false, then 'Some non-B is J' would be true, which converts to 'Some J is non-B', contradicting 'Every J is B'. Thus the contrapositive is valid, and the condition of preserving negation/affirmation is satisfied by using privative terms.

السلب والإيجاب
الشيخ

C) The truth value must be inverted.
D) Only universal propositions can undergo contraposition.

Key Takeaway
The definition of contraposition must be general and include the condition of preserving quality.

Proof and Defense of Contraposition
Author refutes objections against Ibn Sina's contraposition formulations and provides a proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Sīnā's proof by contradiction for the universal affirmative contrapositive, and refute the objection that the universal negative contrapositive changes the subject.

Core Idea
Ibn Sīnā defends contraposition using a reductio proof that hinges on preserving quality.

After defining contraposition, the author addresses two objections raised against Ibn Sīnā's formulations. The first objection concerns universal negatives; the second concerns universal affirmatives. The author shows that both objections are based on misunderstandings and provides Ibn Sīnā's proof for the second.

Guided Path
1
Restate the first objection
The critic claims that from 'No human is a stone', Ibn Sīnā concluded 'Some non-stone is human', which seems to make the subject the predicate. Find this in the Arabic: 'يقال في عكس نقيض السالبة الكلية إن قولنا لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر يلزمه بعض ما ليس بحجر إنسان فيجعل عين الموضوع محمولاً'.
The objection is that the subject 'human' becomes the predicate, breaking the definition.
2
Understand the defense
The author argues that the actual contrapositive is 'Some non-stone is not a human', which preserves the negative quality. The criticised statement 'Some non-stone is human' is only a necessary concomitant of the contrapositive, not the contrapositive itself.
The author says: 'ولأن الشيخ عرفه بالتعريف المذكور؛ وذلك لا يكون عكس النقيض بالنسبة إلى هذا التعريف فكيف يعتقده عكس النقيض'.
3
Restate the second objection
For 'Every J is B', the critic says the contrapositive 'Every non-B is non-J' either changes quality (if taken as negative) or uses privative terms without proof. Find this near: 'وإن كانت موجبة معدولة من جملة ما لأ برهان عليه'.
The critic demands proof that the contrapositive holds.
4
Trace Ibn Sīnā's proof
Assume the contrapositive is false: 'Not every non-B is non-J' → 'Some non-B is J' (by immediate inference). This converts to 'Some J is non-B', which contradicts the original 'Every J is B'. Therefore the contrapositive must be true.
This proof by contradiction shows the contrapositive is valid without changing quality, because 'Every non-B is non-J' is affirmative with privative terms.

The author then notes that earlier scholars disagreed on whether preserving the quality (negation/affirmation) is a condition. The majority said no, but the correct view is that it is a condition, following the Shaykh (Ibn Sīnā), because conversion in reality is manipulation of all parts of the proposition. The author then addresses two objections against the Shaykh's definitions: First, regarding universal negative contraposition (e.g., 'No human is a stone' entails 'Some non-stone is human'), critics claimed the Shaykh made the very subject the predicate. The author defends that this is a necessary concomitant of contraposition, not the contraposition itself—the actual contrapositive is 'Some non-stone is not a human', which preserves the negative quality. Second, regarding universal affirmative contraposition (e.g., 'Every J is B' to 'Every non-B is non-J'), critics argued either it changes quality (if taken as negative) or uses privative terms (if taken as affirmative) and lacks proof. The author counters that the Shaykh provided a proof by contradiction: if the contrapositive were false, then 'Some non-B is J' would be true, which converts to 'Some J is non-B', contradicting 'Every J is B'. Thus the contrapositive is valid, and the condition of preserving negation/affirmation is satisfied by using privative terms.

Key Terms
عكس النقيض
السلب والإيجاب
الشيخ

Contrapositive: Definition and Essential Conditions
Critical analysis of two definitions of contrapositive and defense of the condition of quality preservation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct definition of the contrapositive of a categorical proposition and evaluate whether the condition of preserving quality is necessary.

Core Idea
The contrapositive is not just any conversion with negation; it must preserve truth and quality.

In this passage, the author engages with early logicians (including Ibn Sina) on how to define the contrapositive ('aks al-naqid). He critiques two definitions and argues for a necessary condition: the contrapositive must preserve the affirmation or negation (quality) of the original proposition. Moreover, he insists that the proposition must be perpetual (daima) for the contrapositive to hold. Let's walk through his analysis to understand what a valid contrapositive is and why it matters for Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions under critique
The author examines two definitions: the first for universal negative (e.g., 'No man is stone') claims that the contrapositive is 'Some non-stone is man'. The second for universal affirmative (e.g., 'Every J is B') claims it is 'Everything not-B is not-J'.
2
Understand the author's objection to the first definition
The author argues that the first definition confuses a concomitant (lāzim) with the actual contrapositive. The real contrapositive of 'No man is stone' is 'Some non-stone is not non-man'.
3
Examine the objection to the second definition and the condition of quality preservation
The second definition either changes the quality (negative vs. original affirmative) or uses an affirmative with negated subject (ma'dula) without proof. The author insists that the contrapositive must preserve the quality (salb/ījāb) of the original proposition.
The author supports this with a proof by contradiction and explains that only perpetual propositions yield a valid contrapositive.
4
Grasp the requirement of perpetual truth
The author argues that absolute propositions do not have a valid contrapositive; only perpetual (daima) propositions do, because categorical propositions are equivalent to conditionals where the non-existence of the predicate necessitates non-existence of the subject.
This is derived from the equivalence: 'Every J is B' is like 'If something is J, it is B'.

ثم المتقدمون اختلفوا في شرطية بقاء السلب والإيجاب، والجمهور منهم ذهبوا إلى أنه ليس بشرط؛ والحق فيه أن يكون شرطًا، وهذا هو مذهب الشيخ لما أن العكس عنده بل في الحقيقية هو التصرف في جميع أجزاء القضية. ولا يبعد أن يقال في كل واحد من هذين التعريفين إنه لا يصح: [1] أما في الأول فيقال الشيخ كان يقول في عكس نقيض السالبة الكلية إن قولنا لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر يلزمه بعض ما ليس بحجر إنسان فيجعل عين الموضوع محمولاً. لكنا نقول: إن قوله يلزمه كذا لا يدل على أن ذلك عكس النقيض؛ وإن كان عكس النقيض من جملة ما يلزمه بل يدل على أنه يلزمه لما أنه من لوازم عكس النقيض؛ وهو قولنا بعض ما ليس بحجر ليس بلا إنسان. ولأن الشيخ عرفه بالتعريف المذكور؛ وذلك لا يكون عكس النقيض بالنسبة إلى هذا التعريف فكيف يعتقده عكس النقيض. [ب] وأما في الثاني فيقال إنه كان يقول في عكس نقيض الموجبة الكلية إذا قلنا كل ج ب لزمه أن كل ما ليس (ب) ليس (ج)، وهذه القضية إن كانت سالبة كانت مخالفة للأصل في الكيفية؛ وإن كانت موجبة معدولة من جملة ما لا برهان عليه. فإن اللازم لصدق النقيض لا يناقض الأصل فنقول بل من جملة ما عليه من البراهين التي مرَّ ذكرها، وقد أقام عليه الشيخ من البراهين أيضًا فقال لما صدق قولنا كل ج ب صدق قولنا كل ما ليس (ب) ليس (ج) وإلا لصدق نقيضه وهو قولنا ليس بعض ما ليس (ب) ليس (ج)؛ أي بعض ما ليس (ب) ج فينعكس بعض (ج) ليس (ب)؛ وقد كان كل ج ب، هذا خلف. ولقائل أن يقول إنه ليس بخلف والمطلقة لا يناقضها من نوعها شيء، لكنا نقول مراده من قوله كل ج ب لا يكون قضية مطلقة بل يكون دائمة، ولا حاجة إلى التصريح باللفظ لما يعرض له لبيان عكس النقيض، وقد كان من المعلوم أن المطلقة لا تكون منعكسة عكس النقيض بما مرَّ ذكره في العكس المستوي وهو أن من الأشياء ما يصح حمله على غيره بالإطلاق العام ولا يمكن سلب ذلك الغير عن كل ما عداه بالإطلاق العام. ولأن الحملية في قوة الشرطية، والشرطية المتصلة لا يمكن تحققها إلا وأن يكون انتفاء المقدم من لوازم انتفاء التالي دائما فكذلك الحملية التي مرَّ ذكرها لا يمكن تحققها إلا وأن يكون انتفاء الموضوع من لوازم انتفاء المحمول. وأما من قال بعدم الشرطية فقد عرفه بتعريف آخر وقال إنه عبارة عن جعل بعض المحمول موضوعًا وعن الموضوع محمولاً مع مخالفته في الكيفية للأصل، أو نقيضه محمولا مع موافقة في الكيف إياه. والمراد بالموضوع والمحمول هما أو ما يناسبهما وهو المقدم والتالي. وعلى هذا لا يرد شيء من النقيض على رأيهم في عكس النقيض لكن يخرج منه بعض ما اعتبرناه من القضايا في عكس النقيض؛ وحينئذ يجب تعريفه بقولنا حمل الموضوع أو نقيضه على نقيض المحمول مع موافقته للأصل في الصدق. وعلى هذا يتناول جميع الأقسام المعتبرة في عكس النقيض؛ ومن تأمل في مثل هذه التعريفات فقد علم بأنه لا يكون كما ينبغي. وكيف وقد كان إدخال كلمة أو في التعريفات من جملة ما يحل التعريف. اللهم إلا أن يقال المراد منه كذا وكذا لكن ذلك المعنى الذي هو المراد يمكن أن يعبر بعبارة لا تحتاج إلى التأويل، فيجب أن يعبر بتلك العبارة.
Then the earlier scholars differed about the conditionality of the preservation of negation and affirmation, and the majority of them went to it not being a condition; and the truth in it is that it is a condition, and this is the school of the Shaykh because according to him the converse, rather in reality, is the manipulation of all parts of the proposition. And it is not unlikely that it be said regarding each of these two definitions that it is not valid: [1] As for the first, it is said that the Shaykh used to say regarding the contrapositive of the universal negative that our statement 'Nothing of man is stone' necessarily implies 'Some of what is not stone is man', so he makes the very subject a predicate. But we say: his statement 'it necessarily implies such-and-such' does not indicate that that is the contrapositive; even if the contrapositive is among what it implies, rather it indicates that it is implied because it is among the concomitants of the contrapositive; and it is our statement 'Some of what is not stone is not not-man'. And because the Shaykh defined it by the mentioned definition; and that is not the contrapositive relative to this definition, so how can he believe it is the contrapositive? [B] And as for the second, it is said that he used to say regarding the contrapositive of the universal affirmative that if we say 'Every J is B' it necessarily implies that 'Everything that is not B is not J', and this proposition, if it is negative, conflicts with the original in quality; and if it is affirmative but with a negated subject (ma'dula), it is among those for which there is no proof. For the necessary consequent of the truth of the contradictory does not contradict the original; so we say that it is among the proofs that have been mentioned, and the Shaykh also advanced proofs for it, saying: when our statement 'Every J is B' is true, our statement 'Everything that is not B is not J' is true, otherwise its contradictory would be true, which is our statement 'Not some of what is not B is not J', i.e., some of what is not B is J; then the converse would be 'Some J is not B', but it was 'Every J is B', this is a contradiction. And someone may object that this is not a contradiction, and an absolute proposition is not contradicted by its kind; but we say that he meant by his statement 'Every J is B' not an absolute proposition but a perpetual one, and there is no need to mention the word explicitly for what occurs to it for explaining the contrapositive, and it was known that the absolute proposition does not have a contrapositive as previously mentioned in the direct converse, namely that among things are those whose predication on another is valid in absolute generality but the negation of that other from everything else in absolute generality is not possible. And because the categorical proposition is equivalent to a conditional, and a conditional conjunctive cannot be realized unless the non-existence of the antecedent is a necessary concomitant of the non-existence of the consequent always, similarly the categorical proposition mentioned cannot be realized unless the non-existence of the subject is a necessary concomitant of the non-existence of the predicate. And as for those who said without the condition, he defined it with another definition and said that it is the making of some of the predicate a subject and of the subject a predicate while opposing it in quality to the original, or its contradictory as a predicate while agreeing with it in quality. And what is meant by subject and predicate are they or what corresponds to them, namely the antecedent and consequent. And on this, nothing of the contradictory applies to their view regarding the contrapositive, but some of the propositions we considered in the contrapositive are excluded. Then it is necessary to define it as: the predication of the subject or its contradictory of the contradictory of the predicate while agreeing with the original in truth. And on this it includes all the divisions considered in the contrapositive. And whoever reflects on such definitions knows that it is not as it should be. And how can it be, since the inclusion of the word 'or' in definitions is among the things that invalidate the definition. Unless it is said that what is meant is such-and-such, but that meaning which is intended can be expressed by an expression that does not require interpretation, so one must express it by that expression.
Source pages p. 481

Commentary
This passage discusses the definition and condition of 'aks al-naqid (contrapositive) in categorical propositions. The author begins by noting a disagreement among earlier logicians: whether preserving the quality (negation/affirmation) of the original proposition is a condition for the contrapositive. The majority say it is not a condition, but the correct view (according to the 'Shaykh', i.e., Ibn Sina) is that it is a condition, because the converse (al-'aks) in reality involves manipulation of all parts of the proposition. The author then critiques two definitions attributed to the Shaykh. 

The first definition concerns the contrapositive of a universal negative. Example: 'No man is stone' is said to imply 'Some non-stone is man', which makes the original subject ('man') a predicate. The author argues that this implication does not prove that it is the contrapositive; it is merely a concomitant of the actual contrapositive, which would be 'Some non-stone is not non-man'. The Shaykh's own definition does not yield this as the contrapositive, so it is inconsistent. 

The second definition concerns the universal affirmative 'Every J is B', which is said to imply 'Everything not-B is not-J'. The author points out a problem: if the implied proposition is negative, it differs in quality from the original; if it is affirmative but with a negated subject (ma'dula), it lacks a clear proof. The Shaykh then provides a proof by contradiction: suppose 'Every J is B' is true but 'Everything not-B is not-J' is false, then its contradictory 'Some not-B is J' is true. By converse, 'Some J is not-B', which contradicts 'Every J is B'. The author then addresses an objection that this proof fails for absolute propositions. He responds that the Shaykh meant perpetual (daima) propositions, not absolute ones, and explains why absolute propositions do not have a valid contrapositive, using the concept of universal predication. He further argues that categorical propositions are equivalent to conditionals: just as in a conditional conjunctive, the non-existence of the consequent necessarily implies non-existence of the antecedent, so in a categorical, the non-existence of the predicate necessarily implies non-existence of the subject. 

Finally, the author presents the definition of those who reject the condition: they define contrapositive as making part of the predicate the subject and the subject the predicate, either with opposite quality (original negative becomes affirmative, etc.) or with the contradictory of the subject as predicate preserving quality. This definition avoids some problems but excludes some valid cases. The author then proposes his own definition: 'the predication of the subject or its contradictory of the contradictory of the predicate while agreeing with the original in truth.' This covers all cases. He criticizes the use of 'or' in definitions as causing ambiguity and insists that the intended meaning should be expressed directly without need for interpretation.

شرطية
السالبة الكلية
الموجبة الكلية
الحملية
المعدولة

D) It relies on a perpetual proposition.

Key Takeaway
The contrapositive ('aks al-naqid) is an immediate inference that swaps and negates subject and predicate while preserving truth and quality, and it is only valid for perpetual (not absolute) categorical propositions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

For the universal affirmative proposition 'Every act of usury (riba) is forbidden (haram),' construct its valid contrapositive according to the author's conditions. Check whether the contrapositive preserves quality and is perpetual.

Defining 'Aks al-Naqid and Critiquing Definitions
Author critiques definitions of contraposition and proposes his own.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct definition of 'aks al-naqid and evaluate alternative definitions based on the author's criteria.

Core Idea
The definition of contraposition must avoid ambiguity; using 'or' invalidates it.

In this passage, the author presents and critiques various definitions of contraposition ('aks al-naqid), ultimately offering his own. He emphasizes that definitions must be precise and that ambiguity, such as the disjunctive word 'or', undermines them. We will walk through the author's reasoning to understand the requirements for a correct definition.

Guided Path
1
Understand the initial objection and response
The author addresses an objection that contraposition does not hold for absolute propositions. He responds that the relevant propositions are perpetual, not absolute, so contraposition applies.
2
Identify the alternative definition
Some scholars define contraposition as swapping subject and predicate with a change in quality or using the contradictory. This is an alternative that the author examines.
3
Criticize the alternative definition
The author shows that this alternative excludes some cases and introduces ambiguity, especially with the word 'or'.
4
Construct the correct definition
The author proposes: predicating the subject or its contradictory of the contradictory of the predicate while preserving truth. This covers all legitimate cases.
5
Recognize the criterion for definitions
Definitions must not use ambiguous terms like 'or' without clarifying interpretation; otherwise, they are invalid.

One could object that it is not contraposition and the absolute proposition cannot be contradicted by anything of its kind, but we say: his intention in his statement 'Every ج is ب' is not an absolute proposition but a perpetual one, and there is no need to explicitly state the wording for what occurs to it for explaining contraposition, and it was known that the absolute is not inverted by contraposition as mentioned earlier in simple inversion, namely that among things there are those that can be predicated of others with absolute generality but that which is other than it cannot be negated of everything else with absolute generality. But these earlier folks undertook that not to clarify correctness and said: When the subject of a proposition is according to essence and its predicate likewise, it is called absolute essential; and when its subject is according to external existence and its predicate likewise, it is called absolute existential. And they classified thereafter accordingly, considering modification, affirmation, and negation in the two extremes due to the difference of judgment according to the difference of these considerations in the inversions. They said that the subject of a universal affirmative cannot be devoid of being either affirmed, modified, or negated, and in all suppositions the predicate is in one of three ways. Thus nine types of propositions are obtained, and each of them has four divisions: because the two extremes are either according to essence, or according to external existence, or the subject according to essence and the predicate according to external existence, or the opposite; thus thirty-six types of propositions are obtained, differing in concept, even if some of them are mutually concomitant and some differ by generality and particularity. Four of them are the divisions of the affirmed extremes, namely 'ج' and 'ب' in our statement 'Every ج is ب', one of which is that the subject and predicate are existential, which is the absolute existential; the second is that the subject is existential and the predicate essential, which is called 'subject-existential'; and it differs from the first because the predicate in the first is the actual ب, while in the second it is the quiddity; namely it being such that if it entered existence it would be ب; but even though they differ they are mutually concomitant because if it is true that every ج exists externally

Commentary
This passage discusses the definition of 'aks al-naqid (contraposition) and the classification of propositions necessary for its correct application. The author begins by addressing an objection: some might claim that the contrapositive of a universal affirmative is not a true contraposition because an absolute proposition cannot be contradicted by anything of its kind. The author responds that the proposition 'Every ج is ب' in the context of contraposition should be understood as perpetual (daima), not absolute, so the standard rules of contraposition apply. He notes that absolute propositions do not yield valid contrapositives, as mentioned earlier in the discussion of simple inversion ('aks mustawi). The author then explains that categorical propositions are equivalent to conditionals, and just as a conjunctive conditional requires that the negation of the antecedent necessarily follows from the negation of the consequent, so too in categoricals the negation of the subject must follow from the negation of the predicate for contraposition to hold. Next, the author reports an alternative definition by those who deny the conditional interpretation: they define contraposition as making part of the predicate a subject and the subject a predicate, either with a change in quality (i.e., contrapositive is negative if original is affirmative) or with the contradictory of the predicate while maintaining the same quality. This definition, however, excludes some cases. The author then proposes his own definition: predicating the subject or its contradictory of the contradictory of the predicate while agreeing with the original in truth. This covers all legitimate cases. He criticizes definitions that use the word 'or' as ambiguous, unless the intention is clear and can be expressed without interpretation. The rest of the passage turns to the classification of propositions needed for contraposition. The author notes that earlier scholars like the 'mutawwal' (perhaps al-Taftazani?) divided propositions into essential (haqiqi) and existential (khariji) based on whether the subject and predicate are considered according to essence or external existence. They further divided by considering whether the subject and predicate are affirmed, modified (ma'dul), or negated (salb). This yields nine types of universal affirmative propositions (subject in three forms × predicate in three forms). Each of these nine can be in four modes depending on the combination of essential/existential for subject and predicate: (1) both essential, (2) both existential, (3) subject essential and predicate existential, (4) subject existential and predicate essential. Thus 9×4=36 distinct types of propositions, differing in meaning even if some are logically equivalent. The author then begins to explain the four divisions for the case where both extremes are affirmed, specifically for 'Every ج is ب': the first is both existential (kharijiyya mutlaqa), the second is subject existential and predicate essential (kharijiyyat al-mawdu'), etc. He notes that although these differ, they are mutually entailing because if it is true that every ج exists externally... (the text cuts off). The commentary follows the author's order, explaining each point without adding outside information.

A correct definition of contraposition must cover all cases without ambiguity; authors should avoid disjunctive terms like 'or' unless the meaning is clarified.

Classification of Propositions for Contraposition
Author presents classification of propositions for contraposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify propositions according to the essential/existential distinction and the three forms of subject/predicate (affirmed, modified, negated), and compute the total number of types relevant to contraposition.

Core Idea
Thirty-six types of propositions arise from combining 3 subject forms × 3 predicate forms × 4 modes of essential/existential.

The author explains how earlier scholars categorized propositions to handle contraposition correctly. They divided propositions based on whether the subject and predicate are considered according to essence (haqiqi) or external existence (khariji), and further by whether each term is affirmed, modified, or negated. This leads to a grid of 36 types. We will go through the steps to understand this classification.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two primary divisions: essential and existential
Propositions can be considered either according to essence (الحقيقية المطلقة) of the terms, regardless of existence, or according to external existence (الخارجية المطلقة).
2
Recognize the three forms of each term
The subject and predicate can each be in three forms: affirmed (محصل), modified (معدول), or negated (سلب). For example, in a universal affirmative 'Every ج is ب', the subject 'ج' might be 'not-A' (modified) or 'non-A' (negated).
3
Compute the base types
Multiply the three forms of the subject by the three forms of the predicate: 3 × 3 = 9 base types of universal affirmative propositions.
4
Consider the four modes of essential/existential
For each base type, there are four ways to assign essential/existential to subject and predicate: both essential, both existential, subject essential/predicate existential, subject existential/predicate essential.
5
Calculate the total number
Multiply the 9 base types by the 4 modes: 9 × 4 = 36 distinct types of propositions. These differ in meaning, even if some are logically equivalent.

Source Text

لذلك لا على تبيين الاستقامة فقالوا : إذا كان موضوع القضية بحسب الحقيقة ومحمولها كذلك سميت بالحقيقية المطلقة، وإذا كان موضوعها بحسب الوجود في الخارج ومحمولها كذلك سميت بالخارجية المطلقة. وقسَّموا من One could object that it is not contraposition and the absolute proposition cannot be contradicted by anything of its kind, but we say: his intention in his statement 'Every ج is ب' is not an absolute proposition but a perpetual one, and there is no need to explicitly state the wording for what occurs to it for explaining contraposition, and it was known that the absolute is not inverted by contraposition as mentioned earlier in simple inversion, namely that among things there are those that can be predicated of others with absolute generality but that which is other than it cannot be negated of everything else with absolute generality. And because the categorical is equivalent to the conditional, and the conjunctive conditional cannot be realized unless the negation of the antecedent is among the necessary consequences of the negation of the consequent always, so likewise the categorical mentioned cannot be realized unless the negation of the subject is among the necessary consequences of the negation of the predicate. And as for one who denies the conditional, he defined it with another definition and said it is making some of the predicate a subject and the subject a predicate while differing from the original in quality, or its contradictory as the predicate while agreeing with it in quality. And what is meant by subject and predicate are they or what corresponds to them, namely the antecedent and consequent. And according to this, nothing of the contradictory applies to their view of contraposition, but some of what we considered from propositions in contraposition is excluded. Then it must be defined by our saying 'predicating the subject or its contradictory of the contradictory of the predicate while agreeing with the original in truth.' And upon this it encompasses all the divisions considered in contraposition. And whoever contemplates such definitions knows it is not as it should be. How so, since inserting the word 'or' in definitions is among the things that invalidate the definition. Unless it is said that the intended meaning is such and such, but that intended meaning can be expressed with an expression that does not require interpretation, so it must be expressed with that expression. In summary, the discussion on contraposition and what pertains to it is varied; the majority of early and later scholars, when they came to know the divisions of propositions according to restriction, neglect, modification, and affirmation, did not undertake to explain them in detail in contraposition. But these earlier folks undertook that not to clarify correctness and said: When the subject of a proposition is according to essence and its predicate likewise, it is called absolute essential; and when its subject is according to external existence and its predicate likewise, it is called absolute existential. And they classified thereafter accordingly, considering modification, affirmation, and negation in the two extremes due to the difference of judgment according to the difference of these considerations in the inversions. They said that the subject of a universal affirmative cannot be devoid of being either affirmed, modified, or negated, and in all suppositions the predicate is in one of three ways. Thus nine types of propositions are obtained, and each of them has four divisions: because the two extremes are either according to essence, or according to external existence, or the subject according to essence and the predicate according to external existence, or the opposite; thus thirty-six types of propositions are obtained, differing in concept, even if some of them are mutually concomitant and some differ by generality and particularity. Four of them are the divisions of the affirmed extremes, namely 'ج' and 'ب' in our statement 'Every ج is ب', one of which is that the subject and predicate are existential, which is the absolute existential; the second is that the subject is existential and the predicate essential, which is called 'subject-existential'; and it differs from the first because the predicate in the first is the actual ب, while in the second it is the quiddity; namely it being such that if it entered existence it would be ب; but even though they differ they are mutually concomitant because if it is true that every ج exists externally

The Inverse of the Contrapositive and Its Varieties
Author defines the inverse of the contrapositive and its types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the inverse of the contrapositive and distinguish its essential, accidental, mental, and external types.

Core Idea
The inverse of the contrapositive reveals the necessity between a thing and its necessary consequences.

In this passage, the author defines a key concept: the inverse of the contrapositive. This is not a simple conversion but a deeper relationship that captures the necessity between a thing and its implications. Understanding this helps you see how logical connections can be classified as essential or accidental, mental or external.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of 'aks al-naqid
The inverse of the contrapositive relates to the contradictory of what necessarily follows a thing. In other words, if A implies B, then not-B implies not-A.
The Arabic term is عكس النقيض.
2
Distinguish essential from accidental necessity
The thing and its consequence may be linked by essence (dhati) or by accident (aradi). Essential necessity is inherent; accidental is not.
Essential necessity belongs to the quiddity; accidental necessity is external.
3
Distinguish mental from external consideration
The necessity may hold in the mind (according to reality as known) or externally (in extra-mental existence). This distinction affects how we reason about the world.
Mental consideration is based on conceptual relations; external is based on actual existence.
4
Apply to Islamic science
In usul al-fiqh, the inverse of the contrapositive is used to derive rulings: if a cause entails a ruling, then absence of cause implies absence of ruling.
Example: if riba causes prohibition, then a sale without riba is not prohibited.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم بأن عكس النقيض يتعلق بنقيض ما يلزم الشيء ويتبعه؛ وذلك الشيء وما يلزمه فقد يكون كل واحد منهما ذاتيا، وقد يكون عرضيا. وكذلك قد يكون ذهنیا يعتبر بحسب ما يكون في نفس الأمر، وقد يكون خارجيا يعتبر بحسب ما يكون في الخارج. والقضايا الحملية وإن كانت مخالفة للقضايا الشرطية فإنها في قوة الشرطية كلية كانت أو جزئية فيعتبر فيها ما يعتبر في الشرطيات من امتناع وجود هذا دون ذلك، وامتناع عدم ذلك دون هذا كذلك، وهو النقيض في هذه الصورة. وهذا الاعتبار قد يكون بحسب الحقيقة، وقد يكون بحسب الوجود في الخارج. فإن من الأشياء ما يحمل على الشيء بحسب حقيقة ذلك الشيء، ولا يمكن أن يحمل بحسب ما يكون في الخارج. ومنها ما يحمل بحسب ما يكون في الخارج، ولا يمكن أن يحمل بحسب الحقيقة. ومنها ما يحمل بحسب الحقيقة مرة، وبحسب الخارج أخرى؛ وهذه كلها من جملة ما يعرف بالتأمل إن شاء الله تعالى.
And know that the inverse of the contrapositive pertains to the contradictory of what necessarily follows and accompanies a thing; and that thing and what necessarily follows it may each be essential or accidental. Likewise, it may be mental, considered according to what is in reality, or external, considered according to what is in external existence. Categorical propositions, although different from conditional propositions, are equivalent in force to conditionals, whether universal or particular; so consideration is given in them to what is considered in conditionals: the impossibility of existence of one without the other, and the impossibility of non-existence of the other without this, and that is the contradictory in this form. This consideration may be according to reality or according to external existence. For among things, some are predicated according to the reality of that thing and cannot be predicated according to external existence; some are predicated according to external existence and cannot be predicated according to reality; and some are predicated sometimes according to reality and other times according to external existence. All of these are among what is known through reflection, God willing.
Source pages p. 488

Commentary
This passage defines the inverse of the contrapositive (عكس النقيض) as relating to the contradictory of what necessarily follows a thing. The thing and its necessary consequences can be essential or accidental, and mental or external. The author then states that categorical propositions are equivalent in force to conditionals; that is, a universal categorical proposition implies a conditional necessity: if the subject exists, the predicate must exist, and if the predicate is absent, the subject must be absent. This consideration may be according to reality (essential predication) or external existence (accidental predication). Examples from Islamic sciences: 'Every human is rational' is a real predication; 'Every writer is a human' is an external predication. The author concludes that such distinctions are known by reflection.

Key Terms

ذاتي
عرضي

Key Takeaway
The inverse of the contrapositive is a fundamental tool for understanding necessary connections, and it can be classified as essential or accidental, mental or external.

The Conditional Force of Categorical Propositions
Author explains the conditional force of categorical propositions and the distinction between real and external predication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to translate a categorical proposition into a conditional statement and determine whether the predication is according to reality or external existence.

Core Idea
Every categorical proposition implies a conditional necessity.

The author explains that although categorical and conditional propositions are different in form, they are equivalent in logical force. This means that a universal affirmative like 'Every A is B' implicitly says that if something is A, then it is B, and if it is not B, then it is not A. This insight helps you understand the logical backbone of definitions and statements in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the equivalence
The author states that categorical propositions are in the force of conditionals (في قوة الشرطية). This applies to both universal and particular.
2
Extract the two conditional implications
For 'Every A is B', we have: if A then B, and if not B then not A. These correspond to the two directions of necessity.
3
Distinguish real predication from external predication
Real predication (بحسب الحقيقة) is based on the essence of the subject; external predication (بحسب الوجود) is based on attributes that may or may not be essential.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
'Every human is rational' is real (essential); 'Every writer is human' is external (accidental). The conditional interpretation helps to test the necessity.

Source Text

And know that the inverse of the contrapositive pertains to the contradictory of what necessarily follows and accompanies a thing; and that thing and what necessarily follows it may each be essential or accidental. Likewise, it may be mental, considered according to what is in reality, or external, considered according to what is in external existence. Categorical propositions, although different from conditional propositions, are equivalent in force to conditionals, whether universal or particular; so consideration is given in them to what is considered in conditionals: the impossibility of existence of one without the other, and the impossibility of non-existence of the other without this, and that is the contradictory in this form. All of these are among what is known through reflection, God willing.
Source pages p. 488

Commentary
This passage defines the inverse of the contrapositive (عكس النقيض) as relating to the contradictory of what necessarily follows a thing. The thing and its necessary consequences can be essential or accidental, and mental or external. The author then states that categorical propositions are equivalent in force to conditionals; that is, a universal categorical proposition implies a conditional necessity: if the subject exists, the predicate must exist, and if the predicate is absent, the subject must be absent. This consideration may be according to reality (essential predication) or external existence (accidental predication). Examples from Islamic sciences: 'Every human is rational' is a real predication; 'Every writer is a human' is an external predication. The author concludes that such distinctions are known by reflection.

الوجود الخارجي

Key Takeaway
Categorical propositions are logically equivalent to conditionals, and this equivalence reveals the necessity between subject and predicate.

Immediate Inference
intermediate
8 min
The Converse of the Contrapositive for Affirmatives
Author explains the rule of 'aks al-naqid for affirmative categoricals with example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to perform 'aks al-naqid on a necessary universal affirmative categorical proposition and verify its validity using the modal conditions given.

Core Idea
In 'aks al-naqid, affirmatives behave like negatives in simple converse; the result is an affirmative modified proposition.

Having learned the simple converse ('aks mustawi), we now turn to a related but distinct immediate inference: 'aks al-naqid, or converse of the contrapositive. The author explains that for affirmatives, the rule is analogous to that for negatives in simple conversion, with strict modal conditions. This lesson unpacks the rule, the crucial distinction between negative and modified affirmative, and the reasoning behind the transformation.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish 'aks al-naqid from 'aks mustawi
Note the author's statement: the judgment in 'aks al-naqid is contrary to that in simple converse. In simple converse, affirmatives become particulars; here they remain universal but with negated terms. For affirmatives, the result is a universal affirmative modified (ma'dula).
This inversion of roles is key to avoiding confusion.
2
Identify the modal conditions
The author lists four modal types that allow the inference: necessary (daruriyya), perpetual (da'ima), generally conditional (mashruta 'amma), and generally customary ('urfiyya 'amma). These are the same conditions as for negatives in simple converse.
Only universal affirmatives with these modalities can undergo 'aks al-naqid.
3
Analyse the proof by contradiction
Assume the converse is false; then its contradictory is true (some not-B is J with possibility). This leads to a contradiction with the original necessary universal affirmative. The proof shows the converse must be true.
The proof uses the principle of conversion for particular affirmatives from simple converse.
4
Clarify the proposition type
The converse 'everything that is not B is not J' is not a negative proposition. It is an affirmative modified (ma'dula) because the copula remains affirmative; only the terms are negated.
Do not mistake the negated predicate for a negative copula.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في الموجبات من الحمليات. والمشهور في عكس النقيض عند الجمهور وهو أن الحكم فيه على خلاف الحكم في العكس المستوي؛ وذلك لأن الحكم في الموجبات هنا نحو الحكم في السوالب هناك، وفي السوالب نحو الموجبات أيضًا بحسب ما يكون في الحقيقة وبحسب ما يكون في الخارج كذلك. فالموجبات إذن بحسب ما يكون في الخارج مثلا لا تكون منعكسة إلا وأن تكون كلية، والكلية لا تكون منعكسة كنفسها كما وجهة إلا وأن تكون ضرورية أو دائمة أو مشروطة عامة أو عرفية عامة كما في السوالب في العكس المستوي. أما الضرورية فيقال بالضرورة كل «ج» (ب) ، بالضرورة كل ما هو ليس «ب» ليس «ج» وإلا لصدق نقيضه وهو ليس كل ما هو ليس «ب» ليس «ج» بالإمكان العام أي بعض ما ليس «ب» «ج» بالإمكان العام فينعكس بعض ما هو ليس (ج) ليس «ب» بالإمكان العام كما مرَّ في العكس المستوي؛ وقد كان كل «ج» «ب» بالضرورة، هذا خلف ولا يظن أن العكس وهو قولنا بالضرورة كل ما هو ليس (ب) ليس (ج) هو السالبة؛ إذ هو موجبة معدولة موافقة للأصل. واللفظ مطابق للموجبة فيما مرَّ فلا يمكن أن يكون سالبة. وقد يقال أيضًا إن الصدق في الأصل لا يمكن أن يكون بدون الصدق في العكس، فإن الموضوع في الأصل بدون المحمول لا يخلو من أن يكون ممكنا، أو لا يكون. فإن كان ممكنا فلا مجال للصدق في الأصل، وإن لم يكن ممكنا البتة فلا مجال لانتفاء الصدق في العكس وإلا لا يمكن وجود ما ليس «ب» بدون ما ليس «ج» فيمكن وجود ج) بدون «ب» مع أنه لا يمكن البتة، هذا خلف. وقد يقال أيضًا إن الحملية في قوة الشرطية كما مر ، والشرطية لا يمكن تحققها إلا ولا يمكن
Chapter: On the affirmative categoricals. The well-known (view) regarding the converse of the contrapositive according to the majority is that the judgment in it is contrary to the judgment in the simple converse. That is because the judgment in the affirmatives here is like the judgment in the negatives there, and in the negatives like the affirmatives, according to what is in reality and what is in external existence as well. So affirmatives, for example, according to what is in external existence, are not converted except if they are universal, and the universal is not converted in itself with the same aspect except if it is necessary, perpetual, generally conditional, or generally customary, as in the negatives in the simple converse. As for the necessary: it is said: necessarily every J is B; necessarily everything that is not B is not J; otherwise its contradictory would be true, namely: not necessarily everything that is not B is not J with general possibility, i.e., some of what is not B is J with general possibility; then it converts to: some of what is not J is not B with general possibility, as passed in the simple converse; but it was that every J is B necessarily; this is a contradiction. And do not think that the converse, which is our saying 'necessarily everything that is not B is not J', is negative; for it is an affirmative modified (ma'dula) agreeing with the original. The wording corresponds to the affirmative in what passed, so it cannot be negative. It is also said that the truth in the original cannot exist without the truth in the converse; for the subject in the original without the predicate is either possible or not. If it is possible, then there is no room for truth in the original; and if it is not possible at all, then there is no room for the absence of truth in the converse; otherwise it would be impossible for what is not B to exist without what is not J, and thus it would be possible for J to exist without B, while it is not possible at all; this is a contradiction. It is also said that the categorical is in the force of a conditional, as passed, and the conditional cannot be realized except and cannot...
Source pages p. 489

Commentary
This passage defines the rules of 'aks al-naqid (converse of the contrapositive) specifically for affirmative categorical propositions. The author begins by stating the general principle: the judgment in 'aks al-naqid is contrary to that in the simple converse ('aks mustawi). In simple converse, affirmative universals become affirmative particulars, and negative universals become negative universals. Here, the roles are swapped: affirmatives behave like negatives, and negatives like affirmatives. The rule is then applied based on whether the proposition is considered according to reality (haqiqa) or external existence (kharij). For external existence, only universal affirmatives can be converted via 'aks al-naqid, and they must be of certain modal types: necessary (daruriyya), perpetual (da'ima), generally conditional (mashruta 'amma), or generally customary ('urfiyya 'amma)—just like the negatives in simple converse. The author illustrates with a necessary universal: 'Necessarily every J is B' converts to 'Necessarily everything that is not B is not J'. He provides a proof by contradiction: if the converse were false, its contradictory would be true, leading to an impossibility. He also clarifies that the converse is not a negative proposition but an affirmative modified (ma'dula), because it uses negated terms but the copula is affirmative. Further, he argues that truth in the original necessitates truth in the converse, analyzing the possibility of the subject without the predicate. Finally, he notes that categoricals are equivalent to conditionals, and the conditional cannot be realized without certain conditions. The example uses variables J (ج) and B (ب), but in Islamic logic one could apply this to: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' converting to 'Every non-forbidden is non-intoxicant'.

Key Terms

موجبة
سالبة
ضرورية
معدولة

Checkpoint

D) Not necessarily every non-forbidden is non-intoxicant.

Key Takeaway
The converse of the contrapositive for a necessary universal affirmative yields a universal affirmative modified with the same modality, not a negative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
